Interesting to Brandy and Whisky Drinkers.
THE imports of case Whisky during the year 1897 amounted to 115,357 cases,
those of case Brandy to 43,723 cases. These figures exhibit a marked increase
in the consumption of Brandy, and show a strong tendency of the public to revert
to that spirit as their favorite drink. It was thought by many that the consumption
of Whisky was fully eight cases to every one of Brandy, whereas the proportion is
only about 2| to 1. ... The wholesomeness of
Joshua Brothers’ Australian
BOOMERANG BRANDY,
attested by the highest authorities, and its adoption by the House of Commons, the
War Office, leading hospitals and clubs, etc., has tended to bring about the above
result. In Cases, Casks, Flasks, and Half-Flasks.

THE RED PAGE.
IT is easier to catch your controversy than to
kill it, and echoes of Von Kotze’s assault
upon Oscar Wilde still rumble among the
eastern foot-hills of Australia. Von Kotze
himself forwards a statement jn reply to hisoritics;
bub, unluckily, he is more concerned with the
controversialists than with the point at issue in
the controversy. Arthur A. D Bayldon argues
for him, and more to the purpose. He reiterates
the offending lines-
“ The poor dead woman whom he loved,
And murdered in her bed ”
and askswhv does the complained-of passage jar.
Because, he says, ,l when imagination is strung,
a sudden descent, or, rather, appeal to reason, is
fatal to emotion, since they are antithetical.
Again, the introduction of a realistic detail into
an imaginative narrative, unless it heightens the
effect of the whole, or shoots imagination towards
a climax, collapses interest. More, a concrete
thought thrown into the fervid atmosphere of the
magination must calch fire and be suspended, or
the emotion shocked breaks down. What mat-
ters it where the woman was murdered ? Did
; his little prosaic detail intensify interest or leap
imagination it would be a stroke of power.
. ‘ So the two brothers and their murdered man
Rode past fair Florence. ’ — Keats.
What a terrible touch ‘ murdered man.' Sets
imagination a-quiver. The deed is already done
in the hearts of the brothers. The knife-thrust is
a, trivial detail. When imagination is played on
by a beautiful emotion a word coarse or too realis-
tic checks and drags it down to vulgar or painful
associations.
‘ The bright blade quivered at the victim's throat.'
— (Tennyson’s ‘ Dream of Fair Women.’)
Blade, too realistic for the emotion set up by the
poem’s lead-off—altmvd in last editions to ‘death.’
In Rossetti’s loveliest sonnet: ‘Water for an-
guish of the solstice ’ —we have (or did have, as I
believe it is altered in last edition) imagination
sh icked by the coarse rawness of the word —
‘ fi sh.’
•On the other hand, tremendous force is gained
by a swift transition from a beautiful to a terribly
realistic picture, provided they are phases of the
same emotion. Dob d! delicately touches on the
homely doings of a love sick maiden in war-time,
feeding her canary an 1 sighing all her soul away
to him—change of picture—
‘ Who cannot hear
The raven croak in;/ at his carrion ear.’
“Finally, then: ‘ln her bed’ freezes emotion,
opens no new vista to imagination.; it does not
flash out or curdle; it is, in fact, a mere clap-trap
rhyme. In short, it is a test by which Wilde and
men of his calibre are precipitated. He entered
gaol an artistic excrescence and leaves it a literary
nincompoop. His poem is dull, flat, and un-
wholesome —a mass of blotches, the thin, queru-
lous piping of a smart, vain, but un •stublished
verser.”

There is an illuminating note from Chris: Bren-
nan on the other side -
“A uiord on the origin of ‘The Ballad
of Reading Gaol ’ At Reading, Eng.. on
18th June, 1896, Charles Thomas Wooldridge,
trooper of the R .yal Horse Guards, was charged
before Mr. Justice Hawkins with the murder of
his wife at Ciewer on the 2 )th March preceding.
He had married her two years before secretly
After his regiment was removed to London she
still remained at Ciewer, where he visited her.
She was unfaithful : he heard it, went to her,
accused her. Two days later he told a comrade in
Knightsbridge barracks that he 'expected his wife
that night : if she failed him, he would go to
Windsor ‘ and do some damage.’ He went, and
was seen to arrive at his wife’s house : soon after
her body was found, the throat cut, lying —‘in
the road.'
“ The defence urged a dictum of Mr. Justice
Blackburn’s—that a wife’s infidelity might some-
times reduce the avenging husband’s guilt from
that of murder to that of manslaughter. The
jury added to their verdict of Guilty a recommen-
dation to mercy, ‘which,’ the Judge said, ‘would
be forwarded to the proper quarter.’ The rest —
is told in the Ballad.
“ Inthefoad: the ingenious gentleman who found
such appalling banality in the plain words in the
bed —(what did he want? ‘poetic diction’?, there
is too much of it already in the poem ; or does he
not see that this is a ballad, and that the distin-
guishing quality of this literary form is simple
detailed treatment of a story, that his reference to
Heine’s epigrammatic lyrics is a confusion of
genres ?) —may now perceive, what hasalready been
urged against him, that the author had a special
purpose in view. The true poetic licence is to
deform reality. In order that he may set in
stronger opposition to Man’s grim justice that
goes its way
And will not swerve aside,
the piteous and incalculable recklessness of pas-
sion, the writer gives his guardsman’s love the
appearance of lawlessness —disengaging, by this
slight falsification, the truth, that the crimes,
the infidelities, the cruelties, and the re-
venges, of love, lie, with all its goods,
IjeyOnd' the t each of laws made by the strong and
unmoved spirits, to whom this world b dungs.
They hang their soldier, for his deed was tangi-
ble ; yet
Each man kills the thing he loves—
the ballad will tell the rest.
“ And now, how skilfully, with three slight
touches, is that air of lawlessness created ! The
Ine, and the banal bed —the deed was done amidst
the hot orgy of pleasure, the murderer’s blood
is moved by the drink they had enjoyed to-
thqr, his heart gnawed by the thought that
rehanoe to-morrow she would drive him away
ee more : he would, with his knife, arrest the
ur of his fruition, stay her fleeting will, and
m her for ever (the thought is not confined to
Dse lov. rs that are, in the technical sense un-
ppy)-and they found him by the dead lingering
her, in wild remorse, now that his frantic deed
1 set them eternally apart, unable to end that
' n' 1 — w h® n reading this stanza,
a British husband, with redress-and damages
n the Divorce Court ? No other touches are
pured than these three, and how well they do
nr work except, here and there, for a Herr
11 Kotze. '

\nd Victor Daley says a fine and adequate last
ird—
‘Von Kotze’s carp is merely a querulous re-
tement of the old, old question—What is fib
3 proper and seemly in poetry ? The abrupt
■and-large reply to this is—Anything that by
s vivifying genius of the poet can be made to
pear fit and proper and seemly. The man who
not a poet states dogmatically that common
sryday words should not be used in verse. Bub
3 man who is a poet takes a common everyday
ird and placing it between the teeth of his
ought crushes blood out of it, as one squeezes
b red juice out of an Isabella grape. For the
iod is always there. Common words are root
irds, and, when torn up by a dexterous
ist, can be made to cry like man-
akes. In moments of emotion—in sud-
n crises—even a lexicographer will for-
t himself and talk mother’s-knee language,
le hypercritics of his day said scornfully of
icrates that he took too many of his illustrations
on) the work of braziers and blacksmiths and
ilormen, and other grimy and greasy persons.
•at curled and perfumed Alcibiades replied to
lese railings with a remark to the effect that
oorates was like one of the archaic statues of the
nties —a rude wooden image on the outside, but
ith his belly full of little golden gods. And
•istocratic, empyreal Plato is still a living wit-
ass to the fact that Socrates was a Poet.
* * * *
“ Common, homespun words are the warp in
hich is woven the ideal pictures of the mind,
/anting this warp they would be merely fleeting
pparitions made visible upon a backfog of
vanescent mist. Considered a little closely, any
rticle of man’s invention —bed, chair, table,
irthen crock or Sevres vase—is instinct with
oetry. It contains, for the inspired interpreter,
he history of the Human Race, from the cold,
ncomfortable times when men lived in caves and
hatched holes in the ground, down to this en-
ightened, Nine-
eenth Century. The four walls and chimney of
he rudest hovel represent ages of thought. We
tre too much accustomed to look upon these
hings, and the words that stand for them, as
ird inary stage-properties of our little life-dramas,
ifet which of us has not read with delighted in-
ierest the story of how Robinson Crusoe, cast
iway upon a lonely island, built his own house and
n mufaetured his own cooking utensils ? Properly
; nsidered— Le., from a philosophic point of view
—a wheelbarrow should be to men a more wonder-
mi thing than the mo.it flamboyant sunset. Sun-
lets were before wheelbarrows, but man did not
nvent sunsets by sweat of his intellect, whereas
tie did invent wheelbarrows, and they are visible,
palpable, wheelable evidences of his Develop-
ment, and, as such, qualified to be mentioned in
any epic. Here follows a quotation from Emer-
son on ‘ Art ’ —not long, but enough to show that
the American philosopher agrees with the Aus-
tralian ditto—
' Give to barrows, trays, and pans
Grace and glimmer of romance;
'Tis the privilege of Art
Thus to play its cheerful part,
Man on earth to acclimate,
And bend the exile to his fate,
And, moulded of one element
With the days and .firmament,
Teach him on these as stairs to climb,
And live on even terms with Time ;
Whilst upper life the slender rill
Of human sense doth overfill.’
* * » *
“It is at once admitted that the unnecessary
mention of commonplace things in poetry is bad
art. But when commiinplace things are natural,
unobtrusive accessories of the story, it is worse
art to ignore them. Medea’s cauldron and the
cauldron in which the ‘ Macbeth ’ witches brewed
their hell broth were simply large-sized pots, in
which cabbage and bacon might have been cooked,
instead of sliced-up Jason, and baneful herbs
culled under a malignant aspect of the moon. Yet
we regard them not as common cooking utensils,
but as vessels of Fate. It is the connection that
counts. And—to reveri to text—surely Murder
should count as a sufficiently tragic excuse for
verse-reference to a bed ? Von Kotze is de-
clining into classicism. It is always the way.
Tennyson’s muse has talked pretty for so many
years that many estimable persons believe that
any un-Tennysonian word used in later verse is
poetically coarse or indecent. And, as usual, the
disciples and admirers of 'he late Laureate go
further than their in ister did in this respect, and
take it for granted that a healthy Muse can
exist upon a diet of rose-'-aves and treacle-
water - otherwise hydro nel. The Queen Anne men
made of English literature a mathematically
precise and—to mix metaphors—pentametrical
Dutch garden. Then, in good time, the revo-
lution came with Shelley, and Byron, and
Keats, and Wordsworth, and Coleridsre as its
apostles. Tennyson followed in their foot-
steps, but his fastidious eclecticism has_ perhaps
done more harm to English poetical literature
than was ever done by the keen, cold penta meters
of Pope. The Garden of the Conventional Muse
is no longer Dutch, but is instead a cunningly-
contrived maze covering a certain number of mil-
lions of space-acres with winding paths opening
out here and there upon ingeniously arranged
Vistas of Infinity. Of course, as was to be ex-
pected in the circumstances, the Muse has oc-
casionally—as in the case of Walt Whitman —
jumped the fence and run stark staring naked
through the wide wild unrailed world.

“To close with a paragraph. four lines of E.
B. Browning—
‘ Our Euripides the human
With his droppings of warm tears,
And his touchings of thin /s common,
Till they seem to touch the spheres. ’
And the following from that royal love-song,
which is as a many-colored blossoming tree filled
with singing birds—the Song of Solomon—
‘ How beautiful are thy feet with shoes, O prince's
daughter ! ’
‘ Now also thy breasts shall be as clusters of the
vine and the smell of thy nose like, apples.’
If Von Kotze had been Solomon’s literary
amanuensis and adviser lie would to a certainty
have suggested that the mention of shoes in the
first verse was needlessly trivial and vulgar, and
that as regards the nose of the Beloved in the second
verseit was bad poetic form to mention noses in high-
class Hebraic poetry. Yet Solomon had sufficient
experience of women to know what he was singing
about. And the nose—or, say, the breath of pink
nostrils—of a pretty woman does smell like apples
unto this day. Finally—Congruity in Poetry is
simply a relative term, and is more often than not
a measure of the intellectual inability of the
reader to make sudden mental transitions.”
—
Really, foreign criticism is not all unenlight-
ened. New York Bookman dismisses T. A.
Browne’s “ Plain Living ” with the remark that
“as in all Mr. Boldrewood’s books, since the
early ones that made his name, the style and
matter here rival each other in poverty and lack
of charm.” And Lund. Spectator, noticing Turner
and Sutherland’s so-called “ Development of Aus-
tralian Literature,” declares that “The new
school of Colonial poets, such as Mr. Paterson,
the author of ‘ The Man from Snowy River,’ is
very insufficiently dealt with by Mr. Turner in
his bald and commonplace introductory sketch,
in which he has the dubious taste to bepraise his
collaborator’s verses in a very fulsome and ‘pro-
vincial ’ style. Mr. Turner should bear in mind
that ‘The Man from Snowy River ’ went through
four editions in Sydney before it was published
in London, and that these ‘ horsey ’ verses, so
redolent of the Australian bush, have been widely
read and admired beyond the colonies themselves ;
whereas his colleague’s ‘Love and the Law,’
which he ranks with ‘ Enoch Arden,’ is probably
not known to a dozen persons even in Melbourne
itself. It is this lack of all ‘ proportion ’ which
marks the provincial spirit.”
* * * »
“Seaweed, a Cornish Idyll,” which was issued
serially in The University Magazine and is pub-
lished at 3s. fid. by the University Press, mates
the effect rather of a physiological treatise than of
a piece of literature. It apparently aims to dis-
play—and does display with some novelty, and,
for an English writer, some courage—phases of
the action and reaction of sex-impulse. In spite
of good phrasing and characterisation, however,
the book has an air of unreality, and the interest
is only moderate. H. B. Marriott Watson’s
“The Heart' of Miranda ” collects his latest
sketches and stories in a volume which ex-
hibits the author’s tendency to refine all the
force out of his plots. Some of the tales in
“ At the First Corner,” besides charm of style,
had grip and vigor : in “ Galloping Dick ” a
trifling loss of vigor was more than compensated
by still greater felicity of style. Now, however,
Mr. Watson so stickles and boggles over the man-
ner that his matter never seems really alive : his
people are all too evidently puppets of the literary
intelligence. The theme of “ The Heart of Mi-
randa” is best suited to the precieux method, and
a graceful dexterity makes the wooing scenes re-
readable. The other five items incline to be flat
and mechanic.
* * * *
St. Just de Beaupre Mackay writes from
Numurkah : “ I notice on the Red Page
the suggestion that Marie Corelli, the novelist,
was the wife of the late Eric Mackay, poet and
dramatist. Such was not the case. The facts of
the Mackay - Corelli controversy are: Luigi
Corelli, Marie Corelli’s father, came to London
after the close of the Italian war of independence
and remained in England after Garibaldi’s de-
parture. Luigi Corelli taught languages and
music in Kensington for some few years.
His wife was Paulina d’Oralti, of Rome. Marie
Corelli was born in London ; and shortly after-
wards her father died. Charles Mackay, LL.D.,
poet and litterateur, then on the staff of the Illus-
trated London News, and a writer for Chambers’s,
Fraser’s, Leisure Hour, Edinburgh Review, and
other journals, met Signora Corelli and married
her eighteen months after her husband’s death.
Marie Corelli was then a child of about 1 year
and three months old. So that she was the
daughter-in-law, or step-daughter, of Dr, Charles
Mackay. Dr. Mackay had been married before,
his first wife being Margaret Stuart Mackay, his
second cousin. Eric Mackay, deceased, was
the only child of that union, therefore he and
Marie Corelli were of no actual kin to one
another. Eric Mackay was never married,
neither has Marie Corelli ever entered the
married state. As the eldest son of the
late Dr. Hugo Glencairne Mackay, of Halifax,
Nova Scotia, 1 am the eldest surviving
nephew of the late Dr. Charles Mackay, and
cousin of the late Eric Mackay, and being so I
hope this statement of a scrap of family history
will stop the absurd, imaginative, and often false
statements circulated about Eric Mackay and
Marie Corelli —about the latter especially.”

Peocavimus ! C. B. : Red Page, J uly 30—
“Huysmans probably borrowed Wilde’s thunder.” The
suggestion errs. “Arebours” appeared in November,
1884, “The Decay of Lying,” in January, 1889. In Wilde’s
“ Picture of Dorian Gray ” (1890), the atmosphere of “ A
rebours” continually makes itself felt: one chapter in-
deed—on Dorian’s collection of gems, vestments, etc.,
reads like one of the chapters on Des Esseintes’ library,
flowers, or mouth organ. AndF.M. : Punch artist, E.
T. Ray. Of course, E.T. Reed is meant.”
“ Zadig ” : I join issue with statement (July
23) concerning “Tom o’ Bedlam’s Song’’ that Thompson
“of the fine fragment makes a very tine poem.’’ Thomp-
son’s verses are undeniably good—ht- has caught exactly
the spirit of the original—but I aflirm that, artistically,
he has not made a very fine p ern. The two first stanzas
are excellent—they distinctly give momentum ; but why
did he not rest content with that, addition, making the
splendid 4th stanza (4th and sth of the original), as it
should be, the inevitable conclusion? All that comes after
is anti-climax, destroying the spell cast by the preceding
verses. T.’s poem is not long, but from its peculiar ap-
peal-containing nothing to bring into play the purely
reasoning faculties—it exceeds artistic length. The
original—that “ crazy splendid ghost ’—gives me a thrill
such as one might feel passing the door of a haunted
chamber at midnight—a mental excitement, too intense
to be greatly prolonged. Of course, the higher the ex-
altation the sooner comes the reaction to a corresponding
depression. I fear few will finish T’s poem feeling the
tingle the original never fails to produce. And—in a
carping mood, perhaps “ toad-blown paunch” and
“ burst and blood-biown insect ” seem to me disgusting.

Spots before the eyes?
Do the muscles twitch ?
Have you pains in small of back?
Have you pains in head ?
Have you shooting pains?
Do you feel fatigued?
Are you weary ?
Does your head swim ?
Do your knees knock?
Does your hand tremble?
Nervous Twitchings.
A nervous man easily gets all
of a tremble, gets fits of weak-
ness, fits of twitchings. Here are
some of his symptoms :
Do your eyes twitch ?
Do your hands tremble ?
Do you feel shaky?
Can you walk without halting ?
Are you melancholy ?
Do you have ringing in the ears?
Is your blood poor ?
Are your eyes weak?
Are your eyes bleary ?
Does your head swim?
Nervous Debility-
This affliction destroys ambi-
tion, organic strength, energy,
and hope. Following are some
of the symptoms :
Do you feel weak?
Have you cold feet?
Have you backache ?
Do you shun society ?
Are you losing flesh ?
Do you sleep poorly?
Are you low-spirited?
Are your eyes sunken ?
Do you have hot flushes?
Is your memory impaired?
Have you no manly vigor?
Have you no vital energy?
Do you have sick headaches?
Is there nausea after eating?
Do your hands or feet sweat?
Has'the brightness left your eyes?
Do you feel unfit to marry?

Bards of the Backblocks.
Westralian Judges stand at present pretty
well in public estimation and confidence and it
would be a pity to disturb those relations. Where-
fore one regret's to find it reported that a J udge,
upon the clock indicating the dinner-hour, and
there being still no sign of the retired jury,
asked, “Is this a spec.a! jury ! and added, when
told that it wasn’t, “I suppose that explains the
difficulty of their powers of comprehension.
But if we assume that the reply was thus cor-
rectly reported, there surely isn’t much room tor
surprise at a jury being fogged by His Honors
charge.
Dan.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They are tinkling in the distance, I can hear them
quite distinctly
Clear above the shrieking dummies, and the out
or inward train ;
And I know what they are telling, for in bush-
man’s lore succinctly
They repeat, “The team is making for the
scrub beyond the plain.”
Don’t you see the team out yonder with the moon-
light streaming over,
While the bells betoken slyly should the rogues
incline to stray,
For, when satisfied with feasting, to their knees
in fragrant clover,
Some are sure to seek the thicket for a stolen
holiday.
’Tis a lie ! I am but listening to the sound of
long-dead voices
That in day-dreams and in night-time, round
remembrance come and cling
Like some haunting evil genius, like a siren that
rejoices
Just to see her lover perish, while entranced he
hears her sing.
We have grafted years together, and I loved him
as a brother —
Ay, far better than a brother for I think,
since Time began,
That two chums ne’er hit it better, and I know on
earth no other
Can fill the niche left vacant by dear old
Dare-Devil Dan.
Once again I see the humpy, and the camp-fire
burning brightly,
Till the leaping light sends shadows past the
gum and ironbark,
Past the tallow-wood and black-butt, where the
forest oak-tree lightly
Waves its brown and whip-like branches while
it trembles in the dark.
And the rippling streamlet glitters, flying squirrels
shriek with laughter ;
Flitting bat and pallid pohpoo, and the lonely
mopoke’s call,
All the perfect charm of camp-life, written large
in the hereafter,
On the sands of memory’s shore-line, when the
tide begins to fall.
Ah ! that far-off dark’ning forest, with its ruts
and rough log-bridges ;
From pin-bullocks to the leaders, every beast
was wearied out;
They had worked like very demons, over gullies,
ruts and ridges,
And ’twas but a half-mile further —when I
heard a ringing shout.
Glancing back, I tripped and stumbled—as the
trollies poised above me
Dan sprang forward—drew me out—and crash !
the log had—Mercy, God !
Jets of blood were spurting from him as he said,
“Lad, if you love me,
Put an end—this pain is Johnnie, Johnnie,
kill me !—is there God ? ”
I was sobbing like a woman, for I knew that he
was dying,
Crushed and helpless ; God of Heaven, why
such agony supreme ?
Yet 1 dare not move a muscle—in my hands lus
head was lying—
For I felt his spirit passing like some slowly-
ebbing stream.
“ Never—mind—old —man,” he panted, and a
space the blue eyes glistened,
“Just—a—little— longer—comrade—keep—your
hand—beneath—my—head ;
jf—you —could —poor —chap—you’d—help me ”;
and between each pause I listened
For the words he faintly whispered, till I
thought that he was dead.
But his eyes just opened softly and he smiling
looked upon me,
Ashesaid, “ I’m wearied, Johnnie, will you stir
the fire again ; ”
Then he turned his head a little, and the words
came, “ Rouse up, Johnnie !
Johnnie, lad, the team is making for the scrub
beyond the plain.”
And awhile, Death’s angel waiting, I could hear
the dear voice quiver—
“ Let him —sleep —poor —lad hes tired. I
shall—turn—them—back,” he said ;
Then, upon his lonely mission, out beyond the
Stygian River,
Sped the kind, unselfish spirit, but—a spirit of
the dead.
G. W. McKINNON.

A POLITICAL “SITUATION."
During the late N.S.W. political campaign we heard on all sides of seats to be vacated in favor of leaders who might get “left.” Where are those seats now?
Newly-fledged M.P.: “IT'S ASTONISHING HOW THESE SEATS DO SUCK TO YOU WHEN ONCE YOU GET INTO THEM!"

SATURDAY, AUGUST 13, 1898.
The Most Essential Amendment.
Among all the proposals ventilated in New
South Wales for the amendment of the
Federal Constitution, it is noteworthy that
neither Barton nor Reid nor Lyne nor any
other political leader seems in serious earnest
about the biggest and most urgent amend-
ment of all. That is the insertion of a pro-
viso that the railways shall be handed over
bodily to the Federal Government; that the
sole power of constructing new lines shall
also be vested in the Federal Government;
and that differential rates shall be abolished
and charges be made by distance only. Also,
that provincial boundaries shall be entirely
ignored in future railway-building and rail-
way management, the sole object being to
give every district the easiest and cheapest
access to the most convenient coastal city,
and that the Federal Government shall pro-
ceed to make a uniform railway gauge
throughout Australia. And yet The Bul-
letin says advisedly that this is more
urgent than all the other proposed
amendments put together The “ Brad-
don Blot,” whatever it was meant to be,
is as absolutely powerless for good or evil as
an ink-spot on an Alp. The three-fifths
majority might sometimes put an undue
share of power in the hands of the Federal
Senate, but as the constitution of the Senate
would make it more genuinely Democratic
than any House of Parliament now existing
in Australia this evil was never anything
like so large as it looked. But railway
federation is essential for the safety and
prosperity of Australia; it means, not
theoretical justice, but present bread and
butter; and however valuable theoretical
justice may be, bread and butter count for
even more in the long run.
• • • •
The following is one of Premier Reid’s
recent pronouncements on this subject (since
uttering it he has practically gone back on
his new idea, and his present attitude is un-
certain) :
But if a new system is to be inaugurated, I have come
to the conclusion that there is only one way possible, and
that is that the railways and the public debt should be
handed over to the Federation. (Cheers.) But the great
point about this is that this country would never allow a
system of transferring the railways which did not protect
us as to our absolute freedom of developing our internal
resources by means of extensions of existing lines or new
ones.
Also, this is Barton on the same subject
The railways of each colony have been constructed for
the main purpose of developing its territory ; for the
securing of speedy access to our markets, and for the
giving of quick transit to producers and persons engaged
in internal trade. All these purposes are closely identified
with the retention and utilisation of the lands of each
colony, which are, and must be, secured to it by the
terms of the union ; and our people generally take the
natural view that the main instrument of the develop-
ment of the territory should remain in the hands which
own the territory.
And this, again, is Henry Copeland, M.L.A.:
It was only logical that they must keep the railways to
develop their lands, because Victoria and Tasmania had
no lands to sell, and the people of New South Wales
would never sanction the handing over of the right to
work the present lines, or make new ones, to a Federal
Parliament.
* • • •
The Bulletin’s own opinion is that the
development of New South Wales by pro-
vincial railway management is about the
worst curse in the experience of that pro-
vince. The question of Federal as against
provincial ownership mainly affects two pro-
vinces (Victoria and New South Wales), they
being the only ones so badly pegged out that
a considerable proportion of their territory
is nearer to the next province’s big city than
to their own big city. And New South
Wales is so much more affected than Vic-
toria that it is in N.S. Wales that the ques-
tion has mainly to be settled. Federal man-
agement and construction mean putting the
railways on a strictly commercial basis. They
mean building lines to the nearest seaport city
whether it is in “ our” province or in some
other. Therefore, they mean less cost of con-
struction, less debt, less interest to be paid,
less miles of road to keep in repair, and lower
freights (for even if, in order to bring the
traffic to “our ” city, goods are carried 400
miles to Sydney at as low a charge as they
might be conveyed 200 miles to Adelaide, it
only means that the taxpayer has to make
up the difference). Therefore it follows that
districts which are now practically abandoned
because they are too remote to allow of pro-
fitable railway connection with far-off Sydney
would become habitable by allowing them
to have railway communication with a city
that is comparatively near at hand. And,
being thus made habitable, the New South
Wales population in these parts would be
increased, and the value of New South
Wales lands would go up, and the New
South Wales Treasury would collect more
revenue. In other words, Federal railway
management, regardless of provincial boun-
daries, means the development of New South
Wales lands, where provincial management
doesn’t.
• • * *
What provincial management in New South
Wales does aim at is not the development of
New South Wales but the development of
the one city of Sydney. Its idea is to refuse
the border districts cheap and easy access to
the near and convenient cities of Brisbane,
Adelaide and Melbourne (to refuse it as far
as possible, that is, by giving them no rail-
way communication that can possibly be
avoided with these cities, and making rail-
ways to connect them with Sydney only).
Therefore, enormously long lines are built
where short ones in a different direction
would do the work as well; and the long
lines cost more, and the debt is bigger, and
all New South Wales groans under a need-
less weight of interest contracted solely to
bring trade to Sydney. And there are more
repairs to do, and more railway staff is
required; and higher freights are charged
to the outlying agriculturist and pastoralist
for compulsorily hauling his goods twice as
far as he wants. And through these high
freights and through the artificial remoteness
thus created, the borders are mostly a howl-
ing desert which yields very little revenue,
and which isn’t developed —solely because
of the provincial development policy. And
the whole province is taxed to make up the
railway deficit largely caused by all this in-
sane and useless “development.” The only
place which gains anything by the forcible
dragging of trade out of its natural channels
is Sydney, which makes a trifling percentage
on the goods as they go through. But it
makes very little, and about one-third of
New South Wales is more or less beggared
to do it. The policy is Protection in its
one absolutely and objectionable
form —the Protection of the non-producer in
the city at the expense of the producer in the
country.
* * • *
New South Wales doesn’t want to main-
tain this ridiculous and ruinous system ; it is
only Sydney and its suburbs which want it.
Their attitude to the far-out pastoralist or
agriculturist is just about this : “You are
400 or 450 miles from Sydney, and 200 miles
from Melbourne or Adelaide. You can
only have a railway to Sydney. You
must send your wool over 200 extra
miles of land carriage and 600 or 1000
extra miles of sea-carriage at your own ex-
pense, so that we can make a few small
profits out of it as it goes by. This
enormously long railway can’t pay, therefore
you must help to make good the deficit of
taxation. The burden of all these needless
distances keeps you poor, and the artificial
remoteness created by a policy of cutting
you off from the only city which is near you
combines with it to keep the country empty,
and industry is thereby rendered unprofit-
able, and land practically worthless, but we
do it for the development of the country.
We would rather your district should be
empty and desolate, and should send only a
speck of trade annually to Sydney, than that
it should be prosperous and send a large
trade somewhere else. This is a Freetrade
country, therefore you can’t trade where you
please. You are a free and independent
citizen, therefore we will see you darned
first.”
* • • •
The Bulletin asks the people of New
South Wales to look at this matter with an
enlightened selfishness, and purely in the
interests of New South Wales. That pro-
vince is the only one a very large proportion
of which is nearer to other capitals than to
its own. Therefore if the provincial policy
continues under Federation, and Queens-
land, New South Wales, Victoria, and South
Australia keep on building separate railway
systems to drag trade to Brisbane, Sydney,
Melbourne, and Adelaide respectively, New
South Wales will be the only one a large
number of whose producers are grievously
handicapped by needless distances, and need-
less freights, and whose development, so far
as its outlying parts are concerned, will be
artificially retarded. How the present policy
works is strikingly exemplified in the case of
Broken Hill. New South Wales couldn’t
afford to build a line to connect that very
far-off spot with Sydney. It wouldn’t, in
its bitter provincial policy, build one to con-
nect it with the adjacent South Australian
system. The idea of letting the South Aus-
tralian Government carry its line and its
cheap fares into New South Wales terri-
tory, was too awful to be even dreamed
of. So at last the work was given to a
private co. which fleeces the New South
Wales people of Broken Hill of more than
double the rates which the South Australian
Government would have charged, and makes
50. per cent, dividends out of them to the
impoverishment of New South Wales. And
(here the wild humor of the thing comes in)
the Silverton Tramway Co. is a Victorian
enterprise, and it grinds the bones of the
New South Wales citizen to pay these huge
dividends to Melbourne speculators. And
the provincial policy of developing New
South Wales has done this.
* * ♦ *
In addition to these considerations, Aus-
tralia wants a uniform railway-gauge, to save
the needless expense and waste of time
which the present arrangement involves on
its various provincial borders, and to facili-
tate the movement of troops and material
when this country is called upon (as it
must be some day) to stand upon its defence.
It must get a uniform gauge some day, and
it can only be got by Federal control. Under
the provincial system it is absolutely im-
possible to arrive at any mode of apportion-
ing the expense among the different provinces
which will give satisfaction—the only way is
to have one management, and to charge the
expense to that management without any
apportionment whatever. If the railway
systems are federated now there will be
about 6000 or 10,000 miles of line to be
altered, according to the gauge decided upon.
If the provincial idea is retained Australia
will realise the situation later on—perhaps
when there are 60,000 or 100,000 miles to be
altered. But it will certainly realise the
situation some day or other.
The Tote.
In Victoria, where the snuffle of the saints who
went to gaol about ’93, or who should have
gone to gaol then and also at many other times,
still lies like a load on the public con-
science, another attempt is being made to
legalise the tote. It is being done humbly and
apologetically, out of deference to the snuffle-
some element in the community, and on a small
scale, so as not to shock the public too much at
A private member is in charge
of the Bill, so the Ministry doesn’t risk its
valuable reputation by being seen along with
the unholy thing. The Bill provides that the
tote may be used in strict moderation by clubs
of good standing—not the proprietary variety.
Proprietary clubs are understood to be run for
purposes of dingy lucre only, while the orthodox
variety of club is supposed to rush the harried
gee-gee at great speed round a circular course
out of a love of sport and a desire to improve
the breed of the walloped and exhausted
animal. The tote is only to be per-
mitted on the course where the horse
is understood to be bursting himself for
his own good and the good of his species.
It is not to be used more than four days at any
one meeting. It will be purely optional—
a club may use the machine, or it may stick to
the old, blasphemous, fraudulent Ikey Mo, or
it may take them both at once. The club
which uses it is to deduct not less than 5 or
more than 8 per cent., and one-third of this—
that is to say, from £1 13s. 4d. to £2 13s. 4d.
out of every £IOO invested—is to go to a ohari-
ties-fund. The Government, it is presumed,
would shudder at the idea of personally touch-
ing the ill-gotten loot ; so the State’s percent-
age is to be used in the good cause of charity,
and the balm of benevolence is thus to be
poured over the cash by way of weakly dis-
infecting it
• * * •
In The Bulletin’s opinion the tote doesn’t
require all this weak, mean little apology, and
all these restrictions. There is no earthly
reason why it shouldn’t be used on all courses,
proprietary and otherwise, where there is busi-
ness enough to make it pay—only the proprie-
tary concern, as the less deserving of the
two, might be made to pay a larger percent-
age by way of discouragement. The tote
should be used just as often as it is
wanted ; there is no need for the four days’
restriction. The bookmaker should be abso-
lutely prohibited, so as to sweep all the avail-
able betting business into the clutches of the
machine. The amount deducted by the
machine should be 10 per cent.—the bookie
already takes a great deal more than that—
and the State should take 5 [ .r cent, of this ;
in the case of proprietary clubs it might take
7\, seeing that proprietary courses are mere
private speculations. And there is no reason
why the State should profess to shudder at the
proceeds, and should lift them up with the
tongs, and put them away separately into a
charities fund, lest the Treasury should be
contaminated. The Treasury lives, to a great
extent, on drink, tobacco, and opium revenue ;
on the price of the various iniquities of thieves,
drunks, liars, Flossies, and persons who use
horrible language, as paid in police-court fines ;
on the sums which perjured and fraudulent
traders pay to the Treasury by way of com-
pounding their felonies when they are caught
swindling the revenue; on billiard licenses,
duties on the cards used in the three-card
trick, postal revenue obtained through carrying
Tattersall’s letters, duties on patent medi-
cines—which are mostly only the three-card
trick done up in a bottle to defraud the dying,
and all manner of other unclean profits. A
State which makes its money in ways like
these should be proud to take all it can get out
of the tote, and should put that comparatively
clean revenue along with the other soiled loot,
and give up professing to shudder at the mode
of its acquirement.
* * # •
The alleged sinfulness of “recognising the
gambling evil,” which is involved in the legali-
sation of the tote, doesn’t enter into the case
at all. The evil is already recognised, and no
one has any serious hope of stopping the recog-
nition. The odds are shrieked on racecourses
all over the country ; they are advertised in
the papers, and announced at clubs ; the book-
maker is a legal and duly authorised institu-
tion ; and the biggest holiday in all Aus-
tralia is the great bookmaking carnival at
Flemington. In addition, there is a vast
amount of unrecognised betting which
the State apparently can’t put down,
and which the Church has also failed to put
down. And, even if the gambling wasn’t
recognised, it would make ho difference ; when
an evil exists, and no one can find a remedy for
it, there is nothing gained by agreeing not to
speak about it. The State already recognises
drunkenness and bad language and assault by
fining the perpetrators in a very inadequate
degree, and putting the proceeds in the Trea-
sury. If it fined the gambler 6d. in the £ of his
stake for State purposes and another 6d. in the
£ for other purposes, and severely punished the
bookmaker as a gambler who was depriving the
Treasury of its just and legal 6d., it would
merely carry the same logical principle a little
further towards its logical conclusion. The
State must fine somebody in order to get money
to carry on the government; over and above
what it earns out of its railway, postal and
other services Victoria has to fine some-
body to the extent of about .£3,000,000.
a year, or else go to unutterable smash..
If it doesn’t fine the gambler and drinker-
and opium-eater and the criminal classes,
then it must heap a crushing and utterly
ruinous load of taxation on the good, sober-
persons who go to church regularly. It is cer-
tainly not advisable for the State to invent new
sins, or to promote sins that only exist on a
very small scale, for the sake of making income.
But when one does exist on a very large scale
(merely to save space and explanation, the
orthodox crowd may be allowed to assume that
gambling is a sin) —when it has such a hold on,
the people that any Government which tried to.
put it down would simply be fired out on its.
ear—when all the police force can’t extinguish,
it, and it is so widespread that it is impossible
to get police that will seriously attempt it, or a
Parliament that will back them up in doing so,
—then the only logical course is to regulate it,
and make it as clean as possible, and fine the
gambler in order to ease the burdens of the
people who don’t gamble.
* • • •
At present, this horsey community gambles
away just as much money as it can spare. It
is the most horsey community on earth, and
the horse amusement lives on gambling. The
establishment of the tote can hardly increase the
evil, but it will divert the profits from the
great aggregate Ikey Mo to the State ; it will
also vastly reduce the number of people who
live on the game, and force a very useless
class to take to some more reproductive indus-
try for a livelihood. But if the tote is only
allowed to be used on a certain number of
days and in certain places, the inevitable re-
sult is that Isaac Mo will still flourish on the
days and at the places where the tote isn’t in
evidence. It is absurd to restrict the honest
gambling-machine, which supplies revenue to
the State, to certain days and certain race-
courses, and allow the dishonest gambling
Moses, who doesn’t supply any revenue, to
practise every day on every racecourse. If the
proprietary courses are in such a state of moral
decay that the machine can’t gamble there, the
bookmaker shouldn’t be permitted there either.
In fact, the bookmaker should be prohibited al-
together. When the State goes in for regulat-
ing the drink evil and, incidentally, making
revenue out of it, by charging duties on
liquor, and license-fees on public-houses, and
by fining the drunk, it permits no private in-
terference. No private Moses is allowed to col-
lect liquor-duties, or to issue public-house
licenses and keep the money, or to capture
drunks- and fine them ss. for bis own profit.
And the State should grab the profits of the
betting evil in the same monopolistic spirit.
* * * *
There are several other evils which could be
profitably “ recognised ” in the same way.
While the State permits its mails to carry
gambling money to Tattersall in Tas-
mania, and takes the postal revenue therefrom
without a shudder, it might just as well drop


its cloak of hypocrisy and establish its. own
State lottery, and take the whole profits itself,
instead of only a part of them. Also, it might
as well make the retail drink trade a State
monopoly and take all the profits, as merely
grab part of them in the shape of the duty and
the license money. But in the present case the
main points are that the State should lay itself
out to divert all the apparently unavoidable
gambling to its own machine ; that it should
make the sinful thing yield all the revenue it
is capable of—in Victoria it should be good for
about £BO,OOO a year—; and that it shouldn’t
snuffle about charity by way of excusing a, thing
which requires no excuse. What Victoria
mostly wants is public works to open up the
country and create employment; if there was
more employment the need for charity
would be much less. Also, it wants to con-
struct these works without leaving a load of
debt and a huge interest bill to posterity ; it is
mainly the tremendous liability handed down
by past generations which were too lazy and
improvident to pay their own way that makes
the people poor and creates the necessity for
charity. Victoria’s biggest present need is to
find half a million or a million a year out of
revenue to build new public works with, and
the only reliable way to do so is to set aside
certain sources of revenue for this express pur-
pose. The <£Bo,ooo a year which could be ex-
tracted from the tote should be one of them.
That sum spent on railway construction would
find employment for about 400 men and sup-
port for their families, besides finding perman-
ent work for many agriculturists and others
afterwards, and permanently enriching the
State. If that is not substantial charity, what
is ?
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Reid-Want Ministry Climbs Down.
The prize jackass item of N.S.W. electoral
campaign is surely the following from Sydney
Telegraph, of 4th August: —
Parliament will have a specific Federal programme
when it meets again, with an assurance of prompt and
decisive action. Every Federalist will then have to vote
with the Government or abandon his Federal professions.
Exactly what the Government will propose is not yet dis-
closed. But the programme will he one which all
members anxious for the early consummation of union
can accept as the basis for work that will afterwards have
to be done. Mr. Rkid has not yet communicated with
any of the other Governments, nor has he definitely out-
lined his plans. An impression prevails that another
Convention will have to be held to discuss the question
of the amendments with the other colonies. Whatever
preliminary steps may be taken, the negotiations can
hardly, it is pointed out, be conducted solely by repre-
sentatives of the Government. For example, the Oppo-
sition claims to have as much voice in the deliberations
as Ministers. It represents a considerable minority of
the Chamber, and it. was elected upon a distinct pro-
gramme. The Opposition in the other colonies, as well,
are likely to claim a large share in dealing with the pro-
positions, or rather amendments, that it may be sought to
embody in the Bill.
It is held now by a considerable section of members on
the two sides that the only practicable method after the
first stages have been passed is to get the Parliaments, as
in 1891, to consent to the appointment of delegates re-
presenting all parties. For the 1891 Convention the
delegates were chosen by ballot. There would be no
difficulty in following a similar course now. Negotiations
conducted exclusively by Ministers, it is thought, will
allow' only one side to be represented. This will not be
acceptable to the Opposition.
It appears, therefore, that Premier Reid doesn’t
know himself yet what his plans and ideas are
(previous to the election the same paper de-
clared that he had put a clear and well-defined
programme before the public). Rut, whatever
he decides to do, the Telegraph assures the
public in advance that it will be all right,
and every member who doesn’t follow Reid
(whatever his plans may be, and whatever his
“ proposals ” may amount to when they are
“disclosed”) must “abandon his Federal pro-
fessions.” And the Telegraph announces,
quite as a matter of course, that the Reid-
Want Government is going to “ negotiate ”
with the other provinces, and hold a conference
with them. Before the election the loudest
jeer which Reid and Want and the Telegraph
could heave at Barton was that he was going
to “ negotiate,” and they all three promised
blusterously that they wouldn’t do anything so
mean and weak and despicable. Also Want.,
of the Reid-Want Ministry, promised solemnly
that they were “ done with conferences.” They
were going (not to “ negotiate ’ or to hold
another convention) but to present N.S.W.’s
demands, and the other provinces (so Want
and Reid and the Telegraph all promised on
their honor as three gentlemen) would accept
them. It was the strong and spineful
Reid Government (the one that now hasn’t
even made up its mind what its programme is)
that was going to do this; it guaranteed to
rise like a strong wobbler in its agony and
secure all the required amendments on its own.
And now that the time has come for redeeming
these loud promises it seems that all it can do
is to hold a conference, and let the Opposition
help it to make its policy, and “ negotiate ”
just like Barton —and ask the other provinces
for concessions.
4
“Reid tlie Wriggler.”
Dear Bulletin. —As a REiD-ite, I accept your
jibing title. It is better than P.G., anyway.
Of course, Reid wriggles. Every modern poli-
tician wriggles —it’s the science of the game.
Think of Gladstone fighting the last half of
his life for all he had opposed the first half ;
gaoling Parnell one month, hand-and-glove
with him the next. Of Disraeli, the Radical,
making an Empress ; of Chamberlain, once for
“ peace at any price,” now a raging Jingo.
Moral: He who would lead a crowd must run
the way it wants to go. The man of scientific
or independent thought is out of place in poli-
tics. Stuart Mill was a frost, Macaulay little
better ; John Bright a poor Minister ; and it
is safe to say that Henry George would have
failed as leader of a Ministry. Barton
wriggled ; but, unfortunately for his political
success, not with the lightning change of
his opponent ! Admire, if you can, Reid’s
unequalled skill. He promised Upper House
reform, which seemed a good cry ; but the
alteration suggested fell flat. The Bulletin
demonstrated that it was a step backward—
thereupon Upper House reform is dropped like
a hot brick. Reid took up Local Self Govern-
ment, a standard Ministerial dish, found the
country did not care a hang for it (the average
bush resident is content to forego personal con-
trol if the entire cost of roads and bridges
falls on the central Government) so George
dropped L.S.G. More power to him! Note
how accurately he forecasted the Federal poll
with his famous “ Yes ” and “ No.” He knew it
was a close thing and meant to be on the winning
side. What end is served by measuring such
a man by ordinary standards? He, the boss wrig-
gler, boss politician, boss player of the game of :
“Gentlemen, those are my convictions. I may
have said the contrary last week, and leaned in
quite another direction last month, and I am
likely to find a new aspect of the question to-
morrow ; but staking, as I do, my political
reputation on what I nay now, I ask your sup-
port.” No other speaker’s speeches are so
interesting—you never know what is coming-
no one more apt. How he pricked the gas-bag
of Imperial Federation in London ! Good,
jolly old George, his eye-glass beams the one
ray of perfect comedy in a weary land.—H.F.

This Baleful Rag.
The following infuriated letter from “New
South Welshman ” has arrived on The Bulle-
tin like refreshing dew from heaven :
The country has to be thankful that the attempted
influence of your baleful rag was of no avail. Federation
at your price has been rejected. You never have
attempted in the least to cloak the fact that your in-
terests were wholly and solely with Victoria. Never once
have I noticed in your paper a word of praise, whether
for sport or otherwise, for the endeavors or achievements
of this colony, the country which supports you, and in
which you have your place of business. Your one wish
and thought is for the success of Victoria —you applaud
her efforts, lie over her failings, and, where the interests
of this colony and that are in conflict, you basely con-
demn and side against the country that puts the bread in
vour mouth. Why the hell do you not remove your
establishment to Melbourne, that fever-stricken, bank-
rupt city would suit the morals of j»our polluted and
blasphemous rag. I am —A New South Welshman.
This sort of thing cheers the path of the weary
pressman, and “New South Welshman” is
cordially invited to write and curse again. But,
at the same time, he is a woefully inaccurate
curser. For this baleful rag (good expression)
gathers about three-fourths of its bread (also
its butter and cheese, and tobacco, and its store-
clothes) from the other provinces, and it does
more good work in bringing the money of the
other provinces to N.S.W., and circulating it
there to help to feed and clothe the above
infuriated correspondent than any other six
N.S.W. papers. Also, N.S.W. hasn’t re-
jected the kind of Federation advocated by this
polluted and blasphemous rag; for it put
Barton at the head of the Convention poll,
and it gave “Barton’s Bill” a majority of
5367 over Reid and Want’s anti-Billiousness,
and at the general election (now that the
returns are all in) it seems to have given the
Barton crowd 14,833 more votes than the
Reid crowd. This baleful rag feels rather
good over these results. As regards the rela-
tive merits of N.S. W. and Victoria they don’t
come in anywhere. The Braddon clause, the
equal representation in the Senate, the three-
fifths majority —in fact, everything which is
now objected to in the Bill—were concessions
which N.S.W. and Victoria made equally to
the three less populous provinces ; therefore
Reid is rushing to get concessions for N.S.W.
and Victoria from the other three provinces,
and this baleful rag had an idea that N.S.W.
and Victoria might get on well enough without
these concessions. N.S.W. and Victoria, by
their votes, expressed the same idea and fully
agreed with our polluted and blasphemous
organ. Unluckily, the Cabbage-Tree Mob,
which old Jack Robertson left behind him,
has a fixed idea that there are only two places
in the world—N.S.W. and Victoria ; therefore,
when this baleful rag didn’t agree with Reid’s
minority, “New South Welshman” assumed
that it must be barracking for Victoria, simply
because he couldn’t realise that there was any
other place. Not being a baleful rag himself,
he didn’t know any better. This baleful rag
now sits down for a well-earned rest.

Turner Decides To “ Go It.”
Now that the Victorian Premier has resolved,
at the bidding of the Age, to throw over his
scruples and go on the loan burst once more, he
has evidently decided that it is just as well to
do the thing thoroughly. The new Turner
that the Age has manufactured is so unlike the
old one that the spectacles are about the only
point of resemblance. He announces that
Victoria proposes to go through <£3,750,000 of
loan-money in the next three years. His first
list of public works has just come to light ; it
will absorb .£2,546,870, and among the awful
things which Turner cheerfully shoves among
his “permanent and reproductive” under-
takings are rifles and fortifications (,£62,500),
subsidies to local bodies (.£162,000), schools,
gaols, &c. (£204,500), aids to mining and agri-
culture (£300,000), railway rolling-stock, to
replace the vehicles which wore out and should
have been restored out of revenue but weren’t
(£386,100), and a lot more of the same kind.
Turner so far acknowledges that a great part
of these are not permanent works, and not re-
productive, and are a mere fraud and swindle
upon posterity, that only £1,515,000 is to be
permanent debt, the other £1,031,000 being re-
payable out of revenue at the rate of £25,000 a
year. But even the £1,515,000 contains a vast
amount of expenditure which should be charged
to ordinary revenue, and the promise that the
other sum will be repaid in 41 years is a very
shadowy affair. Turner says he had to go
back to the borrowing fraud, or else propose
new taxation for public works purposes, which,
he alleges, is quite “ hopeless ” ; but, at all
events, he didn’t try it first, and make dead
sure of its hopelessness —he simply went back
to the easy loan dodge as one who returns to an
old friend. Also, with £2,906,000 of deficit to
pay off, and only about i 300,000 paid, and
with only two small surpluses Lo show that
Victoria is really round the corner, and with
an extra £25,000 a year to meet, Turner
already promises reductions in the income-
tax—and the Argus loudly approves. He
only got his income-tax for three years, and
probably when he asks for a renewal
the 48 Rich Landlords will fight hard for a re-
duction in any case, but, at all events, 1 urner s
grovel to the Fat interest, and his voluntary
offer to sacrifice, or partly sacrifice, a source of
revenue which is chiefly a tax on Fat, is quite
unnecessary. Victoria wants to raise .£500,000
a year more revenue instead of giving away
any of what it has to please any Fat section of
the community. The money is wanted to pro-
vide a regular annual instalment in reduction
of the deficit, and to leave something over so
that it won’t be necessary to charge the rifles
and ammunition, and the gaols and court-
houses and lunatic asylums, and the agricul-
tural and mining bounties and similar daily ex-
penses, to posterity —in order that this genera-
tion may have a good time and damn the con-
sequences. The whole story of Australian
finance consists in leaving the bill to posterity
and damning the consequences; and Turner,
with his old loro wn hat cocked wildly over one
ear, and with positive instructions from David
Syme to “go it,” starts on the same road as
the rest.

“The Bulletin's” Inconsistency.
Mr. J. 0. Watson, of N.S.W. Labor-party,
complained in one of his election speeches:
He (Mr. Watson) would point out the inconsistency of
Thu Bulletin. In 1897, in three different issues, that
paper had declared that he (Mr. Watson) was one of the
best of the ten men to be elected who would draft a
Constitution. Now The Bulletin was advocating Mr.
O’Connor’s cause. . . . This inconsistency on the part of
The Bulletin and Temora Independent clearly shows the
value that can be placed upon these papers’ advocacy
And, come to think of it, didn’t Watson and
all iiis party support Dibbs in ’92, and go over
to Dibbs’ enemy, Reid, in 1894 ; and isn’t J. C.
Watson in consequence, by his own showing, a
beautiful individual to preach consistency ?
But, as a matter of fact, The Bulletin did
strongly and cordially recommend J. 0. Watson
in 1897 as a good and valuable Federal candi-
date, and in ’9B this paper didn’t want to be
seen dead in J. C. Watson’s company. In ’9B
it found J. C. Watson barracking for the leader
who announced that he was going to break
all his promises of Council reform and pre-
serve the unrepresentative nominee principle —
for the man who supported the equal represen-
tation in the Senate and the three-fifths
majority, and then had the brazen treachery to
tell the people to vote against the Convention
Bill because these things (which he had sup-
ported) were in it—for the man who assented
to that rascally breach of faith, the 80,000
minimum, and nullified the Referendum, and
disfranchised the majority ; and who had just
taken back Want (self described as the “ arch-
destroying angel of Federation”) to his Cabinet
as a solid guarantee of bad faith. Also, it
found him in the same boat with a whole lot of
the meanest kind of provincialists —men who,
not being able to down the Bill by fair means,
tried to do it by shameless lying. It was
because The Bulletin found Mr. Watson in
that company that it didn’t want its corpse to
be discovered in the same river with his in ’9B.
Aiid it was because it didn’t know that he was
going to be found there that it supported him
in ’97. Its conduct was beastly inconsistent —
just as that of the Eleven Apostles was when
they cut their unfortunate friend, the Twelfth
Apostle, after a certain lamentable episode in
his history. And yet in ’99 The Bulletin
may be supporting Mr. Watson again, if he
improves in the interval—not otherwise.

Two Years.
That little, sawn-off Draco, Judge Docker, of
N.S.W., distinguished himself the other day at
Dubbo by passing the utterly disgraceful sen-
tence of two years’ imprisonment on a girl for
using instruments to procure abortion. Where
her case differed from the ordinary one was
that she used the instruments on herself. Also,
the jury found her guilty with a strong recom-
mendation to mercy, and Judge Docker
(according to the Evening News report) said
“ he was very glad to receive the recommenda-
tion, and would give the utmost effect to it; ”
therefore, the inference is that, but for the said
recommendation, the miserable girl might have
got three or four or five years. The sentence
was not only utterly merciless, and at least 10
times as much as the case called for, but,
in view of the other doings of the law,
it was hideously illogical. When the law
finds one person using instruments on another
to procure abortion, it drops heavily
on the person who performed the act,
but his or her accomplice, the woman who
voluntarily submitted to the operation, almost
invariably, if not quite invariably, goes free.
The law has tacitly decided, in any number of
cases, that her share of the transaction is not
criminal. The decision is illogical, certainly,
but it is the law’s decision. And when the law
falls like an avalanche on the one woman who
had no accomplice and didn’t lead any one else
into her alleged crime, the only possible infer-
ence is that the law is hungry after the blood
of somebody, and if it can’t get its usual victim
it will rather jump on the person whom it has
hitherto decided to be innocent than not jump
on anybody at all. Again, the law connives
at abortion by allowing nostrums for the
“restoration of regularity” to be openly
advertised and sold, without taking any steps
against either sellers or buyers. And the law
which fixes the average price of attempted
suicide at about three months, and murderous
assault by a push at about four weeks, is
a senseless horror when it piles a sentence
of 24 months on the w T retchea girl who merely
attempts bo escape from social disgrace at her
own risk. She inflicted no serious injury on
anybody but herself, and two years’ gaol, on
top of her personal sufferings and the scoffs of
Orthodox Respectability, is a very brutal pro-
cess of jumping on a very small weak victim
when she is down. Even the bilious and acrid
Docker might have found a better subject on
which to demonstrate his utter and well-proved
unfitness for his position.
Claimed that the Reid Ministry has shown
more inclination to restrict or abolish capital
punishment than any preceding N.S.W. Minis-
try. The Reid Ministry has commuted sen-
tences, certainly ; but what previous Ministry
has had the opportunities of the present one for
legislatively abrogating the death-penalty, or
restricting it solely to cases of murder in the
first degree ? JOHN Haynks placed on the
records of the House a second-reading majority
in favor of a Bill for abolition, and, although
the Bill was defeated afterwards, the Reid
Ministry, had it put its shoulder to Haynes’
Bill, could have lifted it over the obstacles that
blocked it. If the Reid Ministry wants credit
for humanity or a desire for more rational
methods of punishment, it will need to exert
itself legislatively as well as administratively.
Cabinet commutations of death-sentences, while
leaving the Statute-book loaded as it is, tends
in a clumsy but effectual fashion to prevent
reform of the law, because the people, relying
on the Cabinet to commute, will not bring pres-
sure on Parliament to amend or abolish.
Brisbane’s Anglican Bishop Webber thinks
that kanakas on Q. plantations are among the
happiest and best-treated of the inhabitants of
this terrestrial ball. Brisbane Courier backs
up Kneebreeches, declaring :
Those who allege slavery should prove it. Is it a fair
thing to charge a British community with this foul
wrong against humanity upon flimsy evidence or no evi-
dence at all ?
Ad captandum ! The slavery—the purchase of
labor from island chiefs—has been proved over
and over again. Webber’s little say-so is ex-
parte, for he is interested in what his church
calls the “kanaka missions” in Queensland.
The Courier foolishly opens a gate for its
enemies :
We admit frankly that if the inevitable result of this
contact was contamination and deterioration it would be
an obligation of humanity not to bring them here.
The almost inevitable result (grammatically
singular this time) is—death. Next, the
Courier barracks for the employment of British
Papuans on Q. sugar-plantations, because “ on
both sides of Torres Straits the natives would
be under the protection of British authority.”
Are not Australian aboriginals now under that
alleged protection ? Yet the Q. native black
has been practically excluded from work by
recent law and regulation, and is snuggled
against his will in the bosoms of his latest god-
fathers, Archie Meston and Parry-Okeden.
The Courier lets the cat out of the bag by
telling how this would “ practically be a settle-
ment of the vexed question of Queensland em-
ployers ever obtaining plantation laborers from
New Guinea.” Just so. Use the name of
Humanity, use the name of Civilisation, use the
name of the Almighty, but, by any means and
every means, keep up the supply of cheap,
colored labor for Fat Man, and let the obstinate
Lean White Man die.
4.
An indecorous judicial wink did duty lately for
a long-demanded reform of the court practice
which deals with the position an accused should
occupy when on trial. In an illegal-practice
case, at Dubbo, counsel requested Judge
Docker to allow accused to remain on the
floor of the court, outside the dock. Judge
Docker : “ Don’t raise the question, and then
I’ll not consider my attention directed to it.”
Counsel, it is needless to say, sat down, and
accused remained throughout the case outside
the dock. This was right in its way ; but is it
not derogatory to every sense of fitness that, in
the Temple of Justice, this right of an accused
can only be obtained by such a miserable sub-
terfuge ? An indicted person should be simply
(Retained ; only when violence is apprehended
from him, or when it is known that lie plans
escape, should the restraint be of a physical
kind. Ordinarily, the dock should be kept, if
maintained at ali, as a place for the guilty to
stand while sentence is being pronounced upon
them. The innocent—the not proven guilty—
should not be put on view to Judge and jury
over its spikes or through its compromising
bars.
Sydney Telegraph gives prominence to the
Privy Council decision in the Maoriland case of
Union Bank of Australia v. Murray-Aynsley
and Archer. The Supreme Court had decided
against the bank ; on appeal the Full Court, by
a majority-vote of three to one, had confirmed
the first judgment; but now the Privy Council
unanimously sets aside the verdicts and gives
judgment for the Bank. Such reversals happen
from time to time under the legal systems of
all countries, but the D.T. means, by prominent
display at this juncture, to supply data for
maintaining the existence of the Privy Council
as an adjunct to the Australian Federal Court.
Such data are, however, beside the main ques-
tion. The main question is, should not every
self-contained country be able to satisfy its
sense of justice within its own boundaries ?
Or, should not every country with power to
make laws possess the absolute power of inter-
preting them ?
“ Annie Marx ” to The Bulletin
Let Vic. Chief Secretary Peacock cast about for some
way of making his Factories Act much more operative.
Dozens of factories and hundreds of restaurants, coffee-
palaces, &c., break the regulations as to hours of work
and half-holidays, and no check is placed upon this
illegal form of sweating, simply because the inspectors
don’t know, and the girls—who know only too well—have
their mouths closed by fear of dismissal. Last Oct. a
fine, healthy country girl came to Melb. to see her mar-
ried sister. She liked the city, and, meeting an old
sohoolmate then employed as waitress, stayed on, and
soon took a billet at the city restaurant where her mate
worked. Last week, after a long illness, she wearily dragged
herself to the station, and returned to her country
home, to try and recover her lost health. During all
these months she got her half-holiday a week—from 2.30
p.m.—that was all. Otherwise she worked 761 hours per
week for the noble wage of seven shillings. Allowing a
similar sum as the equivalent of her board, she
was paid for her health and strength in some
of the best days of her life at the princely
rate of 2 l-sd. per hour ! There were seven of her in that
eating-place, and they slept—or tried to sleep—all through
the nights of last summer, in a room just 14ft. x 14ft.,
which"was also their sitting-room. This den has but one
window, which looks on the backyard, and consequently
receives all the vile smells of the muck-heap of a 6d. eat-
ing-house, and is situated right over the kitchen 1 Wasn’t
there a fuss made over a Black Hole in Calcutta, and was
it any worse than this? And there are in Melbourne to-
day hundreds, if not thousands, more in like case to this
girl—nay, worse ! For the restaurant has a good name,
and the girls themselves say they are not so badly off as
in some places they know of, which is one reason why they
won’t speak—though not the main one. Yet the Fac-
tories’ Act, 1896, provides that waitresses shall not work
more than 52 hours per week, nor more than nine hours
in one day (sec. 41). For all which reasons I cordially
invite the Chief Secretary to devise some plan whereby
his inspectors may put the Act in force “ on their own,”
without causing the dismissal of any of these women, who
badly need relief, but who dare not try to obtain it for
themselves.

SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Royal “ White Heather."
Her Majesty’s “The Gondoliers.”
Lyceum “ Right's Right.”
Royal Standard Henry’s Dramatic Co.
Tivoli . Rickards Co.
MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Princess “ The Harbor Lights.”
Opera House Rickards Variety Co.
Town Hall Newbury’s Saturday “ Pops.”
Gaiety Theatre Federation Minstrel Co.
The Pattie Browne co. is filling in the last
week of its Sydney season with “Jane,” and that
hilarious trifle is good enough to make up for even
the numerous failings of “Nancy.” “Jane” is
not new to Sydney, but it is so long since Myra
Kemble’s production of the cheery little shriek
that it is about as good as new. The visible moral
of “Jane” is that, in a world which is full of
young woman, it is desperately hard to get a young
woman when she is most wanted, even when the
man who wants her is ready to offer £lOO for 24
hours loan of her. Cecil Ward is the man
who wants the loan of a girl so badly, his
trouble being that some eccentric relative left
him a large income on condition he got married,
and he took the shortest way out of the difficulty
by saying that he was married. Then three years
later he has to borrow a girl at two hours’ notice
in order to convince a dreadful trustee that he
hasn’t been getting money by false-pretences. He
finally gets the loan of servant-girl Jane ; and
Jane is rushed off in such haste to buy her new
clothes that she hasn’t time to explain things to
her husband, flunkey William, with whom she has
just contracted a secret marriage. Therefore flun-
key William, when he suddenly finds his wife in
purple and fine linen, receiving sassiety in the
drawing-room, and she coldly orders him to bring
in coals, thinks at first tint it is a horrid dream.
Later, he concludes that it is bigamy. And when
Cecil Ward and his borrowed Jane get the loan of
an infant, and proudly exhibit it as their
own podgy infant, he decides that it is
gross immorality. The sufferings of husband
William while he waits at dinner are painful. He
swells up with smothered rage till his buttons fly
off and get into the soup, and whenever his
anguish is more awful than usual his bride
threatens him publicly with dismissal, and coldly
tells him to go and boil the front window or post
the coal-scuttle in time for the evening’s meal, or
something like that. William’s instincts as a
well-trained servant make it impossible for him to
give back-talk to anything in the outward guise of
the “ missus, ” even though she is his own wife
and fellow-servant Jane; he bursts with rage in
the background, but the torn remains of him run
on errands as actively as ever, and come back more
or less drunk and despairing. And Jane’s fiendish
ingenuity in ordering her flunkey about is a whole
essay on the cruelty of fluffy womanhood when
unexpectedly clothed in gorgeous things that are
bought on credit.
• * * •
Pattie Browne's Jane is good all through ;
she is bright and pretty and cheeky and full of
riot and vim, and the audience can understand the
slavery of her torn William. The Bulletin
wouldn’t like to swear that she is batter artisti-
cally than the far-off Jane of Myra Kemble, now
dimly visible on the far horizon of time ; but at
least she is very close up indeed. Berkeley, as
husband William, is a fine piece of comedy; Cecil
Ward is the excellent actor that he always is ; and
Miss Athena Claudius, who has taken violently to
boy parts, and fills them with very good results,
does an exasperating imp in buttons. Oily Deer-
ing and Miss Marian Medway are the other most
conspicuous characters, the latter as a -gushing
elderly child with a retreating chin and large,
mild, cow-like eyes, whose business is to throw
herself affectionately on the chest of the world in
general with a view to matrimony, but the world
indignantly throws herself off again in scorn. The
only very bad disappointment in Saturday’s per-
formance was Miss May Browne, who was a shock
in three colors—an absolutely white frock, a black
hat, and a pink countenance.
* * * *
“ Her Good Name,” in one act, by Ber-
nard Espinasse, a well-known Sydney writer, is
thrown in along with “Jane.” It is a strong and
picturesque streak of gloom. A guilty wife comes
home in evening-dress and learns, partly from his
words, mostly from the dreadful green stare in his
eye, that her husband Knows All. Her paramour
drops in also, and, being a dull man, he stays on
instead of taking warning by that eye and bolting
for his life into the next continent. The note of
warning which the lady tries to pass him is
gloomily intercepted and used as a pipe-light.
The end of it is the suicide of the paramour in the
next room ; his baleful friend with the green eye
persuades him that he has swallowed an awful
Eoison in his coffee, and is doomed to die of
orrible pains, and he rushes to make an end
of himself. Then the strong man with the green
eye forgives his wife, and things end just the way
the audience didn’t expect. The massive Frank
Macvicars and pretty Beatrice Day are the two
strong features of the piece, the scared and sinful
wife being the best thing Miss Day has yet done
in Sydney. As for the paramour, he is hereby let
off with a caution on condition that he puts <£ll6B
in the poor-box.

The Firm’s new comic opera co. com-
mences at Her Majesty’s on Saturday in the ever-
welcome “Gondoliers.”
** * *
The present Sydney Lyceum drama,
_“ Right’s Right, ” arouses The Bulletin to more
interest than it generally expends on melodrama.
It is a rather clever and well-constructed tale of
two orphans, whose mother was dropped over-
board or otherwise disposed of in their childhood,
whereupon their wicked relative loses the two
infants and shakes the family estate. One girl is
found by a hard-drinker in a public-house, and he
reforms and brings her up happily as his own
daughter ; while a bow-legged criminal carefully
trains the other in the way she should go with a
waddy. By the time the drama proper gets fairly
started, she has been trained so successfully that
the waddy no longer makes any impression on her,
and nothing but the gentle influence of a fire built
under her seems likely to produce any moral effect.
Miss Watts-Phillips represents this aggressive
Ishmael with good effect; she is a torn hurricane
in broken boots and a wild mat of hair, and so
hardened that when she is shut up in the dark
with the rats she is prepared to give three rousing
cheers for the rats, and to take things with per-
fect philosophy. Then the man who stole the
property, not having sense enough to leave well
alone, lays dark schemes for abducting the other
sister, and the two girls meet, and the strange re-
sult is that the Ismaelite is tamed and led away to
be washed and to have her hair combed. In-
directly, and by devious ways, the washing of that
girl leads to the downfall of the gentleman who
embezzled the property ; he comes down violently
about 11.15 p.m., and the law, which goes remark-
ably fast on the stage, crumples him up in about
five minutes. He leaves behind him memories of
a very finely-staged drama, and particularly some
very beautiful views of the Thames—a river which
flows through more melodrama than any other in
history, and which is chiefly useful as a place for
the rightful heir to be thrown into, and to have
bridges built across it so that the father can stand
on them and think of his lost child. In addition
to the lady above-mentioned, Miss Helen Burdett
gives every satisfaction as the clean sister, who is
a very pretty and winsome sister indeed; and
Diver, Alf. Boothinan, Frank Harcourt, and
several others call for some considerable applause.
* * * *
(i Apparently, the reported retirement of
“White Heather ” from Sydney Royal was a pre-
mature rumor, for that drama, now in its sixth
week, still crowds the old big house every night,
and seems quite capable of keeping up for a few
weeks more, if called upon. The “waters under
the earth, ” where Baker walks on the sea-bottom
with the compromising document under his arm, is
an effect which even so elaborate a sensationalist
as Bland Holt will find it difficult to replace,
unless he can work in a balloon, or a view of Para-
dise, or some similar trifle.
