Hard to imagine a more brainlessly wan-
ton outrage than the recent poisoning by arsenic
of 10 out of the 12 pumas at Sydney Zoo. The
puma (the panther or ‘ ‘ painter ” of the American
backwoods man, and the “lion ” of South Ameri-
can vernacular) is otherwise a particularly interest-
ing feline, as, more than any of its congeners,
presenting in its head, habits and motions a gigan-
tic replica of the ‘ ‘ harmless necessary cat. ”
* * * *
“The Sign of the Cross,” which was to
celebrate a 250th Australian performance with a
souvenir on Tuesday of this week, will close its
Kerristian career, at Melb. Princess’s, on Friday.
The bill for Saturday is “Harbor Lights,” by
Pettitt and Sims. Eleven years have tided out
since this breezy melodrama was first seen on the
same stage, and of the original cast only Mrs.
Bryer will appear again this time. Phil Beck is
dead, Pattie Browne otherwise engaged ; Elton,
H. H. Vincent, Herbert Flemming, Isabel Morris
and Louise Davenport have gone away. Julius
Knight is to play the hero, the festive Bert Gilbert
takes Elton’s old part, Miss Florence Terry re-
places Pattie Browne, and Miss Ferrar and Miss
Hughes will be more or less persecuted by the
villain in the foreground of the show. A lot of
new scenery has been painted for “ Harbor
Lights,” including an up-to-date battleship, and if
the situation in China should grow more warlike
this week the battleship will be expected to get a
great reception. In any case, the band will revive
Rule Britannia on a scale of great splendor.

A sudden condemnation of the playhouse
at Perth (W.A.), will, it is said, cause Charles
Holloway’s co. to appear at Melb. Bijou shortly,
in conjunction with Rignold. This official closing
of the Perth theatre when Holloway was first on
the job, after it had been occupied by a succession
of travelling managers, is understood to be due to
defective means of exit, or something. Anyway,
the surprise is a bad thing for Holloway and
others, and the local press seems inclined to make
significant remarks on the subject.
* * * *
Biondi’s new farce at Melb. Opera House
contains six characters, whereas his previous farce
contained only four, and, as the six people chase
one another even faster than the other four
travelled, it is truly hard to believe that Biondi is
the sole mover in this lively disturbance. The
make-up in five cases is pretty elaborate, the most
frequent character being Biondi pure and simple
as a restaurant waiter, and, though the waiter
looks slightly rumpled towards the finish, his com-
posure amidst the hurly-burly of quick changes is
the most wonderful point in the show. Also, it ex-
plains how he can possibly get through without a
hitch m the proceedings. The artist, seemingly,
rushes from dresser to dresser—there are seven of
them behind the scene—but is never bustled. To
the orchestra part of his entertainment Biondi has
added a picture of conductor Zelman —“old Zel-
man,” of Melbourne —with his little military.
Miss DOROTHY VANE , of “ the Firm's ”

hia historical shock of hair. The imitation is
rather less ragged and dusty than the original, and
the sham nose has a glaze of newness on it, but
when Zelman gets seasoned he will be a huge
success. In fact, the hairy identity fetched a loud
bang of applause first time he popped up.

The success of Robert Brough’s forthcom-
ing comedy season (it opens in October) at Mel-
bourne Bijou will depend on the prices charged for
admission. If Brough can’t bring himself down
to the 35., 25., and Is. tariff, he must be prepared
for much the same treatment that Cartwright and
co. received at the same house. The chances are
that he will not bring’ a stronger selection of pieces
than Rickards put up, and, although he has an
old reputation as a high-class comedy merchant, it
will hardly serve for long to crowd the benches
which were empty when “Tree of Knowledge,”
and other good stuff, was being produced at the
old ss. maximum rate.
** * *
The last Newbury “Pop” suffered some-
what from influenza, but business was as good as
ever, and the multitude again took kindly to John
Coleman. Concerning which the inflexible musical
critic observes, “It takes a good many different
sorts of people to make-up a world . . There is the
sort that can thoroughly enjoy these Colemanesque
performances, and the sort that are lost in amaze-
ment at what the others are laughing at.”
Well, the sort that feel surprised when somebody
is amused by the Scarecrow Dance ought not to
take part in making up a world —not this world,
anyhow. They should resolve themselves into in-
gredients of the world to come.

concluded a season at Melb. Athenaeum Hall on

and the bull-frog at suburban halls, prior to rolling

melodious lady, is a very good co., and not too
holy in tone.
Miss Dorothy Vane.
Miss Dorothy Vane, the mezzo-soprano for “The
Firm’s” comic opera co., is charming by reason of
her naturalness and vivacity. She makes her Aus-
tralian de.but this Saturday, at Sydney Her
Majesty’s. She is an English girl, with a musical
father, and a mother who has not even yet become
reconciled to her being on the stage. Miss Vane
studied music, elocution, the organ and piano, at
London Guildhall for four years. She began her
stage career with six months in the chorus at Man-
chester, then moved up to understudy, then made a
success in “lolanthe.” She has played four
years in the English provinces, and one year as
principal soubrette in Mr. D’Oyley Carte’s
00. at the Savoy. Miss Vane is a fair-
haired girl, sft. high. She was introduced to the
Prince of Wales by the Countess of Warwick : the
Prince complimented her on her performances, and
the Countess gave her a sapphire-and-ruby brooch.
Miss Vane claims that the chief factor in her
success has been hard work. She says—“l am
not going to tell Australians how old I am ; but I
will tell them that coming out in the Ophir I
became engaged to be married. It is so lonely on
board ship, and I was all alone ; and that lonely
feeling is responsible for so many things. I have
not yet thought about the fixing of my marriage-
day. Coming out, too, I played cards —for
money!—and won more than I lost. I have felt
very homesick since I have been in Sydney. On
Sunday afternoon I broke down and had a good cry.
I have been out of England only once before, that
was on a five months’ trip to Africa. But as we
were a company 45 strong travelling together I
never got down. lam mercurial; when I’m up
I’m up ! Bight up ! When I’m down —as lam
after the evening’s work—well, I’m limp. I can-
not get hungry out here, somehow; I’ve been
doing nothing but starve since I’ve been in Sydney.
I don’t like beginning on the 13th of the month ;
I’m very fidgety about it; 13 is an unlucky num-
ber. I hope I meet a lot of black cats on Saturday
morning. I never start anything on Friday. I
wouldn’t walk under a ladder for £lOO. Cross-
eyed Women are very lucky to me. On board ship
I turned my money at sight of the new moon. I
do not wear jewellery; I’d sooner have a pretty
frock than a diamond brooch The black wig
which I wear as Pitti Sing caused the Johannes-
burg people to mistake me for a Jewess. The
more I work the more nervous I get; Ido most of
my study in bed of a morning before breakfast; I
learn very quickly. I cannot fix my mind on work
here yet; I’m too restless and homesick j I want
to bore a hole through the world and have a look
at my people on the other side. The part
which I _ have been brought out specially
to play is The Doll in ‘La Poupee. ’ Mr.
Musgrove said that I would make a most
perfect doll in the pretty, quaint gowns. The
performance, on which Mr. Musgrove engaged me
was Phoebe in ‘The Yeomen of the Guard.’ In
my opinion it is the best test of my abilities, since
the character combines singing, dancing, comedy,
pathos. Phoebe has brought me all the luck J
.ever had, and I’m sorry I don’t begin here in it.”
At Poverty Point.
John Coleman asserts with emphasis that he didn’t pet
his famous Scarecrow Dance from “ Ashcroft, the solid
man, who died about 12 years back.” He derived the
Scarecrow idea, he says, from a drunken man in the
Bowery, New York. Ashcroft, a clever Irish comedian
in his day, never did the dance, nor anything: like it;
neither did he die a dozen years ago. Ashcroft lives and
moves and has his being as proprietor of the Alhambra
Music Hall, Belfast, from which address he lately dropped
a line to his old friend Coleman, and will, presumably, in
due course send word to The Bulletin disclaiming the
authorship of the Scarecrow aforesaid.

wards.” As the clever .young pair have a couple of young
children, let us hope that the story of reconciliation is
true.

Kate Terry, a famous actress ere her younger sister,
Ellen, had been heard of, was about to take to the stage
again in April last when she fell ill and was ordered away
from London. Kate Terry retired into private life on her
marriage with Lewis, the wealthy draper, some five-and-
thirty years ago, and if she comes back to the old work
after ail she will draw about the most wrinkled and bald-
headed first-night audiences that ever was.

Dear Bulletin. —So Pattie Browne —with salary rang-
ing from £l5 to £3O per week —had four parts written for
her in England 1 Also, she states that “ the best comedy
line she knows was Tith.’s lovely line in ‘Sophia’ : ‘lf
you object to the shirt, I’ll shift it.’” Lovely, indeed !
And so very original in thought. Previous to the birth
of Pattie there was a London comic song interspersed
with “ patter,” and the patterer, pretending to read a
letter from his girl, would say, “ Dear Bill, I’ve stolen
your shirt to make a shift, so you must make a shift
without it.”— Cockney.
Julius Knight is bent on playing Hamlet and Romeo.
He is working at the parts, and will probably be seen in
them ere many months have passed. The ambition is, no
doubt, laudable. If Knight has real brains, it is, in jus-
tice to himself, about time he sought an opportunity of
more fully demonstrating the fact. At present his repu-
tation rests mostly upon his limbs and such melodramatio
nonsense as his Marcus Superbus and Napoleon.
It is 37 years since Madame Sohier, the pioneer wax-
figure entrepreneuse in Australia, visited the N.S.W.
backblocks. Her son has just returned from a similar
tour, having met many who clearly remembered his
mother’s visit.
The cheerfulness with which the crowd that is being
sung to by a serenaded somebody yells “ encore" is
sublime. Lately, on a frosty night, with the glass down
to 35 (it fell presently to 30 ; and this was at Broken Hill,
too) Amy Sherwin was warbled out on to a hotel balcony.
She pleaded vainly that it was too cold for a response,
and when there were shouts of disappointment (from the
crowd of listeners, not from the warblers) she heaved a
hint at them wrapped up in a verse of “ Home, Sweet
Home.” But they didn’t git; they just stayed and.
yelled “ Encore,” “ Go on,” till Madame disappeared.
Dear Bulletin. —A Bulletin correspondent has a say
about the “big cats.” Well, he may know all about them,
but I don’t think so. I have known every trained ( i.e .,
performing) lion and tiger that has been worked in this
country for more than 20 years, and have been personally
acquainted with all the animal men, from Fernandez up.
In addition, I have reared, trained, and performed wild
animals with no more serious accident than a torn coat.
You can make a lion or tiger change its nature if you
know your work and are fond of it, and leave alone
what circus-men call “ shiker.” (There are always lots
of people who want to shout for the “ Lion King.")
Why, Psyche—a performing lioness owned by me—was as
tame as a cat with those she knew, and she gloried in her
work ; but, then, Psyche was brought up from a small
cub on warmed milk and raw-meat juice administered
per a baby’s bottle by a game little woman who would
stand no nonsense.—A.F.R.
Clara Butt, the famous English contralto, was recently
thrown from a carriage and sustained concussion of the
brain.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Premier Turner proposes to raise the first of the
three proposed Vic. loans (£1,000,000) locally, and, pos-
sibly, the second also. The Bulletin desires to suggest
that it money can be borrowed locally (which it certainly
can) it can be raised locally by taxation just about as
easily, and much more fairly, and with no more burden
on the community. Borrowing locally means that Govt,
gets £1,000,1)00 (which it has no intention of ever repay-
ing) from (say) 50 people. It is invested in works, which
yield (say) 3 per cent., and the 3 per cent, is handed over
to these 50 people as interest. If the money is raised by
taxation, the Govt, gets £1,000,000 (which it has no inten-
tion of ever repaying) from (say) 1,000,000 people, and in-
vests it in the same works which yield the same 3 per cent.,
and the 1,000,000 people who advanced the money get the 3
per cent, just as the 50 people would do, only in the form of
reduced taxation instead of in cash. And, in either case,
if the works don’t yield 3 per cent., the people have to
stand the loss. The Bulletin respectfully asks a dunder-
headed community to consider this point. Supposing,
when Govt, wanted to borrow a million in a
community of a million people, and instead of get-
ting (say) £20,000 each from 50 people it bor-
rowed £1 from each person, wttich would be exactly
the same thing, then the situation would begin to become
apparent. For everybody would owe himself £1 and pay
himself his own interest by taking it out of his pocket
and handing it to himself and giving himself a receipt.
The advantage of raising £1,000,000 for public works by
local taxation instead of by local loan is that it prevents
the 950,000 people ultimately drifting into slavery to the
other 50,000 ; it saves the expense of constantly raising
and distributing the interest; and there are no paper
securities to ultimately depart out of the country when the
holder goes to Yurrup in search of a title and thus makes
another addition to the long list of absentees who drain
this country of its wealth. Otherwise the two things are
exactly equal.
* • * •
Premier Turner reports that he has a scheme on
hand for the conversion of the many different varieties of
Victorian debt into a uniform 3 per cent, stock. This
suggests, firstly, either that Turner doesn’t believe
Federation is coming, or that he doesn’t expect the
Federal Govt, to take over the State debts. For if Feder-
ation is coming; and the Commonwealth takes over the
State debts (as it will do if it intends to be any kind of
Commonwealth worth mentioning) it is quite obvious that
the Federal Govt, with its vastly superior credit, could
convert the provincial loans on far better terms than Vic-
toria could do by itself. And if Victoria has a local loan
conversion now, and there is a Federal one later on, the
only result of the first operation will be needless waste of
the gum-sucker’s legal tender.
Assuming that Turner’s local loan conversion
comes off, the method of it should prove interesting. The
first essential is to make the new stock interminable,
reserving to the Government the right of repayment at
any time after, say, 20 years. For the constant renewal
of loans is a huge expense, and their compulsory renewal
at a fixed date, even if the market is utterly unfavorable,
means disaster some day. For instance, Victoria has
£7,000,000 falling due in one year, and the year after
N.S.W. has £17,000,000, and Queensland £13,000,000 fall-
ing due. And if these two years find Europe up to its
neck in a great disastrous war, money may be unobtainable
below 6 or 7 per cent., and these provinces may have to
either pay that price for renewal, or else default. Also,
another essential is to put the conversion on a reasonably
honest basis. The object of every conversion is to reduce
interest, and in practically every case the saving of in-
terest is only effected by a great increase of prin-
cipal. Then the usual course is to leave posterity
to pay off the extra principal and grab the reduc-
tion of interest for the benefit of the present
generation—which is really only another way of borrow-
ing money for revenue purposes. If it were made part
of the bargain that the amount saved annually in interest
was to go towards paying off the principal the conversion
idea would have a lot more to recommend it than it has—
barring the fact that the promise about so applying the
saving wouldn’t be kept. Another wise idea would be to
put the amount saved into a Public Works Fund for rail-
way-building purposes so as to diminish future borrowing
—only that promise wouldn’t be kept either.
• * * *
So far as things have gone both the N.S.W.
land and income taxes appear to be declining sources of
revenue. The 1896 land-tax panned out for £263,468 ;
1897 land-tax, £232,636. The 1896 income-tax yielded
£193,575, and the 1897 income-tax, £169,020. There are
still arrears to come in, but apparently not enough to
make up the deficiency.
* * * *
Westralia has struck several streaks of hard luck
in the London money market besides the present one,
and is getting used to being kicked by its uncle Bull.
The £1,100,000 worth of 3 per cent, stock given in pay-
ment for the Great Southern Railway was offered at £9B,
and only £233,000 was taken. Then a loan of £1,000,000
hung fire, the amount offered being only £750,000. The
present £1,000,000 loan only brought in £550,000, despite
the very low minimum. Also, £1,000,000 of 4 per cent.
Treasury bills are vaguely understood to have been sent
’Ome, a long while back, for the Agent-General to raise
the wind upon, and, so.far as is known, they are still on
hand.
• * * *
Maoriland State Land Bank, at 31st March, ’9B,
had advanced £1,405,965 on mortgage. Out of £71,545,
due during the year as interest and repayments, the
amounts over one month in arrear were only £922, and
none of the arrears went back over 12 months. Also
there was only one doubtful security among all the mort-
gages. If any private institution can make as good a
showing, its name and address will be thankfully received.
* • * *
Cash in Victorian banks for the quarter just
ended £6,708,122, a decline of £463,000 as compared with
previous quarter. Ditto in N.S.W. banks £5,885,537, a
decline of £341,000. Even with the £1,500,000 which
Premier Reid lately borrowed in London to keep the era
of Jolly and Fat afloat a while longer, N.S.W. doesn’t
show any too well.
* * * *
The most dismal bank on earth —the Standard
Bank of Australia—has decided to formally give up the
ghost and liquidate. One obvious reason for this course
is that its alleged £1,308,393 of assets are estimated to be
really worth about £90,000 (£435,000 of the alleged assets
are accumulated loss and depreciation, and £520,000 are
doubtful debts taken at face value, and many of the other
items are dubious and only worth a little in the £).
Another good reason is that the loss has grown larger and
the income smaller ever since 1894, till, for the half-year
ended 31st Dec., 1897, the bank’s earnings were £1246 and
its outgoings £10,749. A bank whose expenses are nearly
nine times its receipts is about the deadest thing imagin-
able. The final reason for abandoning the struggle is
that £300,000 of reconswindled deposits fall due
this week, and there is about £5OOO cash to
meet them with. The best that is hoped for in the
dreadful case of the Standard Bank is that the
shareholders will lose all their capital (£552,000) and
•depositors will get about 2s. in the £ if they have very
good luck ; if they have moderate luck, the dividend may
be anything from Od. to Is. 6d. There are over £700,000
•of deposits altogether, and they have been reconstructed
three times ; and for at least five years the depositors
have been getting their very moderate interest (it is only
2J per cent.) out of their principal and have thus lived by
eating their own tail It will be a joyous thing to hear
the last of the Standard Bank, but presumably there are
years of slow liquidation before it, and its name will be
preserved in the land for a very long time yet.
* • * *
The Mutual Life Insurance co. of New York re-
ports that the total premiums for 1897 were £8,029,813,
which is £172,222 more than t hose for 1896. Considera-
tion for annuities was £704,249, an increase of £273,500 ;
and interest and rents were £2,158,262, an increase of
£82,507 ; also, £420,509 profit was made on the realisation
of securities over and above their book-value. The total
result was an addition of £3,910,120 to the funds, which
now stand at £52,112,204, and the surplus of assets over
liabilities has risen to £7,291,210.
The Mutual of New York realises about £4 Gs.
6d. per cent, interest on its investments, which is very good
as rates go in the U.S., though below the present
Australian average. And its expenses amount to rather
over £lB 4s. per cent, on total receipts as compared with
something over £2O per cent, last year, so the rate, though
still somewhat high, shows a considerable improvement.
One rather curious announcement is that all policies
issued on and after January last are to be based on the
assumption that the funds will, in future, yield 3£ per
cent, interest ; hut apparently the co. leaves its old
policies on the previous 4 per cent, basis. And it is inti-
mated that the premium rates have been “adjusted”—
which, presumably, means increased—to correspond with
the new rates. The A.M.P. started reducing its future
estimates to 3 A per cent, some time earlier, though it was
earning much higher interest than the Mutual of New
York, and, therefore, had less visib'e need of such a pre-
caution. Also, it reduced its estimate, not merely on its
new policies, but on all its policies, and it did this without
any alteration of its premium rates. The Maoriland State
Life Department and the Mutual Life Association are
doing the same without increase of rates, despite the fact
that their premiums are both much lower than those of
the American office. The Citizens’ ordinary life branch
also values at the 3£ per cent, rate all round. And another
large American office doing business in Australia —the
New York Life Insurance Co. —now estimates its future
interest receipts at the still safer limit of 3 per cent., and
applies this valuable precaution not only to its new
policies, but to all its policies alike : and it also made the
change without any readjustment of premium rates. Un-
doubtedly the reduction of the estimates on which new
business is based to 3J per cent, is a wise and valuable
precaution, and the pity is that several Australian offices
don’t go and do likewise, instead of adhering to the old 4
per cent., which threatens sooner or later to become
unsafe. But if the Mutual of New York had applied this
precaution all round, even at the sacrifice (on paper) of
part of its big surplus, it would have been still wiser.
* * * *
The Civil Service Mutual Building and Invest-
ment Society (Sydney) shows a profit of £5Ol for the year
ended 30th June, ’9B, as compared with £359 last year, and
£453 in ’96. The profit equals a little over 3 j per cent, on
capital, and a div. of 2£ per cent, absorbs 379 of this (the
board has a quaint way of declaring a dividend for the
second half of the year and missing the other half, and in
this peculiar little fashion it manages to call its 2J per
cent. div. 5 per cent.). Also, £3OO is put to the reserve
fund, making it £BOO, but, as the income isn’t large
enough to cover this, most of it comes out of previous
profit-and-loss balance, which is reduced to £29. The
great trouble with this institution is its expense account—
the total year’s receipts are £1304, and expenses swallow
up £659 of this, or more than half. Five directors, a
building-surveyor, manager and staff are evidently a big
load for so small a concern to carry.
* * * *
Adelaide Critic objects to The Bulletin’s idea
of audit of banks and insurance co’s by State-appointed
auditors. It suggests that: “ What is wanted is a law
compelling every co., insurance or otlierwise, to appoint
capable, independent, professional auditors, who should
have assistance and protection from the State instead of
leaving them, as at present, to the mercy of company-
mongers who, if the auditor tries to do his duty, turn him
out without any consideration.” Well, if the State is to
compel the appointment of “ independent” auditors, and
is to protect and assist them, and deprive the directors of
the power to discharge them so that they may continue
independent, what is that but State audit ?
• * » *
Ex-Agent-General Playford returns to S.A.
convinced that it takes a man at least four years to
fathom the mysteries of London money market. He says
Australian stocks have been rigged for years by one large
syndicate which has made big sums by tendering for
loans and gradually unloading on small investors. Play-
ford made a study of its operations and secured big
successes for all his loans by correctly gauging the time
for flotation. He attributes the recent Westralian failure
to a “green” Agent-General. Playford, by the way,
returns to S.A. a martyr to rheumatic gout.
» * * *
The Bulletin advises Hooley to write—“ The
Book of Revelations.” Nothing has come up to Hooley
in the way of letting the cat out of the bag since Kirby
died. His examination in bankruptcy grows more inter-
esting every day. Hooley appears to have started with a
lot up his sleeve, so that, as soon as Lord This and Earl
That denied receiving the sums mentioned for the use of
their names, Hooley hunted up his cheque-book to prove
that they had received a good deal more. Now, one
lordly decoy-duck admits receiving “about £20,000 by
way of gifts,” but offers to return the amount to the
official receiver, who, by the way, has declared that all
those in a similar case must perforce stump up. Evi-
dently over a million went in boodle in one form or an-
other, and Hooley will probably prove that the two most
rotten institutions in London are the aristocracy and the
financial press.

The gold returns of four provinces for the first
six months of ’9B :
Westralia .. £470,6900z.
Queensland (about) .. 414,0000 z.
Victoria .. 398,1770 z.
New South Wales .. 127,2590 z.
In proportion to population the Westralian gold-return is
now about three times that of Queensland, while Vic-
toria and N.S.W. are out of sight below the horizon. If
the figures keep up the gold yield will do a lot to let
Westralia down gently from the top of its boom. And
if it wasn’t that most of the big dividends go out of the
province it might do a lot more. But, thanks to the
glorious “ influx of British capital,” Westralia’s dividends
go everywhere except to Westralia.
» * * *
Broken Hill South made a profit of <£16,100 in
its last half-year, which is the best for some time. Of the
total, £lO,OOO has been paid in divs. ; balance goes to de-
preciation account, rather neglected lately. Liquid
assets are £17,578 in excess of liabilities, as against
£13,264 at the end of the preceding half, only they are not
quite so liquid. There are much more of the uncertain
“ estimated surpluses ” in them, less cash, less stores, and
more liabilities. Which probably accounts in part tor
the directors’ announcement (not contained in the report)
that they will sell locally as much of the product as
Eossible. The concentrating mill is said to be answering ;
ut Mr. Green way, who superintends this as well as the
Block 14 works, gives precious little information on that
point. In the mine things have gone better, some good
lead ores having been met at different points ; the most
important development has been between the 700 and
800 ft. levels. Average assays, however, not given, and no
remarks passed upon this mine’s old bugbear, the appall-
ing hardness of the ore. Still, as concentrating costs have
not increased —they stand at about ss. per ton of ore—
there is apparently no change for the worse, at any rate.

The open-cut system at Broken Hill, backed by
close filling with refuse underground, has brought the
B.H. Prop.’s timber-bill from £78,748 in the first half of
’92 down to £11,762 in the first half of ’9B. The figures
for the corresponding periods of ’96 and ’97 were re-
spectively £21,906 and £17,039.
After being drenched and smothered for 15
months, the fire in the workings of the B.H. Prop, is still
active enough to overcome with its fumes five men who
incautiously descended a pass in the walled-in area. But
this time ail were got out alive.
9 * * *
Miners, &c., to the number of about 300 have
gone on strike at the Gibraltar mine, Adelong, N.S.W.
Hitherto miners have been getting Bs. 4d. a day, and now
they are asked to work for 7s. 6d., the standard rate of
miners’ wages for many years in Vic., but not in N.S.W.,
where it varies—the greater number receiving the Bs. 4d.
rate. The local directors say the mine is not paying as
well as anticipated, that up to date over £140,000 has been
spent, and thedivs., so far, only amount to £15,000 ; that
the mine is well ventilated and the men’s comfort other-
wise studied; also, their work is made easier by the use
of drilling-machines. If the strike is continued there is
little doubt the men will go down—and so, too, will the
co. in the long run. The rock in the Gibraltar is as hard
as anything in N.S.W. All good miners are not good
machine-drill men, and there is a big demand for air-
drillers in several N.S.W. camps, notably Cobar. The
best of the Adelong men will no doubt go where they can
get better wages. Other managements quite as capable
as that of the Gibraltar (the Gibraltar directors aren’t
mining men) have found it false economy to cut miners’
wages. Much better keep well-trained men at Bs. 4d. than
break in new ones at 7s. 6d., especially in a mine like the
Gibraltar, where the difference might be more than
doubled in explosives alone. This principle has long been
recognised in America, where the average wages paid to
miners have always been above those paid in Australia.

if the newspaper hummer is to be believed. A Melb. syn-
dicate has “despatched prospectors to Pullitop to work
wolfram and tin.” Some sluicing claim “is running off
and expects to clean up about Xmas.” The United
Trades are also “ running off,” and the Golden Cross is
breaking stone estimated to go 4oz. or 50z., and every
other mine within miles of Wagga is “looking well.”
Under which circs., probably, the Wagga paper-man is
feeling well.

Croydon items. —Croydon Consols, mostly Bull,
prospecting under difficulties for reef cut out by slide-
pumping machinery inadequate, and work in deep ground
consequently suspended. Break-down of pumping
machinery in Croydon Consols seriously retarding output
at No. 7N. Golden Gate. No. 7 looked, at latest advices,
like turning out another 3000 oz. cake. Nearly 200 tons,
estimated lOoz. per ton, on top. Big reef in sink and
bottom stopes. Golden Gate Consols shares took a
sudden jump about 25th July consequent on striking
quartz in shaft ; assay proved stone very poor. When
mail was leaving, reports in circulation that gold had been
found, and scrip was in demand. Fictitious values rule,
as regards this mine, because if Croydon Consols stop
pumping, Gate ponsols will be flooded : and here, again,
machinery inefficient. Peculiar forfeiture case at Croy-
doy. Royal Bank held Mark Twain reef for two years by
means of nominees (fact admitted in Warden’s court)
without spending a shilling in bona-fide work. Miners
applied for forfeiture on grounds of non-fulfilment of
labor-conditions. Then bank offered applicant to take a
tribute in mine, which would mean, for a start, six
months’ hard work cleaning out the caved-in shafts. Ap-
plicants declined : wanted ground to work it. Warden
declined to recommend forfeiture, while admitting that
bank had not worked the mine for two years. Minister
decided in accordance therewith. Hard lines for the field
and miners thereon. If the holder hadn’t been a bank
how would it have gone? Golden Valley,Croydon,look-
ing up. Grace Leigh, Isabella, and King of Wallabadah
all looking well and promising.
* * * *
Wired from Cairns that Schlapp has spoken de-
cisively re Chillagoe. The possibilities are too big for
words, while the probabilities are highly favorable. He
disagrees with Stewart as to the permanence of most of
the ore-bodies, which he (Schlapp) says are true fissure
lodes, and as likely to prove permanent as any in the
world. He has no hesitation in asserting that the field
will be one of the richest in the world, and the wonder
is that the railway was not built years ago by the
Govt, or a syndicate. Chillagoe will never be allowed to
remain in its present undeveloped state. He went to
the field expecting to see a good deal, but the reality far
exceeds his expectations. The public will await with in-
terest the full text of Mr. Schlapp’s deliverance.
* * * *
“ Old Miner ” : On the much-boomed Harper
Leases, Chillagoe, two shafts have been sunk. No. 1
shaft is down 120 ft. in very soft rock. About 400 tons of
good ore was taken from a bunch in this shaft, but it cut
out a few feet below the surface. Though much cross-
cutting has been carried out from the bottom of this shaft
nothing of value has been met. No. 2 shaft is down 208 ft.
in soft country; an enormous amount of exploration
has been carried out here without good result. This so-
called Harper Lode occurs in erratic surface bunches
which completely give out a few feet below the surface.
Judging by expert reports from Chillagoe, the W.A.
mining expert must be well represented there.
Dear Bulletin.—Correspondent “ Mainshaft ”
states that I took great pains to prove my right to the
last third of my name. Well, “Mamshaft” takes great
pains to prove himself a Chillagoe boomster, and his
paragraph neither proves, nor attempts to prove, any-
thing else. What the dickens has the yield of silver at
Broken Hill to do with the future of Chillagoe?—Copper-
Headed Juggins.
• * • •
Dear Bulletin.—“ Irvinebank ” writes you as
if he knew—writes with an appearance of fact that argues
his one-time employment by the old co. Also, he writes
with a nastiness that argues his abrupt dismissal by the
old co. aforesaid. And, getting down to his facts, what
are they ? He the favorable comparison between Chil-
lagoe and the Barrier and Lyell, I suppose these last
mines > were at one time in “the initial and difficult
stage,’’and it does not call for much knowledge to un-
derstand that without railways—the one thing 1 maintain
necessary to the success of Chillagoe—Mt. Lyell and the
Barrier would be to-day where they were yesterday.
Another of this person’s ‘‘ arguments ” against the success
of the new co. is that the old co.’s machinery was bought
at “ waste-paper prices ’’—which statement is emphatic-
ally untrue. And even if it were true, Mr. “ Irvinebank ”
wants us to believe that as success depended on the
“ waste-paper prices ” of worn-out machinery then, and
there is no more worn-out machinery to be purchased
at “ waste-paper prices,” the new co. must fail. Then
look at the man’s arguments and comparisons of
cost of supplies, freight and realisable value of the pro-
duct. Says the railway might save £2 per ton. And yet
he knows that freight from Mareeba (the present railway
terminus) to Chillagoe has never been under £4 per ton,
and often as high as £B. And he should know, and, if he
doesn’t, I nail the statement right here—that the cost of
packing ore at present, from Chillagoe to Mareeba, is over
£5 a ton. And then, in the attempt to make his figures
look as if they were figures and not mere hollow
lies, he mentions that copper (the metal, mark you) is
£lO a ton dearer than it was when Chillagoe had its first
perfunctory trial—and from that statement he deduces
the important conclusion that £2 extra per ton freight on
supplies, coke, &c., is more than made up for by the £lO
per ton rise in the price of the metal. And then, having
painfully shaped the house of sand, he pulls it down again
and throws mud at himself by declaring that the price of
copper is sure to have a fall ! Why, in the carriage of
fuel alone the mines could make a profit. Local charcoal,
with about half the smelting value of coke, costs 365. 6d.
per ton against £3 13s. a ton for coke; and, with a
railway to the sea and cheap coke from Japan to work on,
the difference in cost of smelters’ supplies is a profit.
Finally, under the old regime the lack of skilled manage-
ment, the absence of mining and metallurgical specialists,
ran away with what would have been profitS"under more
favorable circumstances. Geologists Jack and Skertchley,
mine-managers Stewart and Smith, and metallurgist
Schlapp, have seen these things and believe in them, and
know enough not to believe without evidence. The
country is all right—big and rich ; and the people who
run the fields down are mostly those who were too late to
get in on velvet, or the one-time who has been
enthusiastically sacked.—lrvinebank Junior.

• ♦ * *
As they chisel their way down to 3000 ft. and
more, where few men can do a sprightly six days’ work in
a week, Bendigo co’s with pocket-handkerchief claims
begin to think of amalgamation. Hitherto, co-operation
in sinking and prospecting has been practised to some
extent; but now the deeper plunge is mooted in several
places. The South Belle, Yire and Eureka (the latter is
crosscutting at 2900 ft.) will probably lead.
♦ * * *
The Garden Gully—the “good old Garden
Gully ” —suddenly springs to the front. For years, it was
mostly in the first half-dozen of Vic.’s payers, several
times it was right ahead of all. In 25 years it paid 296
divs. Then hard times came, and scrip fell to a few odd
shillings, and finally it made a call, and Bendigo went
into mourning. A month or two back Mr. Lansell lent
the co. £2ooo—and Bendigo took off the mourning; for
Lansell can smell gold. And now, after only a few weeks,
4oz. stone is broken at 1448 ft., the overdraft is paid off,
an 18d. div. is sent out, something is left in hand, and
there is talk of the old show shooting again into its old
familiar place.
* * * *
So experienced a stager as Bendigo Lansell
came a cropper the other day. Adjoining one of his
mines was some private property—a pub., in short—under
which he wanted to mine ; and he took what he thought
a short-cut by buying Bung out. Promptly thereupon
an Ikey Moss popped in an application to mine on private
property, and pegged out the pub. Held that Lansell had
bought the land but not the gold ; wherefore an adjourn-
ment, “ to allow of a settlement ” —which means, in out-
side-court English, to give the pegger an opportunity to
get trade off the ground, if that was what he was after.
And there probably isn’t a Moss in Vic. who wouldn’t
rather have a dig at Lansell’s pocket than at the best of
the unpegged mines on Bendigo.

“ C.M.G.” : A Govt, party is employed cutting
a road along the side of a spur in the vicinity of Cassilis,
Gippslatid, and all day long weary workers lounge about
in the vicinity, smoking and watching. These latter are
enterprising prospectors shepherding on the off-chance
of a reef being cut by the road-making contingent. An
apt illustration of the local idea of the duty of “ the
blanky Guv’ment.”

shareholder gets a spell from call-paying—which, as in
the case of the North Cornish, at Daylesford, he has been
doing for longer than he can remember.

Westralian gold-production for July, 76,9800 z.
Good, but below the average. East Coolgardie—Hannan’s,
9101 oz.—a big drop to be accounted for. The other
camps are, if anything, above the average—except Cool-
gardie. None at all officially reported from that camp,
which is in a terrible plight generally. A returned pro-
fessional man paid as much as £lO a week for a small
business stand in Bayley-street three years ago, and
before he left there lately the same place was declined at
10s. a week.

Parke and Lacy Company Ltd. are manufactur-
ing in Sydney four sets of the largest rolls ever made in Aus-
tralia. They weigh 12 tons each, and the rollers are 36in.
diameter x 16in. face. They are being constructed to the
order of the Associated Gold Mines of Western Australia,
Ltd., and are of the new “Roger” type. The same com-
pany has also placed an order for four 150 ft. x 14ft. Ropp
straight-line furnaces. These will have a combined
capacity of some 200 tons per day, and are to be used for
roasting the sulphide ores for subsequent treatment. The
Broken Hill Proprietary Company, Ltd., was the pioneer
of this new type of furnace, and they have now four of
them in operation.

An old lady hauled up her alleged husband
in Wellington (M.L.) police-court for desertion, a
little while back, and told a story that reached
nearly to the Deluge. “We were married in
1850,” she said, “and had two children. He de-
serted me in 1855.” Then she went on to relate
that in all the 43 years that had intervened she
had never seen or heard of the old man until she
dropped on him casually in the street the other
day, and hauled him before the strong arm of the
law for 43 years’ arrears of maintenance. Then
the Court fixed an astonished glare on defendant,
who looked quite young enough to be the son of
the man who bolted in ’55, and in a dazed sort of
way asked the old lady if she would swear to him.
She swore to him right there. She hadn’t seen
him for 43 years, but she swore, and wanted him
taken away from his other wife and handed over
to her on the spot. Finally, the Court told the
defendant to get out and run for his life, and he
disappeared with his aged persecutor in full bat
after him. As a vigorous effort on the part of a
lady of 70 to get a husband, this episode about
takes the cake. If only people were born old, and
grew down young, and went to school late in life
to forget their information, and finally died of
premature birth or exposure in infancy, or unsuit-
able food while they were losing their teeth, a

S.A. police-force, owing to a burglary-
boom, has been recently recruited, and all new
constables had to pass an examination in geogra
phy, arithmetic, grammar, &c. The old idea that
the only qualifications necessary for a policeman
were ability to burst in the prisoner’s skull with
a club and go through him for his cash has appar-
ently exploded in S.A.

“ Milky White We hear often enough
of “ casers” and “ half - casers,” “ deeners,”
“tanners,” “thrummers,” etc., but has anyone
ever heard, in Australia, a slang name for a florin?
I believe it to be the only silver coin of the realm
that has escaped the attention of the slang artists.

The Tasmanian police system again. Some
time ago a man was arrested by a village con-
stable on a charge of criminal assault. Prosecu-
trix failed to identify, and prisoner was dis-
charged. The other day the man was travelling
through a neighboring township in search of work.
He was again arrested, kept in the lock-up a
week, and remanded to a township where the lady
concerned was supposed to reside. The magis-
trates then found that, although the man had
been discharged nearly a year before, the notice
that he was “ wanted ” still appeared in the
Government Gazette ! As each one-’possum-
power hamlet in Tasmania is a law unto itself the
unfortunate man has chances of compensation
akin to Buckley’s. Which is a Y.D.L. of a state
of things. The Braddon-Fysh Government is now
crowding a Bill through to put the island police
under one control, instead of each village appoint-
ing its own bobby who won’t arrest two-thirds of
the people because they are related to his wife,
and wants to club the other third at sight because
they don’t deal at his uncle's grocery.

cently for accepting the executorship under the
will of butcher Martin, who left <£lBo,ooo to Adel-
aide charities, but declining to receive the legacy of
£lOOO which accompanied the appointment. One

cause delay in the signature of documents.

that he receives only £4OOO a year, out of which

his staff, right -down to the cook and gardeners.
But his XXX gets a handsome revenge when he
compels the people to listen to his speeches.

The predicament of a southern church
crowd through the protracted silence of its be-
loved pastor about an advantageous offer, re-
calls an old joke —age and birth-place un-
known. The Clergyman of a poor little country
parish received a call to a large and wealthy one
in a big city. Asked time for prayer and con-
sideration. Didn’t feel sure of his light, he said.
Nearly a month passed without any further light
as to his intentions, so a parishioner, on meet-
ing the holy man’s little boy, asked “How is it
Joseph ? Is your father going to B. ? ” “ Well,”
answered the youngster judicially, “Pa is still
praying for light, but— most of the things is
packed! ”
* * * *
Westralian Supreme Court sits in a brick
barn—built in 1830 as a commissariat-store—and
the Judges, lose no opportunity of calling upon
Govt, to build them a greater barn. These prat-
ings, justifiable enough, become ludicrous when re-
peated in season and out. Justice Hensman
lately, during a shower of rain, announced that
the State had provided a court, but had provided
no ceiling, and that he would leave the bench till
the shower was over. Which he did ; but the
shower stopped abruptly while his Honor was
climbing down the steps, and he had to make an
immediate and undignified re-appearance.
* * * *
A champion of popular music challenges
the snortful Marshall Hall to produce something
yqual to any of the simple melodies of any nation
—and the fact remains that the almighty pro-
fessor couldn’t do it. He is merely one of a
multitude of little-“ great ” composers. The great-
little composer of ballads that live is few and far
between. There is no arguing against the charm
of such old-established melodies as, say, “The
Mill Wheel,” nor does the most seriously musical
nation on earth—the German, nation—attempt to
argue against them. It sings them. The home of
Wagnerism is the home of dreamy sentimental
ballads, which are the expression of the average
soul in its tenderest moods. Marshall Hall pro-
bably hasn’t a soul. He has the brains, education
and industry for composing music of the school
wherein he has studied—but soul, No !
* * * *
For the justification of Marshall Hall’s
“ impiousness as the Argus terms it —in such
•poor little lines as
“ Hideous scarecrows that flop in the wind
Are your Jew-gods, to frighten the weak and the blind ”
one has only to turn to- last week’s Melb. Punch.
The late Jim Grotty’s sister wrote a letter to said
Punch, wherein th it lady rabidly suggested that
brother James had righteously cut off his wife
with £lOO a year b cause she was a convert from
Protestantism and would, possibly, slip back into
“ that slough of darkest heresy” after dear James’s
decease. Grotty sister’s letter is more than a
pitiable thing. It is a painful suggestion of the
bitter, treacherous bigotry that pervades every
form of ardent church religion. It is full of terror-
stricken faith in hideous scarecrows that flop to
frighten the blind, and, although The Bulletin
has never beard of a sightless person being afeared
by a scarecrow, it recognises Marshall Hall’s howl
as a not-untimely deliverance-

Holy James Balfour, sporting his most
gloomy air of piety, arose in Yic. Leg. Council,
the other week, to remark that there were “ people
performing the ceremony of marriage who had no
moral qualifications for that sacred duty.’’ Sacred

the.register does the trick every time in a regis-
trar’s office as much as in a vestry.

That weird and untruthful legend about
how the son of a couple who lately died tragically
in Westralia kept the event.dark for a few hours
while he tried to trade off “exclusive informa-
tion” to the local papers, arose out of one
of those simple blunders which the tired
and hurried telegraph-wire is always making.
The son gave his evidence first, and then “another
witness” related liow he had tried to sell the
information and found the local press too slow to
do business. And the dpt-and-dash machine
apparently dropped “another” and thus ran the
two bits of evidence into one.

* * * *
Melb. Hospital committee is unwillingly
interested in the Wallace family law-suit. Mil-
lionaire Monahan provided in his will, that any
beneficiary contesting that document should for-
feit his share of the estate to Melb. Hospital. It
doesn’t seem likely that the Wallace action will
be ruled a contesting of the will in the Monahan
sense of the term, but meanwhile the Hospital
lias to take up the character of a contingent bene-
ficiary, or something, and hire lawyers to “watch ”
the case.
* * • *
Kingston’s success in running Adelaide
Hospital with a practically all-paid staff prompts
Bendigo Hospital committee to take the plunge
and abolish its own honorary staff. The plea is
that hon. staffs do all the bossing—and no self-
respecting hospital committeeman can ever stand
that. But the subscribers demand time to ponder it.

“Bargo”: Speaking from personal ex-
perience, I would abolish the “books’’ of de-
positors in the Sydney Barrack-street Savings
Bank, most of which are kept for safety under mat-
tresses of beds. Also, in the case of many elderly
women, the books are carried to and from the bank
in the bosom of the dress. The unlucky receiving-
clerk is thus exposed when making the entry to
any stray germs which might have been hanging
about bedclothes —even, in the case of the women
aforesaid, to the infection of skin - complaints.
At branch office where a friend of mine has just
been working, the four counter- clerks are down
with influenza ; nobody else in office affected.
Significant!

Recent M.L. obituary “pome” (printed
in all seriousness at the instance of the sorrowing
husband): —
She once was merry and gay,
Though sometimes in trouble and strife ;
It affords me some pleasure to say
She was a good mother and wife.
Her children she never neglected,
Her home she kept tidy and clean ;
Although she was sorely afflicted,
Her toilet was always serene.
Here we have quite a new note in the jauntiness
of the metre and the cheerful retrospectiveness
with which the bereaved hubby accepts the situa-
tion.

The only democratic railway in Australia
is one that runs from Kalgoorlie to Hannan’s
Lake, skirting all the great mines of the Boulder
belt. There is but one class and one fare on the
line.
* * * *
At Melb. City Court, the other day, a
policeman giving evidence against a cyclist for
travelling without a light swore, in answer to the
J.P., that he had “ felt the lamp to see if it was
warm,” and found “it had not been recently
lighted.” The cyclist then deposed that he had
no lamp ; and, on the trap being recalled, he said
“it might be so.” Also, at a suburban court, two
constables swore positively that they had seen a
certain man throw a stone at some Chinese, only
to be contradicted within five minutes by the ad-
mission of a companion of the actual thrower,
whom the Bench promptly fined 10s. or 48 hours,
without commenting on the false testimony of
the policemen ! And if policemen airily—well—
blunder like this over a small matter, what are
the odds against their speaking the truth where it
is necessary to their own interest or safety to
secure a conviction ?
• • * ♦
The only Australian interest attaching to
the centenary of the battle of the Nile is the fact
that the powder-hulk Aladdin, of Hobart, is identi-
cal with the brig La Mutine, captured from the
French in 1797 and u-ed as the despatch-vessel
of Nelson’s fleet. Her commander, Capel,
carried the first news of the victory to Eng-
land. La Mutine was also before Sebastopol.
When being overhauled for repairs in her Tas-
manian whaling days, a round shot was found
between her planking and lining. The shipwright
who made the discovery declares that it had worn
as smooth and bright as the bearing-balls of a
bicycle, with many years’ rolling up and down in
a dry place.
* * • •
When Father Long, of Kanowna, W.A.,
who asserted that the alleged 1380 oz. nugget had
been shown to him, was pressed for infor-
mation, he would only say that one of the
fortunate finders was then in Kanowna and under
the influence of liquor. The crowd thereupon
rushed violently down town, and, pouncing on
every drunk it could find, endeavored to extract
a confession. Some even took their subject home
and slept at the. tent door lest he should mizzle in
the night. Said that Father Long lias promised
to divulge particulars in a week or two if the
secret has not previously leaked out.
Maoriland Govt, has introduced the Short-
hand Reporters Bill, which provides for the
Pitmanising of cases in higher and lower courts,
thus abolishing the existing laborious and idiotic
and criminally-expensive method of longhanding
the proceedings. “ Watch his Honor’s pen, wit-
ness.” But that is another story, to be obtained
at first hand from an eminent member of the
N.S.W. junior bar.

Numbers of N.S.W. country newspapers
publish as supplements pages of stereo-matter
which has done service in the same papers several
times. One suburban paper lately published as a
supplement the same matter three weeks running.
These supplements are, of course, Sydney or Mel-
bourne productions, and the newpaper owners
who buy and issue them seem in most cases to
never read them.

He that Victorian proposal to
stop attachment of wages under £2
a week. In Scotland, about 20
years ago, wages under £1 a week
were protected from attachment,
except where the debt was for the
support of an illegitimate child.
This piece of legislation had one
curious result, not quite antici-
pated by the framers. An Aber-
deenshire farm servant had
always paid his shoemaker’s
account religiously at the half-
vearly term, when he received
his wages. The new legisla-
tion came into force between
terms, and at the 51 artinmas
term following, the shoe-
maker’s debtor failed to
put in an appearance.
Meeting him shortly af-
terwards, the shoemaker
asked him why he had
not called as usual to
pay up, and got the re-
ply—“ Man, do ye no
ken that the Act’s
passed ? ”

The right to the
possession of a medical
prescription threatens to
be soon tested in a Syd-
ney court. The chemist
who compounded the
mixture sticks to the
prescription,' while the
patient who believes the
remedy an infallible cure
for his disorder demands
the return of the paper.
The doctor, being re-
ferred to, held that the
prescription is really his
property and that chem-
ist and patient were mere
temporary holders and
declined to give a copy
of the document without a
special fee. English law is un-
settled on the point and, so far,
Australian law has made no pro-
nouncement.

* * * *
A Wangaratta (Vic.) farmer, 27 years of
age, took a fatal dose of poison lately “ because
his mother had rated him for coming home late.”
Marcus Clarke was right in ascribing a “ weird,
melancholy” influence to the bush. Even when the
bush proper has been cleared away for 20 years
the melancholy still hangs around the lone gum-
tree that has survived. Some day The Bulletin
expects to hear of an aged grazier having hanged
himself on account of his niece having tojd him to
chop the wood himself.
• • • *
The religious federation of Maoriland with
Australia seems to have been found as impossible
as the political. A Bishop, lately arrived from
Rome, declares that the Pope will never ask
Maoriland to become one with Australia. Each
must go its course, independent in every sense of
the other.

V. K.
Apology.
In the issues of The Bulletin, dated July 2 and
July 9 last respectively, the following paragraphs
appeared :
A britfht young Australian at Perth, when his father
killed his mother and suicided, suppressed the news for
several hours while he tried to trade “ awful tragedy ”
copy to the Perth newspapers. His enterprise missed
cash, for they wouldn’t deal. Fancy such a boy being
wasted on a community of sandgropers !
“T.D.”: What a singular contrast between the cases
of the Westralian boy who suicided on discovering his
illegitimacy and the other bright youth who suppressed
for many hours the news of his parents’ tragic death with
a view of trading it off to the newspapers ! Maupassant
would have seized the second story with no less avidity
than the first. One seems the natural outcome of our
European fatherhood, and the religious and hysterical
temperament; the second goes to prove that we are
developing, to an extent, the business traits of the Yanks.
That smart boy has the makings of a Barnum —if he isn't
watched too ciosely at first.
The Bulletin learns that these paragraphs are
not in accordance with the facts. The evidence
at the inquest proved that it was a stranger quite
unrelated to the deceased persons who discovered
the bodies and endeavoured to sell his information
to the Perth newspapers before reporting the
matter to the police. The son of the deceased is,
The Bulletin is assured, not a boy, but a married
man, and he. was in no way concerned in the .pro-
ceedings in question. The Bulletin’s error was
due to being misled by certain telegrams from
Perth appearing in the Sydney press. Under the
circumstances The Bulletin hastens to correct
its error, and to express its sincere regret to the
son of the deceased persons for anything which
might be taken as reflecting upon him.

Health Conversazione! Doesn’t that suggest
apple cheeks, peach blossoms, and pomegranate
lips, and tresses yellow like sponge cake, and com-
fortable pudding figures, and milky brows, and
almond eyes, and honeyed voices, and similar
delicious physical confections ?
Anyone whose imagination ran after these de-
lights must have been stunned to find the chilly
Centenary Hall filled during Dr. Emily Ryder’s
Winter Lectures with worn drab faces rising out
of weary black dresses, and crowned with wearier
and blacker bonnets. Then, too, the peculiar
nature of the lectures brought a peculiar and un-
beautiful expression to these worn maternal
countenances. Dr. Ryder herself sported the only
apple-cheek in the hall. The brisk, grey-haired
American smartened her cap and gown with a
ruffle of mauve chiffon at the neck, and an old-lace
scarf upon her chest, and backed up her platform
with piles of hygienic garments, books, blankets,
and dolls that played babies to illustrate the
hygienic dressing of an infant.
To a crude Australian mind there was too much
business transaction in the entertainments. One
afternoon’s proceedings opened with an appeal for
the little wives in India. Then the plate went
round, and the worn black gowns were promptly
relieved of five pounds. Later on the hygienic
garments were exhibited for sale, with an awful
denunciation from the doctor of any garments
that_ were not these. _ Later on again, a Yankee
medical work was lavishly eulogised. Price quoted
135., and the audience urged to purchase at the end
i lecture. Then there was an intimation
that. the doctor would see anyone who wanted a
special confab, and the fees would be so and so.
After each of these business specs, the doctor
underlined the fact that the money was for “the
little wives in India.”
Much straight and sensible advice co women
only got mixed up with facetious verbal illustra-
tion, and tedious repetition. It seems that some
women find these straight talks overpowering.
‘ ‘ Pix your thoughts outside yourselves. Some-
times I have strange demonstrations at these meet-
ings. Let us wait and see.” We all waited
Presently Dr. Emily nodded hard to a Salvation
lass at the door. A glass of water was instantly
brought forward. “That lady there, third row,
veil up.” The lady with the veil was handed a
fl&ss of water, probably much to her surprise.
’resently another nod from the doctor. Another
glass of water advanced. “Back row lady with
her chin on her hand, ” in the same loud platform
tone. Ten minutes later a third nod and a third
glass of water and a third unveiled injunction
from the platform. We were all afraid to lean our
faces on our hands after that. For it can’t be
exactly stimulating to be pointed out as the poor
weak thing who couldn’t stand plain talk. This
was only one of the fair doctor’s discrepancies.
I am sorry to learn that I have chronicled a
fabulous report—the matrimonial engagement of
Mrs. Wolstenholme and Professor Anderson.
It’s quite, a long time since a society girl got
married. Miss Marcia Cox, fourth pretty daughter
of Dr. James Cox, throws herself into the gap this
week and weds Mr. Livingstone Learmonth, at St.
•Tames’ Church, with a toney following of brides-
maids.
By the way, when it’s a question of a wedding,
is a loan a gift, legitimately? Because a country
bride naively reports the loan of a coach-and-four
among her butter-knives and salt-cellars. Follow-
ing this generous gift-to-be-given-back, comes the
name of the local cabby. What a brilliant way of
showing off what you’ve got. Now if anyone I
know well gets married, I am quite willing to let
her chronicle the loan of my best wax fruit under
a shade, and my twelve remarkable antimacassars
that represent with strict fidelity three mad pea-
cocks staring at nothing.
The amateur Orchestral made their usual com-
mendable music at the Town Hall on Wednesday.
Miss Edith King was iil with measles, Mr. Weir
had influenza, and the vocal part of the programme
fell entirely on the chest of Mr. Burns Walker,
who was in good voice and made much of his
Dinorah selection. The rest of the concert was
orchestral. The front seats revelled in a superior
feeling of culture when Tannhauser equalised the
instruments as only Wagner knows how. And
the back seats had their innings when Sylvia
appeared—Sylvia of the innumerable pianoforte
arrangements. Lord Hampden, the Misses Brand,
and an aide, sat under the nose of the conductor’s
pulpit, and realised that there are better things in
life than sitting in the front row when the big
drum and the loud bassoon have the telling of the
story.
Another excellent musical evening was the
Pleyel co.’s concert on Saturday night. Jessie
Middleton, gracefully gowned in frail yellow,
played the heroine and Lesclietizky together. A
large grey musical rat ran down the wall and
stayed to listen not ten feet from the piano.
Whereupon the gallery nearly fell over the verge
of hysterics, in spite of the hall between, but the
plucky pianiste played calmly to the end, when
bravery was rewarded with an encore and flowers.
A highly polished performance was the opening
Haydn Quartette, by Herren Stael, Vollinar,
Carter.and Alfred Hill. The exquisitely balanced
rendering of the smart fourth movement was the
gem of the evening, and had to glitter twice over.
Miss Violet Birkenhead, in white brocade and an
aigrette, appealed nervously to the house to “Hear
Ye Israel,” a suitable invocation for the usual
Saturday night audience. Arthur Deane put less
of himself into his songs than might be expected,
considering how much more there is of him. But
this is a bad time of the year for the throaty pro-
fession. . Every second larynx is relaxed, or has
fallen into the grip of the influenza demon.
All the same, concerts are the leading game just
now. A benefit for poor Lottie Hyam, who
has long been disabled by a broken ankle,
comes off at the Town Hall on Saturday, August
20th. The following Saturday, August 27th, the
same hall sees Florence Schmidt’s benefit, for
which vice-regal patronage and presence have been
promised. Inez Bensusan, now recovered from
her illness, comes before the public with an after-
noon tea recital at the Y.M.C.A. Hall. Madame
Kellerman continues her pianoforte recitals, and
Madame Alva has already started a third series of
concerts.

Spring fashions are here. How is it that nc
other season can rouse such a fine sartorial thrill ?
Autumn and winter are taken as necessities, but
frock and hat is to go on a genuine
spree. All Sydney has been living in the shops
lately, society gambols considerably ceasing till
Nicest and the Newest were definitely acquainted.
Mats take a queer turn this summer. In front,
e brim turns up smartly into the crown, or is
cut off altogether. Trimming has gone up in
front and down behind. One pretty blue hat in
wing-street had a turquoise-blue scarf hanging
down three inches at the back.
Then I saw a marvellous arrangement of red
j j S . fjy 1 ? 1 ?, round a rosier hat, and the largest and
reddist bird had flown right off the brim, and was
b a< ?k of the hat by two claws.
VV hen the rest of this effort was on the owner’s
head, the fat bird would claw her somewhere be-
w , 0 s boulder-bones. I shouldn’t wonder if
that lady died a martyr’s death. It was just the
sort of gaudy bird to lure a sportsman to a crooked
aim.
Hordern’s in Pitt-street are showing a particu-
iarly fetching kind of blouse, just the thing for a
mild bpring day. On a simple lining of mauve or
green washing zephyr, a blouse of tucked book
muslin is arranged, with smart goffering of lace-
edged muslin at the neck and sleeves. Periwinkle
blue is to reign over colors, with the old-fashioned
hue of a damask rose as a maid of honor. White
hats are to go in Vo the silences for a while, but
white muslins, embroidered muslins, and mous-
selms de soie, embroidered with true lovers’
knots, are to sing loud songs this spring and
summer.
“Jane” follows “Sweet Nancy” at Her
Majesty’s. The change of maids is the usual huge
success of the first week’s service. Pattie Browne
is her charming self again in red-brown hair and
pmk print gown, and is more charming still after
she has shopped at Jay’s and Robinson’s. Her
glittering white satin evening dress, embroidered
with silver leayes and traceries, shows the wan-
tonness of wasting Jane’s taste on a milk-shop. A
pretty little blue-and-white shoulder - cape har-
monises with this creation, and two long lace ends
hang gracefully from the neck in a manner which
all good capes should imitate. Pretty May
Browne, who is little sister to Pattie, plays the
ingenue in seemly white, muslin and sash crowned
with a black hat and pink roses. The sprig'htly
Athena Claudius is put into boy’s clothes for the
third time. Soon she will cause a sensation if she
appears m skirts. Pity to see such delightful
work as “Jane ” spending itself to poor houses.
Bernard Espinasse is the local playwright who
answers for “Her Good Name,” the curtain-raiser
which precedes “Jane.” The audience ought to
answer for his, after seeing this unusual little play,
built up on a situation that grips, and showing no
empty places in the action. As the Professor,
Prank M‘Vicars reveals his own clean-shaven
countenance, and appears as the handsome actor
Sydney has been wearying for ever since the
superior Marky took his departure. The charm of
voice that carried him through the pasty part of
Nancy’s accepted elderly lover (I suppose you
know that a refusal is the only thing that makes
an elderly stage-lover acceptable), comes out
strongly m the Professor’s lengthy and cultured
observations. Beatrice Day is appropriately un-
happy in a black moire velours skirt, with a pink-
and-black bodice that lacks distinction, and those
big-tongued shoes that are going to trample all
other evening-shoes under foot next season.
I read that Mrs. Aria, queen of fashion writers,
managed a stall at the famous Press Bazaar that
stirred London up to such life last month. Her
counter was placarded “The World of Dress,”
and she and Miss Irene Vanbrugh helped off Paris
hats and chiffons at rattling prices. Our own
Lady Jersey, presiding over the Strand stall and
hundreds of fluffy Persian kittens, seems to have
mixed places with Lady Carrington at the Daily
Chronicle stall, among such strong-minded ladies
as John Oliver Hobbes and Mrs. Massingham.
Hilda Spong was selling nick-nacks at the stage
stall alongside of Ellen Terry, Nellie Barren and
Juha Neilson. The bazaar was held at the Hotel
Cecil, and the crush was so enormous that half the
stall-holders couldn’t get in till the second day.
On my way to Gladstone’s funeral at the
cmemetograph the other day, I looked in at
Searl’s window. There I saw such a wonderful
mourning decoration that the funeral came quite
easy afterwards. Some ruthless hand had built up
a gate of white camelias and placed it on a' cushion
of purple violets. Over the gate a big white fowl
was sitting with wings outspread. The weird
enigma was. explained by two words written
underneath in snowdrops on a pad of violets,
Gates Ajar I Cruelty to dumb vegetables will
soon need a committee of its own.
Nor was the funeral the decorous proceeding it
ought to have been. I regret to say that the
mourners jigged past as if they were at a common
wake, I fancy that a serpentine dance is a more
suitable spectacle for the whizzing cinematograph
than a funeral procession. Still, the picture is
worth seeing. Excellent imitations of Tummy,
Lord Salisbury, and many fine young aristocracies
go by ; and somebody may be glad to hear that
the phiz of the gentleman who heads the London
police is depicted beyond mistaking. It’s just as
well to know whom to avoid.

annual opportunity came round, so you can guess

“Pa Madden swallowed his Beethoven-Mozart-
Marshall-Hall pill cheek-by-jowl with the Acting-
Governoress in her amber-satin cape and their
prettiest daughter, Ruby, whose white shoulders
gleamed above a black corsage, cut extra low at
the back. Of course the municipal mob which
Melb. Liedertafel council delights to honor were
conspicuous on the stuffed furniture. Half-way
through a complimentary baton was tendered to
the cheery, chirpy conductor Siede, and what
must the donors do but confide the presentation
to Jabberwock Marshall Hall ! On he stalked
with a . MS. in his hand to read a lecturette on
things in general, while poor little Gus Siede,
shifting uneasily from one foot to t’other, seemed
to ask wistfully, ‘ Where do I come in ? ’
“The explosion conqdetely staggered the fash-
ionable audience. The Professor’s choice assort-
ment of squibs and crackers made a great
splutter and no end of a row. The Sermon on the
Mount, the New Woman, Bismarck, parsons,
blood and iron, popular music and church-going
were his themes. The Professor is a student of
Nietzsche, the German philosopher, and all his
notions bear the impress of the influence of that
wind-genius. And the recipient of the baton
was cold-shouldered out of the effusion with a
couple of by - the - bye lines at the tag - end !
Waxellency Madden tried to take the sting
out of Marshall Hall’s epigrams in a subse-
quent speech, but his florid gas-and-gaiters
seemed flat and flabby after the Professor’s barbed
periods. Melbourne’s verdict on the whole is that
Hall is. “balmy.” When people can’t answer a
man it is comforting to say that. Marshall Hall’s
hearers especially pricked up their ears at his
side-slaps at skirt Government. Hid its Professor
run up against some nasty snag in petticoats, then ?
Did ums ?

“On Wednesday (August 3rd) sassiety, bound
for the Mayoress’s reception, dropped in to see
Longstaff’s pictures. Perhaps the artist is wise in
not charging for admission, for sassiety likes to get
something for nothing, and there is a lot of enter-
tainment in this show. Everyone rapturises over
that picture of the girl whose pa struck it rich in
the Golden West. _ Sweet seventeen, with big
blue eyes, golden hair hanging over the shoulders
of her riding-habit, and expression both dreamv
and determined, is a subject worthy of Longstaff’s
portrait power. Then there’s beautiful Mrs. Long-
staff, the tiny curls and tendrils of whose hair look
soft enough to stroke, and wearing a furred cape
gracefully over her simple muslin frock. A paler
frailer presentment of the same sitter is less popu-
lar- In this case, where there’s more of an all-
round view, she L spectators (envious, perhaps.)
accuse the pictured fair of being one of your
‘slim-jims,’ ‘too ethereal,’ ‘not enough of an arm-
ful. Doctor Chas. Ryan’s son, Rupert, a smart,
prize-scooping youngster, makes a winsome por-
trait, a present to his father from a friend. Last,
but not least, the artist’s bush-fire picture.
Really it s dramatic, and, when first you see it,
you are amazed at its realistic glow. Then, as you
notice the faces of close-gazers lit up as if by a
doctor’s lamp, you ‘ drop ’ to the presence of a
w ® l i'P laced bght. There are malcontents vvho
sniff and say ‘ tricky,’ but when I saw it Marshall
Hall was trumpeting to the spectators that its
flames are even more. convincing minus the arti-
ficial light, which is said to be absolutely necessary
m the picture’s present gloomy location. The ‘Bush
I ire is not the conventional blaze that has been
often painted and never realised, but an Effect of
approaching fire —an illuminated night scare ; a
line of fiery redness showing in the black forest
background and the foreground aglow with the
promise of a warm time coining. Some figures in
the foreground are well placed, and the reflected
firelight on the big gum - tree trunks gives the
spectator a feeling of ghost-story trickling down
one s chest.
“At the private view, Preinieress Turner affected
cheerful pink millinery, while her daughter’s grey
frocks were appliqued with black lace foliage.
Madame Dejardm’s purple velvet toque topped
braided coat and skirt of same Imperial shade :
her crisp Frenchy daughter brightened her green
suit by a scarlet parasol. The Mayor and Mayoress
braced themselves up by a course of the fine arts
before entering on reception labors next door, and
Lonsul and Mrs. Pinschof (Madame Wiedermann)
swelled the Conservatorium contingent. Scherek
conscientiously did his duty by the collection of
engravings, etchings and curios, and a few of the
exhibitor’s brother artists strayed through the
throng. One of that rather melancholy brother-
hood sported all unknowingly a long wavy hair
(presumably human) on the shoulder of his untidy
jacket. Perhaps there’s no necessity to alarm his
womankind? Sitters will be sitters, and what’s
the use of kicking against the inevitable ?
“Miss Mary Clarke, who drove up with Uncle
Snodgrass, was more spring-like than most of the

“ The Acting-Vice-regal clan is rather songful,
and the other afternoon, when the administratrix
of the Govt, was at home to the Austral Salon, the
daughters of the house twittered tunefully and
often. An attractive quartette, those sisters, es-
pecially on the domestic hearth, where freshness
scores no less than feature. Their parents, too
welcomed their guests with affable resignation, and
Salonites assure me that on this occasion their
vitals were not preyed on by the hurried suspicion
which marred the Brassey festival—viz., that their
deepy host would bite as soon as look at them
Ihe Ancient Mariner was all right, ancient his-
torians maintain, while reciting; but, later on, an
enterprising Salonite trying to institute some
prattle was glared and grunted at for her pains.
Miss Daisie Madden wore navy-blue Russian
blouse and skirt with cream chemisette, at the
reception. Sisters Sylvia and Ruby, blue-bloused
and cream-vested, affected green-velvet revers •
while little Miss Leslie, in a fawn frock, clad the
smartest feet in the family in Parisian tan. Son
and heir Guy Madden, a frock-coated cherub of
benignant aspect, palpably wilted under the unani-
mous gaze of the Salon, so I’m told, unlike a
serene six-year-old sister in cream cashmere and
curls.
“ ‘ Me and my young man,’which is to say Miss
Sylvia Madden and lmbby-elect Vallange, do some
Block parading in these latter days—especially in
the afternoons when the girls are flitting about.
Human nature is very much the same through all
the grades. ‘ Wodyer think o’ my boy wot’s goin’
to be me ’usban’ ? ’ asks Sarah Jane of her female
pals in Collingwood. And the Acting-Governor’s
