“Oh, the weary waiting in the trenches before
that reception of the Mayoress (Aug. 3). Once in
it was all right: breathing space, lively flowers
and ferns, afternoon tea in adjoining coffee palace.
But that only made matters worse, for the women
outside, who never had dreamed of ‘ paying their
respects, ’ heard of these delights, got excited, and
joined the invaders on the spur of the moment.
1 he Mayoress was pale as a ghost and her glove worn
to shreds _ ‘ ere half her heavy task was done.’
After all, isn’t it time official pump-handling was
abolished ? Is there anything so desperately
desirable in a municipal hand-squeeze that for its
sweet sa,ke the recipient is willing to martyr an
inoffensive matron and condemn her to an arm-
sling for a week ?
“Hawthorn Benevolent Society took the Town
Hall on Thursday night, to read the annual report
and hand round the hat some more for the charity.
Many prominent names were billed, but the meet-
ing resolved itself into a Society for Being Grate-
ful to Madden. Nobody could get away from the
Great Fact that his acting-Ex. and family hap-
pened to be there. A remark referring to tne
Great Fact was sandwiched in everywhere. Levy’s
Lyric orchestrated the arrival with ‘God Save,’
and the back-rows craned their necks to see
the deputy - vice-regal top-knots all the time.
The orchestra, harper Barker, Mrs. Bing-
ham in a pink frock and gloveless—a display of
bare fingers ring-encircled is the. ‘ thing ’ now—
and Walter Kirby made up a good, though thin,
programme. Col. Bingham and Hilda Fraser
rattled cleverly through a playlet. The collection
totalled £25, and the audience looked as if they
thought so much Madden cheap at the price.
“Salvation Army Booth and co. hired Exhibi-
tion building last Monday evening, and gave a
show absolutely unique in its frankness. Realism
m religion—when it means showing real-life models
and cine, views of slummery, squalor and sordid-
ness—can give points and a beating as an absorb-
ingly interesting show to all the drawing-room
drammers of the woes of high-toned impossibles
that ever bored an audience. The shilling-a-head
public that crowded the gloomsome hall and
pervaded it with a smell of mud and orange-
peel and _ damp, musty garments gazed
with the interest born of close sympathy
at the dead-beats in their progress through
the various ‘Homes.’ There was a particularly
‘ real ’ series of living pictures, wherein gentlemen
with battered boxers and frowsy coats were
offered WQod-chopping jobs and subsequent meals
and beds, and the scene where a stalwart officer
‘ chucked out ’ two larrikins, struggling to get
into the same bed in their boots, ‘on the never,’
made the _ audience roar. Also, the maternity-
home act, in which a batch of infants were hustled
into little pink nightgowns and deposited in cots,
where they lay howling, only wanted a spanking
or two introduced to make it come home to every
mother’s heart.
“But the Melb. City Court operetta was great.
Fancy having a real Traviata, a lamp-post Flossie,
in the dock with a loud tartan blouse and a straw
hat with sodden cock’s feather, standing sullenly
while the Irish bobby gives his evidence in recita-
tive. Then accused sings her defence while the
court takes out cambrics and sobs audibly; Panton,
P.M., discharges her in song ; and the Hanny
sisters carry her off in triumph ! There is the
germ here of a whole ‘ new ’ opera-school that
would knock the fancy Italian and German warble-
dramas into the limbo of all discarded childish toys.
“It is humiliating to learn that Australia
stands very low in the list of colonial alliances
with the British aristocracy. Altogether eight
peeresses are from the British dependencies, but
of these not one is an Australian, Lady Bertie—
who will one day be Countess of Lindsay—and
Lady Charles Scott being the only connecting
links this beastly country has with the Hinglish
House of Hooleygans—l mean Lords. St. James’s
Budget points out these facts in a recent article,
and adds : ‘ It’s chiefly in the lower ranks of the
nobility that colonial and American wives are to
be found.’
‘ ‘ Basques are fashionable, and yet they thicken
the waist regrettably. Knowing ones have their
bodices made without, but simulate the effect
without its disadvantages by strips of ribbon or
braid from the skirt-band downwards. Miss
Elliot-Page black-ribbons that successful grey
Zenda frock of hers like this, outlining the strips
with gold, and braid fringes of this build are pre-
scribed for serge suits where extra thickness is
nothing to sneeze at.
Yours affectionately,
GOULI-GOULI.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
The wife of a certain Australian Governor
is beginning to exercise a sensational discri-
mination in the choice of her lady-guests.
Edmund Barton and R. E. O’Connor were
both offered the seat on N.S.W. Supreme Court
Bench made vacant by the death of Justice Innes.
The will of David Henry, the Melb.
“financier,” has been proved at .£33,000, besides
other property in England, Germany and else-
where. Everything is ieft to the widow.
The first woman mining-director is Mrs.
Jelbart, recently elected to the board of Croydon
(Q.) Nos. 5 and 6 Golden Gate. Another outpost

friends have noticed the accounts of his lonely
death and burial in N.S.W., and have sent the

Stafford Bird, an ex-Congregational parson. The
other day a newspaper comp., with all the humor

Bird.”
The widow of Charles Harpur still possesses
some of the poet’s unprinted verses. Lately she
presented a number of interesting fragments to
a country newspaper editor, now a N.S. Wales
M.L.A.
Two descendants of Bounty mutineers
made homes in Tasmania —Fletcher Christian and
Jack Quintal. Both entered the territorial police,
and both, singularly enough—though excellent
swimmers—were drowned.
J. P. Abbott holds the second longest
record as Speaker of N.S.W. Assembly; Arnold
acted from 65 to ’75, Allen from ’75 to ’B2, and
Abbott from ’9O. These three A’s ruled more than
half of the whole time of Responsible Govt.
Queen Victoria has never appeared in
public with a political identity since she was
nearly hit in the eye with a leg of mutton aimed
at a Scottish politician sitting at her side in her
carriage on the occasion of a royal visit to Edin-
burgh.

The indignation of Macleanites over the
arrest of D. T. Burt, late proprietor of Clarence
(N.S.W.) Advocate, for failing to pay costs in-
curred in a libel-action is only a spark of the
smouldering fire of condemnation which will yet
destroy the local system of thumbserewing for
“costs.” Why should not costs be treated as an
ordinary debt ? Why should a lawyer have any
more claim than a grocer on the body of his cus-
tomer ?

Dear Bulletin. —Your paragraphic refer-
ence (19/3/98) to David Syrne not thinking Jardine
Smith good enough for Melb. Herald recalls a
curious coincidence. Was visiting Dr. Morrison,
Lond. Times man, the other day, at his little
Chinese house off Legation-street, Pekin. Con-
versation turned on his early press efforts. “ Once
I called on Cunningham (present assistant-editor
Melb. Argus) to ask for a billet on the Argus,”
he said. “Very sorry, old man,” was Cunning-
ham’s reply, “but you’re not up to Argus stand-
ard, you know.” “And,” went‘on the Dr.,
musingly, “ I wasn’t, either.” I didn’t press for
an explanation.—Curia Burke.

“ Vic.” : Re your par. on colonial military
titles. A dashing Australian (say) colonel, with
his blushing honors printed on his cards, and be-
plastering his travelling-trunks, &c., went ’Ome
for the jubilee. On the way, an Imperial
general joined the boat, and the Australian
was introduced to him as colonel and,
shortly after, the top-notcher invited him
to take a drink. Our own colonel just
then spotted a passenger he wished to
speak to, and, turning with a hand-
flutter, said, “ One moment, old boy !”
This flattened out the general worse than
a dynamite gun, and he fairly gasped to
a friend, “ One moment, old boy! Old
BOV ! ! —is he mad ? ” After consulting
the Army-list, the colonel was told he
was sailing under false colors—he “was
only a damned volunteer, doncherknow,
and that, while he was on that boat, he
would please be plain Mister ! ”
Bishop Marsden wrote the other
day from Gloucester, England, to S.M.
Herald to set at rest doubts which ex-
isted regarding the ordination of his
grandfather, the sheep-breeding, cattle-
trading, convict-flaying chaplain of early
N.S. Wales. The bishop (who, by the
way, was himself born in Sydney) proves
through the Archbishop of Canterbury
that his grandfather’s ordination was
regular; but room was left for doubt
owing to the impulsive manners of the
then young Sam. Twice he returned to
the black-smithing of his boyhood, and
he left the University without degree
or appointment. His’letters to England
from Botany Bay almost all dealt with
sheep and cattle, the introduction of prime
rams being apparently the main thing he con-
sidered wanting to make the settlement happy.
Besides, the appointment of Colonial Chaplain,
which he held, was at that time chiefly a political
office, and the lav magisterial position he filled
with such terrors to all the unfortunates who came
within the sphere of his authority, also added to
the suspicion that he took his kingdom to be
here.

A Bulletin correspondent: “My butcher
tells me how an ex -member of N.S.W. Assembly
used to get his meat on the cheap. Always before
leaving the House he would fill his capacious
handbag with Government Gazettes and other
official documents, and literally paid his butcher’s
bill in ’paper throughout his parliamentary career.
The same politician’s meanness —he slunk out of
pub. back-doors to dodge buying drinks for his
hard-worked committeemen—lost him his seat.
An enthusiastic dig-
ger once presented Barry
Sullivan with two fine
nuggets, which the trage-
dian afterwards kept in a
leather case. It was his
intention, on returning to
England, to have them
worked up into a bust of
Shakespeare. One night
after “ Richard,” at Melb.
Royal, he supped with a
prominent jeweller, and
between the broiled bones
and coffee he produced
his beloved nuggets so
that he might gloat on
them as he drank. The
jsweller took them in his
palm, sniffed suspiciously,
and dropped ’em rudely
on a plate of oyster-shells.
“ They’re brass,” he said
coldly; “mere gun-metal
castings.” Barry’s hand
went to where his sword
should have been. His
f ice was terrible. “ Brass
nuggets? By Heaven,
you have mistaken your
occupation, sir. There
are no such things in the
earth as brass nuggets ! ”
The jeweller was calm. He
rang the bell and sent a
messenger for acid. Re-
sult—nuggets burnt be -
yond recognition. An
old-time attache of the
theatre years ago owned to
the writer that he had
taken the original nuggets
to a moulder, who dupli-
cated them in brass, while
a host of beery stage-
caitiffs pledged themselves
to secresy. In Barry’s
dressing-room was a blue-
hilted dagger that be -
longed to Othello. Sulli-
van, with murder dozing in his eyes, would snatch
it up and grip an imaginary digger by the throat;
then, with a mocking laugh, a cunning up-and-
down stroke, he would give the dastard his
quietus.
A brainy young son of Israel, who was
nominated for a Sydney constituency but was
persuaded to retire to prevent the party - vote
being split, distributed a stirring address to the
electors per half-penny post. The circular being
overweight those of the addresses who thought-
lessly paid the fine imposed by the P.O. and took
the letters in became hopelessly alienated from the
candidate. Another incident in that young man’s
candidature was that some darned fool of a friend
of his got placed, just under the three glittering
balls of a William-street pawnshop, a big calico
sign bearing the legend “ Vote for Levy ” —Levy,
the candidate, being utterly unconnected with the
three-ball business—being, in fact, a very dis-
tinguished graduate of Sydney University and a
barrister by profession. Whereupon a wag: “ Vote
for Levy—and Distress.” A purely legal joke.

BILL: “Hullo, May; shake 'ands!”
This quaint hunk of criticism on Victor
Daley’s book is going the rounds of the Q. press:
“ Daley intended to call his book “ Wine and Roses,”
but a prosaic Sydney publisher insisted on changing the
title to “ At Dark and Dawn!” leaving the imagination
to conjure up the scenes enacted in this wicked world
between sunset and sunrise.”
Truly the literal faculty of the Respectable press
is a fearsome and wondrous thing !
The deaths of Gladstone and Bismarck
have done away with two great anxieties of long-
standing in daily newspaper offices all the world
over. The editorial mind, and that of the head-
printer, have always been worried lest those two
big living figures of history should die at in-
opportune moments. Queen Victoria and Albert
Edward —and possibly Salisbury and Pope Leo —
are now about the only very “ big ” people whose
obituaries will be kept standing in case of emer-
gency.
A curious illustration has come
to light of the shams of the world.
Louis Napoleon would have been
blocked in his progress to the French
throne but for obtaining an advance of
,£BOOO from one Rappalo, who raised
the money on forged Exchequer bills.
His instrument was a high offi-
cial of the Exchequer, who forged
no less than .£400,000 worth, but
declared that he only got about £2OOO
out of it, such being the financial net
in which Rappalo, who suggested rhe
crime, had entangled him from early
years. Rappalo escaped scot free. The
official, transported for life to Tasmania,
died there at an advanced age.
When Richmond Thatcher edited
the Western Independent, he hit
around pretty liberally. And, among
others, he smote Alderman Mutton.
Used to make cheap jokes about his
“ sheepish ” looks, and such like.
Therefore, everybody in Bathurst was
surprised when Mutton planked down
a guinea to the subscription which
Thatcher’s admirers had got up to buy
the editor a buggy. They said it was
Christian-like and magnanimous on
Mutton’s part. But Mutton was a man
of truth. “It isn’t that I love the
,” he remarked; “but he can’t
drive. Knows as much about a hor; e
s I do of Heaven, and he’s pretty well
bound to break his blessed neck out o’
that buggy. See?” But Dick knew
too much for that. Got the buggy and
sold it right a way, and went to Sydney
and bought a toat —a sort of thing lie
could drive.
Ulverston, the Norwegian, who has tra-
velled over Australia in search of soil and sur-
roundings suitable for his countrymen, has given
his preference to the neighborhood of Inverell
(N.S.W.). An incursion of hardy, blonde agricul-
turists called Jorgensen, Bjorgensen and similar
names may, therefore, be soon expected.
The weightiest Cabinet (for its size) that
ever held office in S.A., and probably in Australia,
was that of John Colton (June ’B4 to June ’B5),
which consisted of Colton, Kingston, Rounsevell,
Coles, Playford, and Baker. Each Minister was
over 6ft. high, and broad in proportion, the aggre-
gate weight of the half-dozen being little less than
lOOst.
A hostile, foolish notice of Daley’s poems
in July Review of Reviews has the Turner-
Sutherland bias which has done so much to spoil
Victorian verse. Whether Turner or Sutherland'
wrote it doesn’t appear; but Turner and Suther-
land seem to frequently choose for quotation the
least meritorious items in an author’s work—as
the reviewer in this case.
Marshal] Lyle has just secured from the
Government a fine collection of photos, of Vic.
criminals for transmission to Prof. Lombroso. As
in most collections of the sort in all parts of the
world, the presence of tattoo marks (usually
women, ballet-dancers and the like) is noticeable.
Among the photos, are those of Capt. Moonlight,
Weechurch, and Deeming.
An old Western pioneer—he crossed the
Blue Mountains in 1839—Mr. George M‘Kay,
died at Orange (N.S.W.) the other day in his 80th
year. The murder of his son, manager of a bank
at Barraba, for which crime Cummins and Lee
were executed in ’94 at Tamworth, undermined
the old gentleman’s health. He represented
Orange in one Parliament.
Since the Australasian Premiers have been
in English upper circles they are affecting some-
thing like the childlike innocence of judges. Sed-
don told Maoriland Parliament, the other day,
that he didn’t know the meaning of “ blow the
gaff.” Of course “ Digger Dick ” knows ; but the
Right Hon. Richard John Seddon, P.C., LL.D.,
&c., is quite another person.
A. H. Jacob, chairman of the N.S.W.
Leg. Council, who turned into his 70th year the
other day, is son-in-law of Colonel Snodgrass, who,
after Bourke’s departure, was Acting-Governor of
N.S. Wales. Snodgrass was also Governor of
Tasmania for a time. He was a noted hero of the
Peninsular War, and came to Australia with
wounds and a consoling pension.
The wife of S.A. ex-Premier John Colton,
who died a few days ago, had a record of over 50
years’ most active philanthropic work in the pro-
vince. Though, like her husband, much given to
Methodism, there is practically no charitable
society for which the lady had not done hard work.
Her husband (generally suspected of having some
sort of say in the local ’Tiser) still lives, in semi-
retirement.

Cherubic Dr. Bevan has decided not to let
the offer of a plumper salary in Wales lure him
from the tending of his Melbourne flock. Brecon,
in Wales, would seem a pretty dull spot after Mel-
bourne anyhow. Neither is the head-mastership
of a Theological College half as cheerful a position
as the pulpit of a church which has no irksome
parish hanging to it. Perhaps Heaven hath
pointed out these things to the hairy dootor.

Bendigo’s pioneer Chinaman, Jimmy Ah
Poo, has passed out. Forty years ago h * was ap-
pointed Govt, interpreter, and he held the billet
till death. But the merry little fellow will be
most remembered as the organiser of those Chow
pageants which have put many hundreds into
Bendigo aud other Vic. charities.
John Sharpe, whose death at 85 was
chronicled the other day, was not, as here and
there stated, the first to prove the commercial
success of sugar-growing in E. Australia. N.S.W.
Govt, had shown this in the early days, and Capt.
Hope was successfully carrying on the industry
about Ipswich (Q.) some years ere Sharpe went to
the Richmond.

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

“Raspberry” : What hideous sarcasm to
name a bullock “ Smiler ” ! The God-forgotten-
man-bespanked-and-blanked bullock ! Why not
“ Weeper ” ’—for he has much cause to weep. Ye
dainty ladies and bunchy-stomached bald-heads
of the Societies, who befriend the collar-spotted
city moke and the mangy street cur, go ye into
the bush and look up the bullock-owners and
drivers ; superintend a day’s log-hauling, and
then turn round and kick yourselves for very
shame. No more diabolical instrument of torture
exists than the infamous bullock-yoke and bow.
Anyone, save the savage machines who curse
themselves hoarse for a living, will note the grind-
ing of the bow into the animal’s shoulder and the
sawing of the yoke on the neck of a poler. Bul-
locks are martyrs whose intelligence far exceeds
that of the average driver. To say that the poor
beasts work harder and quicker by being cursed
and hammered is a fallacy and a lie which requires
to be upset and trodden into the ground and
pegged there. The best drivers are the most
silent, and the most docile and best-conditioned
team is one which is least flogged and cursed.
The inventor of a yokeless collar would confer a
blessing on poor, suffering bulloclcity, which cannot
be gauged even by the intelligent members of the
cruelty-prevention societies.
* * * *
“Gippsland”: Saw a queer flock being
driven towards Bairnsdale just before Xmas, ’96.
Two hundred head of turkeys, all in excellent
condition, despite the fact that they had been
driven over 60 miles. The drover was a woman,
and she managed her mob like an artist. Each
bird had one wing clipped, and the lot moved
along in excellent order, but at a gait that would
have ruined the nerves of an active tortoise—of
anything, in fact, but a bush-bred Australian
woman. The proprietress told me that it had
taken eight days to do the 60 miles, and she had
lost eight birds en route. At night the gobblers
perched on stumps and fences, and the petticoated
drover camped under a log. The birds fed along
the way, and were in fine condition, and the
owner anticipated averaging 7s. a head for the lot
when she reached Bairnsdale.
* * • *
Haulem was a travelling dentist, who re-
cently struck the town of Bandicoot and “did it
bad.” Allured by his stirring advts., the Bandi-
cootians gathered from all quarters to have their
teeth drawn and to make ready for a new set.
Many a tough, serviceable old grinder, capable of
Miss FRANCES ROSS (Bland Holt's Co).
Photo., Grouzello Studio.
worrying through a waggon-load of hides, was
wantonly sacrificed in the hope of better things to
come—and Bandicoot waited anxiously, till its
gums had shrunk, and the new set of ivories was
ready. . . . Then Haulem disappeared with the
lucre of some 200 patients, who had paid partly in
advance. And there was weeping, and wailing,
and gnashing of gums—and the population pre-
pared mournfully to live on pap, porridge, and
bread-pudding for the rest of its days.
* * * *
A German girl of 17 sums up her ex-
perienee of life in the mallee (Vic.) : “When I
worked for a farmer, besides doing the ordinary
slavey’s duties, I didn’t mind feeding the pigs,
grubbing, ploughing and harrowing, but when
they put me cutting mallee shoots I resolved on a
change.”
“ S.S.” : My greatest scare
happened when I was about 18.
Self and father were working a small
coal-seam in a densely-wooded range
about 10 miles from Morwell (Vic.).
The old man had gone into the town-
ship for supplies, and I was sleeping
alone in the tent peacefully enough
when awakened by a strange voice,
saying distinctly : “ Jim’s dead ! ”
I knew of nobody within five miles
of our camp, and had gone to sleep
under the impression that I was
alone on those hills, consequently
that voice brought up straight one
young mail who was feeling very un-
well. I hung on to my emotions,
and listened, hoping to Heaven it
was only a dream ; but the voice
came again clearly and distinctly
enough, “Jim’s dead ! Jim’s dead ! ’’
And now I saw in the dim light of
a low moon the outline of a man’s
head thrust in at the tent-flap. The
intruder repeated five or six times his
cheerful information respecting Jim,
and then withdrew ; and although I
lay awake till daylight he did not
return, and I was almost convinced
that I had seen a real bush spook.
But Jim’s friend came along at mid-
day next day and informed us in
strict confidence that Jim was dead,
and I then heard that he was a harm-
less old Scotchman living in a hut in
the valley. He was subject to peri-
odic fits of mooning, when he strayed
all over the hills scattering the in-
telligence about Jim. Who Jim
was, and how or when or why he
died, nobody knew and nobody
cared.

Dear Bulletin. — Be alleged
bunyip in Eurombah lagoon (Bulle-
tin (25/6/’9B). I have three times
during my 30 years’ experience of the
tjackbloeks seen what I believe to be
the Australian aboriginals’bunyip. In 1870, on
the shore of Lake Corangamite (Vic.), I was lying
in wait for a shot at water-rats, when, hearing a
snorting or wheezing sound, I cautiously looked
over my ambush-rock, and saw a dark form in the
water not 20 yards away, staring at me with a pair
of large, yellow eyes. Can best describe it as
like a big Newfoundland dog. Noticed liair fully
Gin. long floating out on the water from the body.
The creature had a tail about 18in.
long, with long hair on it also. The
total length of the animal I judged
to be about sor 6 feet. For about
half a minute it stayed perfectly mo-
tionless, but as I was cautiously rais-
ing my gun it disappeared under water,
and I watched in vain for its reappear-
ance. Next, in 1880, at a big water-
hole in the Leichhardt River, N.Q.,
I was fishing off the limb of an over-
hanging coolibah, when an entirely
similar creature swam past, distinctly
visible at about two feet under the
surface of the clear water. Again,
in 1885, in the Paravituary waterhole,
in the Georgina River, W.Q., I saw
the same kind of animal, which
speedily disappeared on sighting me.
This waterhole is 15 miles long, salt
at one end and fresh at the other.
A Central Queensland resident told me
the other day he had seen such an
animal as I have described in the
Omo lagoon on Barcoo River. It is
certain, in any case, that the aborigi-
nals will not camp on Omo lagoon.
I think it very likely that the “ bun-
yip ” will prove eventually to be a
hitherto unclassified amphibious ani-
mal indigenous to Australia, the won-
der being that its remains have not yet
been found. —Bauhinia.

“W.”: A blackfellow’s grave
(Western N.S.W.) is usually heaped
over with wood, the destruction of
which is considei’ed a horrible deed.
A man named Kennedy once acciden-
tally burnt a stack whilst boiling his
billy. They called him “ Wokka-
atchie,” and no black would willingly
go within miles of him. Can any-
one give meaning of “ Wokka -
atchie ? ”
It is well-known that the Western rabbit
can climb fences, shin up trees, burrow down half-
way to Sheol, swim creeks, and so forth. He is
also an ingenious and daring acrobat. T’other
day, Jenkins went into his rabbit-trap to kill the
vermin encaged there, when one of the bunnies
ran up his back, sprang over the enclosure, and
escaped.
* * * *
Birdsville, on the Queensland-South Aus-
tralian border, gets four inches of rain per annum.
Geraldton (N.Q.) often gets over lOin. per diem,
Queensland has the honor of combining in one
province one of the droughtiest and one of the
wettest districts on the face of the earth. Also,
the S.E. seaboard is about the pleasantest portion
of Australia to live in, and the Carpentarian coast
the most disagreeable.

“He was a good musician, but he teas always a bar behind."
“Well, old chap, that'll never be said of you. The BAR'S always in front in your ease !”
Seems to be a strong smack of white-
slavery about the N.S. W. stage-coaching industry.
Some single-man shifts; Wilcannia to Cobham,
33 hours ; short rest, and 33 back again. Broken
Hill to Fowler’s Gap, 24 hours ; latter to Tibboo-
burra, hours ! The Postmaster-General says
that “ the exigencies of the public service demand
that he should accept the lowest tender for mails.”
Same old sweating-business !

D. : The “old man” once had a pet sow'
and a male pig (this has nothing to do with the
invitation to the pork Ananias). The female was
killed and the male porker took the sulks, lying
down continuously on one side for six months,
except at feeding time. At the expiration of that
period he vvas slain, and the skin on his “lying-
down side ” was half-an-ineh thick. The old man
converted a slab of his pelt into a bat for one of
my brothers.

“Guam”: Met an old swaggie, and,
noticing he was not smoking, handed him my
plug. He waved it aside- lie was “ orf the pipe.
Ton see, it’s this way,” he explained. “When I
makes a bit I buys a stock of ’bacca, and for about
three months I smokes hard night and day. 1
don’t keep count of the pounds of the black stuff
I get through-but, Lord, it’s something awful !
After a ’bacca bust of three months I chucks it
complete, and it takes seven or eight months
before I can bear to think of it, let alone smoke
it. I’ll be startin’ again in ’bout ’nother month.”
Anybody ever hear of a man going on the ’bacca-
jamboree before? ■
r * * * *
A peculiar trait in the crape-haloed man
from out back, which is worked to some advantage
by the professional magsman. Ask him if he is
from a certain place and he will reply, “No, I’m
from such and such a place.” Thus—Magsman :
“ Are you from Bourke? ” Speewa : “ No, not
me. I’m from Riverina, I am.” Once the locality
of origin is let out the spieler, if at all conversant
with the district, can make the intimacy thicken
to an alarming extent. Nothing warms the heart
of a backblocker in town more than to meet a man
who knew “that big fence back of M’Bride’s.”
And, of course, the spieler does.

musical talent. Pick up bits of popular songs and sing
thern sweetly enough, and their own songs have often a
plaintive beauty. Most tribes have a poet or poetess,
who makes up the songs for their corroboree. Asked an
old gin what was the meaning of one particular song
they often sang, and she said it was about a young girl
lying asleep under a tree and being awakened by the
noise of a flight of swallows over her head. The “lan-
guage of one tribe ” was called “ Commoroy,” as near as
I can get it. They invited me one day to watch them
prepare a feast of “ yutondally and “ comnioma eggs
(goanna and eggs of jew-lizard). They scooped out a
good-sized liole and put in a fire, and, when it had burnt
down, r iked a hole in the centre and put in the goannas,
without skinning or cleaning; but on taking them out
the skin and inside separated easily from the flesh, which
was very white and smelt like eel. They are very in-
diligent to their children, the parents waiting on them
even after they are grown up, especially the daughters.
The gin 1 had helping in the kitchen had a grown-up
daughter—herself a mother—who could nt cook a damper
and would wait for a drink until one of her parents came
back to camp to bring her one from the waterhole. Her
father—a fine-looking, old, white-bearded fellow—had
taken her back from tier husband because he had knocked
her on the head with a yam-stick—probably because of
her inability to build aforesaid damper.

Fivecorner : Saw jackass fly to and
,io between coolibah and ground. Rode over to
see vvl.at Johnny was doing and found 3ft. 6m.
black snake lying stunned or paralysed. Stood
off about 50 yards and waited till John, after
about three minutes’ inspection of the enemy from
limb above, flew down and swallowed snake whole
and head-first. Thought no more of the matter
at the time, but about three months later, when
crossing same flat, half-caste who was leading
pack-horse found a sugar-bag in coolibah tree,
Sent him up to chop it out. Dry spout on limb
broke to pieces, and down with a lot of feathers,
bones, and the skull of a kookaburra fell a black
snake angry at being thus rudely awakened from
his winter sleep. Killed him ; measured 3ft. 7£in.
This happened on the Upper Dawson.

The parson’s horse jibbed in the main and
only street of the little bush township, and the
local humorists all gathered round and kindly
advised the poor sky-pilot of different ways to
start him. Loud was the laughter from the crowd
as the shepherd tried several of the ridiculous
plans suggested, and when one brilliant joker told
him to “try puttin’ dirt in ’is mouth,” all held
their sides and prepared to go off with a loud bang,
The good pastor gathered a handful of dust, care-
fully inserted it in the mouth of the quadruped,
who chewed it thoughtfully, and, to everyone’s
amazement, trotted off at next time of asking,
Also there was a deathly silence when the parson
drove back and thanked the humorist aforesaid
for his kindness. P.S.—This is a tolerably sure
way of starting a jibbing horse. It monopolises
his attention and he almost invariably moves off
if taken quietly. , , *

Libel Law Amendment.
Pink and M‘Cay are introducing an amend-
ment of the L’bel law into Vic. Assembly, as a
private measure. Newspapers will, no doubt,
welcome any improvement in the present sys-
tem, but there is little doubt that, even if
passed, this Bill will leave much to be desired.
Following the usual fashion of “colonial”
statesmen, these two lawyers, who ought to
have known better, have slavishly followed the
English precedent of 1888.
* * » *
In adapting it to the circumstances of Vic-
toria, they have omitted the reference to reports
of meetings of “ boards of guardians,” but do
not appear to have provided for fair reports of
benevolent asylums, hospital, and general “ pri-
vate ” charitable committees. This is a most
serious omission, as Vic. editors may find, if
some of the retiring Melb. Benevolent Asylum
committeemen have to wince more than usual
in the next few weeks. It is, indeed, a matter
for wonder that Launceston’s magnates did not
take advantage of the disgraceful weakness of
the law in this respect, to prevent Ben
Tillett’s recent exposure of their “benevo-
lent ” management getting into the Tasmanian
daily press.
The definition of “ public meeting ” is prac-
tically an open invitation to all and sundry to
exclude reporters when any hanky-panky work
is on the board. The clause defining it will
also be particularly fruitful in litigation.
* • • »
One good clause in the Bill puts the burden
of proving malice upon the shoulders of the
plaintiff, instead of, as before, compelling the
paper to rebut the presumption of malice.
* * * *
In one respect the English Act and this Bill
interfere with a useful right already enjoyed at
common law by newspapers—namely, lhe right
to publish, provided it is not done maliciously,
reports of judicial proceedings, no matter when
they took place. This is materially interfered
with by the insertion of the word “ contempo-
raneously ” in the clause relating to reports of
such proceedings. For instance, an extract
from the proceedings, say, of the Tichborne,
or the Mount and Morris or Mount Rennie
trials, would not be protected under the clause
as it stands.
* • * *
The clause relating to the consolidation of
actions against several defendants for the same
libel is a good one, and applies to all libels,
whether by newspapers or individuals.
* * * *
The clause which enables a defendant to plead
in mitigation of damages, that plaintiff has
recovered damages against other defendants in
IF YOU CAN’T THINK OF ANYTHING, SAY SOMETHING.
A flock of sheep having suffered severely by the ravages of dingoes, called a meeting to discuss
the situation. A great deal having been said to little effect, an old man with an air of great
wisdom observed that it was a simple case of natural selection and the non-survival of the fattest.
The profundity of the remark satisfied the hearers that all had been done that could be done;
they withdrew, and went to dinner —a good few with the dingoes.
respect of the same libel is, in the case of news-
papers, a good one, and will to some extent stop
an odious form of blackmail practised by un-
scrupulous solicitors on papers innocently copy-
ing libels. But whether the right should be
extended to the private libeller is another
question. In the English Act it only applies
to newspapers. This is the only material de-
parture from the English Act in the Vic. Bill.
This clause sets aside what is known as the
rule in Creevy v. Carr.
* * * *
The clause providing that it shall not be
necessarv to state the actual words used in
presentments for obscene libel, sets aside the
rule in R. v. Bradlaugh.
One serious result of the slavish following of
the words of the English Act will be that Vic-
torian newspaper proprietors and editors will
be in a far worse position than at present with
respect to criminal libel.
* * * *
By a practice initiated by Higinbotiiam
when Attorney-General, the Crown Law officers
invariably enter a nolle-prosequi in cases of
criminal libel started by private individuals.
They are left to their remedy by grand jury
indictment; and no more effectual safeguard
against the wanton setting of the criminal law
in motion for purely civil matters could
have been devised. Of course, the prac-
tice is not necessarily law, but it has
been so constantly followed for such a long
period that any departure from it would be
met by grave public disapprobation, and pro-
bably a vote of censure on the Minister allow-
ing it.
® * * * *
Bub this Bill provides that no criminal pro-
secution (which words, by the way, do not in-
clude “ criminal information”)- for libel shall
be commenced without an order from a Judge
in Chambers. According to an English case, in
1892, there is no appeal from the order of this
Judge. The leanings of the average Judge are
historically so notoriously against defendants
in libel-cases that it will be a comparatively
easy matter to get such order ; and if it is got
no Attorney-General would care to enter a
nolle-prosequi.
* * * *
The enactment of this clause means the repeal
of the present wholesome practice, with the
result that Vic. journalism will lose far more
than it will gain by the other provisions of the
Bill.
* * * *
Criminal libel is an anomaly to-day ; it is a
relic from a semi-barbarous past, which, un-
touched, will soon disappear. The existence of
the Victorian Crown Law practice with respect
to it in all cases, save those by criminal infor-
mation for libels on public officers of justice,
represents its period of obsolescence. But the
enactment of this clause will revive criminal
libel to its malignant activity of a century ago,
when England was only saved from a crushing
judicial censorship of the press by the heroic
obstinacy of juries, who refused to be directed
by the judges in libel cases, even in matters of
law.
• * » *
There are many matters of great public im-
portance not referred to in this Bill. For
instance, the question raised in the Austral-
asian libel case, whether a jockey was a public
person whose actions might be freely com-
mented on ; the conduct of individuals at
Government House shivoos ; the proceedings ol
private clubs of great public interest, as foot-
ball associations, cycling leagues, and the like ;
the proceedings of unregistered societies, such
as Orange lodges and churches ; and the pro-
ceedings of publicly-elected bodies, such as
shire councils, even when conducted in camera.
* * » *
Another extraordinary omission in this Bill
is any reference to the repeal of the absurd law
whicii makes truth to be a complete answer to
a civil action, but aggravates the offence, except
in certain oases, in a charge of criminal libel.

Business, Robbery, etc.
Curious that most of the title-bearers who are
asserted to have accepted Hooley’s magnificent pay for
the use of their names are of the genuine historic brand,
and not of the newly-gilded variety whose comparatively
recent emergence from the ranks of commerce might have
been expected to make them grasp the chance of turning
an honest thousand. It would also be interesting to know
in how many other instances the flat denial of having
received a thumping honorarium would have, as in Lord
Albemarle’s case, to be discounted by an admission of
having received a big sum “as the result of the sale of
shares.” Smacks of the racing “ £5O to nothing” racket.
* * » *
Peers, promoters, and boodlers generally are
rising as one man in London to declare on their honor
as highly-bred gentlemen that they did not get big
cheques from Hooley. But ask any man who has
floated any concern in London of late years if he ever
found it necessary to part with boodle to the owners of
big names, and the expansiveness of his smile is a suffi-
cient reply. It is never paid in the form of cheques, nor
are receipts ever given. One methodical, but badly-
trained, vendor brought the boodle money home to Aus-
tralia again because the boodlers refused to give receipts,
and he failed to see how he could acoount for the money
otherwise. Is it likely that the boss boomer of the century
could get off his schemes without giving boodle, or that he
would run the risk of gaol for life by falsely giving a list
of amounts and using the names of men whom he never
greased ? Hooley’s list of the newspapers he fed is incom-
plete. The names of several that loudly barracked for
him are absent. Is it possible that, after getting big
boodle to sing for Hooley, they have had to buy Hooley’s
silence ?
* * * *
That awful person, Lowries, M.P., the Papuan
grabster, says that for years he has been advocating the
investment of British’ money in colonial rather than
foreign securities, and has had the satisfaction of direct-
ing many thousands of pounds into colonial channels.
The Capitalist publishes a cruel list of the colonial com-
panies largely promoted and controlled by Lowles. They
are mostly, if not all, dividendless, dead and dying.
Lowles and Vine paraded Australia as advocates of Im-
perial Federation and the holding of a great mining exhi-
bition in London, whereas they should have been loudly
labelled—‘ ‘ Boomsters —Beware. ”
* * * *
A recent Melb. law-case provides a shove-up for
Isaac Isaacs’ Anti-Usury Bill. Edith Maloney proceeded
to quash an agreement made with Abraham Marks —since
deceased—under which the latter lent £220 to the former,
who assigned him her interests in her father’s will. Marks
exacted in return a £lOO bonus, and 20 per cent, com-
pound interest, and continued to advance sums to plain-
tiff’s husband on the same terms and backed by the same
security. Justice a’Beckett cancelled the bargain, and
allowed the ghost of Abraham only the original £220, and
6 per cent, to date. Good enough, without the new Bill !
cries Israel; but, then, these bargains do not always come
to light, and would be impossible under the Isaacs legis-
lation. Prevention is better than cure in any case—for
cure is almost as expensive as Abraham.
* * * *
“ Ultimo ” : Recently had dealings with a pro-
minent Sydney brick-merchant. Wanted 13,000 of his
baked mud-bats ; signed an order to that effect, and
bricks were duly delivered—9oo to the good. Thought
the extra 900 was a present, a sort of 13-to-the-dozen
affair, till I got the bill, when I found I was charged for
full amount. I arose and waited on the brick boss for an
explanation ; told him I had ordered 13,000 and no more,
adding that if he felt inclined he could come and cart the
900 back (my place is 10 miles away from brick-yard).
He listened quietly, and then producing my signed order,
explained, with a pitying smile, that a few small words,
“ more or less,” inserted after the 13,000, gave him per-
mission to either send me 10 per cent, of the number over
or 10 per cent. less. He advised me to try the matter in
a court of law, but I paid with a green smile and walked
out.

Does the pawnbroker flourish in any mining
town, trade of which depends almost solely on wage-
earners ? There is no pawnshop on the Towers, nor did
the globular trinity assault the air in Kalgoorlie, Kanowna
or Cue, when writer was last there.
* * * *
The Argus, in its case for the pawnbrokers,
states the Melb. Mont de PRt<s lost its capital in the biz.
But that wasn’t through “ broking,” but through finan-
cing mining speculations. Since the concern has confined
itself to the “ uncle ” line it has got on the up-grade again.
Fate is so dead against Ikey Mo getting outside his avuncu-
lar functions that in one case where Ikey junior became
a medical student he was shot by a man burgling the
paternal pop-shop. * * »
The multitude of pawnbrokers in Melb. is ad-
vanced as the excuse for their high rate (lOd. in the £) of
monthly interest on loans. Sou .e of them can scarcely do
enough business to keep themselves warm, it is pleaded.
Hence the urgent necessity for limiting the number of
pawn-shops in each district on a population basis, so that
a number of shivering Lazaruses may be reluctantly per-
suaded to go and work for the benefit of their health.

Much Victorian litigation is likely to grow out
of the transformation by the cyanide discovery of com-
paratively worthless heaps of tailings into valuable pro-
perties. That the law on the subject is still in a state of
muddle was made evident upon a recent application for
an injunction to prevent tlie treatment of 40,000 tons of
tailings on the Foul’s co.’s old ground, and the seven or
eight parties to the case left the court as hopelessly be-
fogged as to their respective rights in the matter as if no
Mines Act were in existence.
* * * *
“Tanna Tommy”: On the Clarence, the
Colonial Sugar Relining Co. recently took a vote of the
sugar-growers as to whether this season’s cane crop would
be paid for by individual analysis or by the average
analysis of the district. The result was largely in favor
of the average, the result being 316 to 54. Explanation :
The farmers whose cane is below general average naturally
voted for that system, as they would get a slightly higher
price, at the expense of the minority who grew superior
cane. The minority naturally argue that if this basis of
payment is to be adhered to in the future it will result in
a deterioration of the crop, as no man will spend his time
and energy in the unbusinesslike occupation of taking
money out of his own pocket and putting it into the other
fellow’s. Several of the best farmers begin to find out
there is more “sugar” in “cow-juice” and pig-raising
than growing cane. The return is more sure, and the
capital invested is not subject to the discount of bad
seasons, premature frosts, and tariff-tinkers, while the
payment is by test, in which a man gets the benefit of his
brains, experience, and outlay.
* * * *
Pseudonymous employment advts., requiring
statement of name, references, and wag;es wanted, give
advertiser an unfair advantage over applicants who know
nothing about advertiser’s personality, or the class and
amount of work required; yet have to estimate their
value of it and, beyond slight cost of insertion, have no
guarantee of bona-f ides of the ad. The addendum “ con-
fidential,” also, has often resulted in underpaid men ap-
plying unwittingly to their own employers—with result of
sack. The pseudonymous system, in short, gives em-
ployer, at slight cost, a correct gauge of poor labor’s
estimate of its own value—under stress of necessity and
competition.
Dear B.—lt is a bad game for a man advertising
for work to give address at a P. 0., as other unemployed
whose capabilities fit the case are apt to drop in early and
collect the replies. The I'.O. rule that requires personal
application for letters addressed to pen-name or initials is
no bar to a man who can lie with tranquillity. E.g. :At
a Sydney branch P. 0., the other morning, as soon as the
office was opened a colored man called and received two
answers to ad. for job as handy man, and an hour or so
afterwards a white man called and gave same initials
and was only prevented from demolishing the office by
receiving full description of the nigger, whom he is doubt-
less now seeking with intentions of assault and battery.
Of course the white may have been the fraud, but his
wrath was very convincing.—J.F.D.
From Queensland, Vic., and elsewhere comes
the grumble that smelting companies are doing more to
kill than to encourage the mining industry. Alleged that
recently some 400 tons of copper-ore was sent from one
mine and divided between two smelting works. Nothing
was mentioned of gold or silver contents. The return
from each was 28 per cent, copper, and no return for gold
or silver. Assays made on account of the mine showed
the ore to contain both gold and silver in varying quanti-
ties, the average being gold a little over 4dwts., and silver
over sUoz. Both, together, would add considerably to the
saleable value of the copper and represent a certain extra
profit for the smelters. Four dvvts. gold in 28 per cent,
copper don’t count by itself, but it would associated wiuh
50oz. silver, as one would be extracted with the other
at same cost.
* * * •
When the London promoter happens to get hold
of a good thing in a mine it is next to impossible to get
him to leave well alone. The Sons of Gwalia is probably
the best Westralian mine outside of Hannan’s camp. The
promoters must needs give a gorgeous banquet in London
recently, at which each guest received a richly-printed
souvenir. It was a decoy to catch subscribers to “The
Star of Gwalia ” to acquire the next mine in a co. with a
capital of £160,000. Unlucky omen—since this gold-
printed souvenir was issued the Sons of Gwalia shares
have fallen 6s. Od.
• * * *
John Bull’s “Victorian Gold Estates C 0.,” near
Maryborough (Vic.), is perhaps the biggest alluvial ven-
ture in the Australias. Capital, £350,000; it holds about
eight miles of frontage along the supposed course of the
main trunk formed by the “ Berry ” and “ Ascot ” leads, to
say nothing of others from “ Deep Creek,” “ Blanket
Flat,” “Mount Glasgow,” &c. Sinking, about 350 ft. ;
they are now sinking two shafts 400 ft., 22ft. by
7ft.' 6in. in the clear,‘down which they expect to drop
£lOO,OOO ; a thousand feet of driving will reach the deep
ground, which will then be about 45ft. overhead. To
shift the water two 26-inch lifts will be dropped in each
shaft capable of lifting 10,000,000 gals, a day. The lan-
guage of some of the local people connected with it is
fairly strong. “We will have seven shafts like these
working here before long, and 3000 men will be em-
ployed.” Peacock (he of the kookaburra smile) is on the
local directorate. A splendid undertaking, whatever it
ends in.

CARTOONLETS.


“ Washington ” Was Killed 1
SOME UNPUBLISHED FACTS ABOUT
HIS LAST ILLNESS AND THE LES-
SON THEY CON TEY TO MEN AND
WOMEN OF THE PRESENT DAY.
It is not generally known that General Washing-
ton was killed in a most cruel and barbarous man-
ner, but it is, nevertheless, a fact.
At the time of his death Washington was in Ins
68th year, a strong, robust man. On December
12, 1799, he contracted a severe cold, and pneu-
monia threatened. Two doctors were summoned,
and, in accordance with the absurd custom of those
days, they proceeded to bleed their patient.
Nearly a quart of blood was taken from his veins,
until at last he begged them to let him die in
peace. He died that same day—not from disease,
but actually from loss of blood. He was killed by
ignorance 1
In these days any doctor who treated a patient
as Washington was treated would be indicted for
manslaughter. The world has moved since then,
and there has been a complete revolution in the
practice of medicine. Instead of. lowering the
vital forces by thinning the blood, advanced
physicians now endeavor to build up the strength
of their patients. They employ only the latest
and most scientific remedies.
And vet, in spite of this fact, we find thousands
of people endeavoring to improve their health by
taking old-fashioned medicines. They forget that
chemists and scientists have made great disco-
veries in recent years, and do not go back to the
antiquated remedies and concoctions that were
used a hundred years ago. It will not do.
What you, reader, need to cure the headaches,
dizziness, tired, worn-out, and irritable feelings,
constipation, and bearing-down sensations is some
modern discovery based on scientific truths that
would strike at the root of the trouble. You are
sick because your kidneys and liver are out of
order, and you should at once see that they are
put in order. “ Yes,” you say, “this is easy to
assert, but what shall Ido ? ” Take the best and
most scientific discovery for these troubles you
can find. Ask any advanced scientist what this
is, and he will tell you, Warner’s Safe Cure. This
discovery stands alone, by itself, and far above
all so-called medicines of the past or nostrums of
the present. In speaking about it, Dr. William
Edward Robson, of London, says :
“ I conscientiously and emphatically state that
I have been able to give more relief and effect
more cures by the use of Warner’s Safe Cure than
by any other medicine attainable to the pro-
fession.”
This is high praise, but no higher than the sub-
ject deserves, as you will readily ascertain upon
using this great, modern discovery. *

Many a man has often wondered why sheep
driven through any narrow passage will all imi-
tate the leader if he gives a leap. This ex-
planation by Dr. Louis Robinson in “Traits of
Wild Animals ” may be of interest. According
to Robinson, when sheep in a wild state dash off
together among broken ground, or are crowding
together along some perilous ledge on a mountain-
side—their usual habitat —it would be quite useless
for those in the middle of the band to endeavor to
see obstacles at their feet. Hence each keeps his
eye on the leader (or on those in front), and imi-
tates his or their movements. If the leader thinks
a 3ft. jump is sufficient, every member of the
flock follows suit. . . . Hereditary transmission
has passed the wild habit on to the tame de-
scendant.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Questions to be asked as soon as N.S.W.
Assembly opens : In what state is the Mercadool
conspiracy case now? How much have the in-
quiries and prosecutions so far cost the country ?
There are five barristers and 13 attorneys
in N.S.W. Assembly, including ex-practitioner
Meagher and conveyancer Nobbs. In the Leg.
Council are seven barristers and seven attorneys.
Mudgee, which now returns to N.S.W.
Assembly a newspaper man, has, in its time, re-
turned two men —Innes and Martin —who attained
the Supreme Court Bench, and three—Parkes,
Martin, and Robertson —who became Premiers.
N.S.W. Government should explain to
Parliament what action has been taken on expert
irrigationist Home’s expensive report, and, if it
be determined to lay it aside as waste paper, on
whose authority and recommendation is this course
to be adopted ?
The Kingston Govt.’s State fire-insurance
scheme has caused a split in S.A. Tory ranks.
Opposition-leader Downer strongly opposes the
measure, but several of his party favor it, and, as
the Labor crowd solidly support, the Bill will
probably go through the Assembly.
“ Jack ” Want was pretty active during
the general election in endeavoring to label the
Barton party as “Conservatives,” and yet Gar-
land, his own nominee for Woollahra seat, was
proudly showing letters from Salisbury, the Eng-
lish Tory Premier, thanking him (G.) for services
rendered to the Conservative cause.
What Federation, guaranteeing immunity
from border duties, means to border districts. In
’92, which was the “ strike year,” and altogether one
of the blackest in its history, Broken Hill paid
£115,000 through the Customs, mostly for S.A.
produce, since nothing can be produced locally
and little can be sent round from eastern N.S.W.
Melville, of Yic. Leg. Council, has hit
upon a very “ advanced ” reason for not encourag-
ing narrow-gauge railway construction. He said,
the other night, it was questionable whether the
48 Rich Landlords should not “pause to see if
steam-power was not superseded.” Whereat
several of his sapient colleagues nodded their
heads gravely, as though they would be in favor
of a royal commission to enquire into the subject
of a balloon-traffic system, or something in that
way. The Councillors are always deeply im-
pressed by any proposal to Pause. As jnagnifieent
pausers they sit unrivalled.
Bishop Webber is of opinion that
the kanaka has a good time in Queensland.
In proof of this, he tells a yarn of how a
deputation of islanders approached him
and requested him to remove a certain
clergyman who was “not much good,” be-
cause he could not teach singing. Dr.
Webber further ridicules the idea that
there is anything like slavery in Queens-
land. Courier pats him on the back, and
remarks: “We frankly admit that if the
inevitable result of this contact
was contamination and deterior-
ation, it would be an obligation
upon us not to bring them.”
The Courier sees no evidence of
deterioration in the circum-
stance that the kanaka death-
rate is about 30 per thousand,
or in the fact that the popula-
tion of Polynesia is not 40 per
cent, of what it was 30 years
ago—not the least. No ! That
gallant organ goes on to argue
that because Tommy Tanna is
so well off, it’s quite time
Queensland introduced the Pa-
puan. See ?
Reid has been through-
out his term as N.S. Wales
Premier notably kind to M. ’sP.
The services rendered were in
all cases at the expense of
the country, however, Par-
nell’s motion to cut off the
late trams was, when an effort
was made to carry it out, hushed
to rest by Reid’s assurance that
in his reign there would be few
late sittings. He was kind
especially kind to the Public
Works Committee, giving them
in every session pretexts for draw-
ing fees when no actual value could
attach to their reports. In his Bill
proposing to amalgamate N.S.W. and'
Govt. Savings Banks under a paid di-
rectorial board of seven he thoughtfully
provided that M.’sP. were to be eligible.
In fact, members who want anything, from frank-
ing their letters upwards, at the country’s ex-
pense, could depend upon Reid’s encouraging
support.
The talk of appointing J. H. Carruthers,
a solicitor, to the office of N.S.W. Attorney-
General, recalls the ominous fact that a similar
appointment of James Martin, when a solicitor,
was the cause of an immediate and successful vote
of censure on the Cowper Govt. It was that vote
that determined Martin to obtain admission to
the Bar.
In every N.S.W. Parliament since pay-
ment of members became law one or more members
refused to accept the monthly cheque. With
Adrian Knox, the last of these has disappeared.
Dugald Thompson w.ill still distribute his pay in
charity, but he will take his cheque. Bruce Smith
allowed his to accumulate till it reached £BOO and
then lifted it. In the Victorian Legislature,
Higinbotham out-Smithed Smith, for he declined
to touch his member’s salary even when he retired
from the House. He declined to touch it while
Chief Justice, but at his death it went in a lump
sum to his widow.
The Yellow Pup has worried its way into
the Vic. Labor-party. A split in the voting has
already occurred through his yelping in South
Melb. Although two nominal members of the
Parliamentary party Gray, of Prahran, and
Vale, of Ballarat—are pronounced Orangemen,
the color difficulty has not before arisen in the
counsels of the organisation.
There is promise of trouble in N.S.W.
Labor-party by the arrival of the eloquent and
pungent debater, Holman, from Grenfell. He was
never wanted by the present party, because he
forced them, at the 9th of Nov. conference, to
accept the metaphysical fallacy that a politician
has no conscience worth troubling about, and
insisted on the now accepted pledge.
Tas. mines now employ 5016 whites and
262 Chinese. White increase over last year, 1036 ;
Chinese decrease, 64. Nobody seems to know’
where these 64 Chows, together with the few hun-
dred new arrivals, have gone. Certainly meat
has been at famine prices in Tas., but ? One
can’t imagine that the increased demand for vege-
tables has found work for all the surplus heathen.
Only one Labor-member in the last
N.S.W. Parliament failed to get back. And—
bitter irony ! —-it was that particular one—Black—
who, on the morning of the vote, wrote to S.M.
Herald to correct a “ mis-statement,” and to
announce: “There will be no need to take a
caucus as to whom we shall support when Parlia-
ment assembles. We know with whom we have
to deal.”
Turner Govt.’s Land Bill provides for the
protection of a certain area, containing the house,
from the ravages of the mortgagee and sheriff.
There have been several unsuccessful attempts by
private members to introduce the Homestead
Protection principle, so successful in some of the
United States, into Victoria ; but this being a
Govt. Bill may escape the usual mutilation by the
House of Mortgagees.
Broken Hill polled a majority for the
Commonwealth Bill. Yet, so solid is the Labor
vote there that, when it came to a choice between
the Labor man and a Bartonite candidate—a
fairly-strong local man—the former had a
majority of 943 ; which beats Carruthers’
majority at St. George by 14. And the position
in the neighboring Alma, Sturt, and Wilcannia
was only “a little less so.”
Q. Premier Byrnes has apparently sized
up the great services rendered to Mongrelia by
his predecessor, Nelson. The Governor’s speech
at the openin : of Parliament contains no reference
whatever to the past doings of the Govt., and has
not one word of thanks for the great financier
who “ thoroughly investigated ” the Q.N.B.’s
affairs in ’93 and found them as regular and satis-
factory as those of the Bank of England.
Lord Kilmorey is trying to pilot his big
outer-harbor scheme through S.A. Parliament.
Proposed to spend £2,000,000, mainly English
capital, within five years. Apparently there is
to be no restriction upon wharfage charges, and
Govt, is not to be allowed to purchase until 1925.
The proposals will meet with strong opposition
from S.A. Liberals, who intend to advocate the
construction by Govt, of a similar work.
A MILITARY INCIDENT.
ADVANCE GUARD (to Australian Highland regi-
ment): “ Back there I if you don't want to
he eaten to the bone.”
Wrixon has a Gladstonian consuming fire
to get into the limelight, be in evidence. Hence
his democratic aberrations, even as his father-in-
law, Money Miller, stood almost alone in the
Council as a champion of M'Culloch. The day
has bygone when Wrixon was thought the only
possible man for Vic. Chief Justice, Speaker-or
Premier.
The persistent rumor that one coasting co.
is about to man all its steamers with coolies
renders it imperative that, in default of the possi-
bility of immediate federal action, combined
representations should be made to the Imperial
Govt, to put the merchant shipping laws, as far as
they apply to Australia, on a “ white ” footing.
Grave doubts seem to exist as to the power of the
provincial legislatures to act effectually in the
matter.
THE VENTILATED PAUPER.
“Cold? Nonsense, man! This fine bracing weather you can
enjoy your clothes.”
One of the jokes of the recent N.S.W.
campaign was the constitution of the Reid candi-
dates’ selection committee. It contained two
recent Prudent Federalists, a member of the now
almost defunct National Ass, and a man who lost
his .£4O deposit in ’9l, and who could not poll 20
votes at a municipal election in a suburb where he
was well known. These men “ selected ” the men
whom people were asked to vote for as Federalists.
J. H. Cann, the Labor-member “ men-
tioned ” for a Reid portfolio, was, curiously
enough, the very member chosen by the party to
go up to Lithgow and run as opposition candidate
to Cook when the latter accepted N.S.W. Post-
master Generalship. But not even Cann could
hold the Broken Hill seat against the “ Soli-
darity ’’candidate, who would be run in opposi-
tion if he took the loaves and fishes without the
party’s sanction.
“ What’s in a name ? ” Sydney D.T. now
persistently labels S.M. Herald “the Sydney
morning Protectionist organ ” and “ our morning
Protectionist contemporary.” The Herald wants
Federation. TheD.T. doesn’t —because Federation
means Australian National Protection! “Our Pro-
tectionist contemporary ! ” A straw shows ! The
D.T. is clearly prepared to sacrifice Australian
Nationalism to its Freetrade-shoddy fetish. The
Herald isn’t. The D.T. is now the Sydney
morning Freetrade organ—with a vengeance.
It was generally expected that Sargood,
and not Fitzgerald, would have been put up in
Vic. House of 48 Rich Landlords to scoff an old
rich scoff at the revolutionary proposals of Wrixon.
But Sargood remained silent, because, so it is
said, he was not altogether out of sympathy with
Wrixon’s proposed reforms. Yet Sargood left
the Turner Ministry on account of its One-Man-
One-Vote proposals—which, by the way, were
adopted by the Assembly on second reading last
week by 61 to 18.
Premier Byrnes puts it : Before any
Japanese immigration into Q. can take place the
persons desirous of introducing the little brown
alien must first communicate with the nearest
immigration-agent, who will apply to the Home
Secretary to obtain the desired permission.
Further, Byrnes'avers the Jap. Govt is not favor-
able to its people coming to Q., and will not
permit any emigration unless Q. Govt, expressly
sanctions it. It will be very easy, then, to fasten
the responsibility of the next landing on the right
shoulders.
There are 11 Labor members out of 54 in
S.A. Assembly, and 4 out of 24 in the Council. It
is the strongest Labor-party in Australia, and
nominally it bolds the balance of power. But in
reality it does nothing of the kind, for, though it
could turn out Kingston.by allying itself with the
Downer crowd, a Labor-party which threw in its
lot with that gang of bitter Tories would never
come back after next election. Glynn tried to fix
up an alliance with the Labor-men for his want-
of-confidenee motion, but the best conditions on
which they could join him made the hair of the
Tory Oppositionists stand up with horror.
The Australasian (the weekly edition of
Melb. Argus) has begun to curse Premier Turner
with its support, and especially approves of his
proposed reduction of the income-tax. “An
income-tax,” it says, “ is essentially an emergency
tax, and the rates now levied could not be in-
creased in case of emergency.” Victorian income-
tax rates range from 4d. to Is. 4d. in the £, ac-
cording to the wealth of the taxpayer. The man
who earns, by personal exertion, not over <£l2oo a
year pays only 4d. in the <£ with .£2OO exemption.
There was a time in Britain when all incomes
above .£l5O paid Is. 4d. in the £ (four times the
Vic. rate); even now they pay Bd. in the £ (twice
the Vic. rate); and Britain is manfully liquidating
its big debt with the proceeds. If the Australian
had J. Bull’s grit he would do likewise, instead of
wailing that he is a poor crushed worm who
can’t afford to pay his way.
That fine old Maoriland
Tory, G. F. Richardson, wants
the secret ballot introduced
into Parliament. What a lovely
episode it would be when a
motion was on (say) for the re-
duction of members from 100
to 50 ! There would be a big
House—even the dying mem-
ber would get down on an am-
bulance ; and the whole 100 dis-
interest d patriots would sup-
port the suggestion, and then
the Bill would be defeated by
99 votes to 1. And every one
of the 100 would swear that he
was the one..
A State-owned .steamboat
service between Wellington and
Lyttelton (M.L.) to connect the
railways of the two islands is
threatened by theSeddon Govt.,
the existing private service be-
ing regarded as inefficient. But
what is obviously wanted is the
completion of the northern
trunk railway from Auckland
to Wellington, and the exten-
sion of the main South Island
railway to Blenheim and Pic-
ton. Then a State-owned ferry
service across Cook’s Straits
would give the State a practical
monopoly of the traffic from
Waitemata to the Bluff—some-
thing approaching 000 miles in
a bee-line.
Premier Seddon’s Second-
Ballot Bill, now before M.L.
Parliament, provides that if
the candidate at the top of the
list doesn’t get nine-twentieths
(why not make it one-half,
which is quite as fair and much
less complicated?) of the votes
recorded he and the next man
on the list must poll over again.
But the M.L. House of Reps,
has already thrown out two
similar measures. The average M.H.R. doesn’t
mind being liberal when he gets in, but he is
bitterly Tory about the mode of getting in.
The latest yarn circulated (and printed) by
S: A. Opposition, for the purpose of injuring the
Ministry, is the canard that Education Minister
Butler and his wife had “ perverted ” to the R.C.
Church from the Anglicans. The flimsy founda-
tion of the story is that, some years ago, the sister
of Butler’s second wife married a Catholic, and
agreed to attend church with him. There are two
Catholics (J. V. O’Loghlin and L. O’Loughlin) in
the Kingston Cabinet already, and the Premier’s
wife also belongs to that church, so that, in view
of the coming general election, the story referred 1
to above was calculated, when unscrupulously
used, to do considerable harm A Register
writer to the country press has. the debit of invent-
ing it.
Men are the slaves of names and signs and
outward pretences. The N'.S.'W. anti-Bill party—
the Salomons-Want-Pilcher-Heydon-Dibbs push
with nine-tenths of the N.S.W. Council enthusi-
astically supporting it; and Premier Reid, the
man who voted for the three-fifths majority and
who promises to preserve the Nominee Upper
House ; and the Cabbage-Tree Mob; and all the
landlords of Sydney, who are prepared to
damn any and every Federation unless the
Federal capital is placed right on their property—
had the ineffable cheek to call their gang of re-
actionaries the Liberal and Democratic party.
And casual individuals, hearing that these hide-
bound Tories are the Democratic party, still
write at intervals to The Bulletin to know why
it isn’t supporting the Democratic crowd. This
paper has a vague' idea that the Spanish institu-
tion which combusted the local Protestant used to
call itself the Liberal and Progressive Democratic
Inquisition, or something like that.
Sydney Telegraph about put up the
Ananias record last Thursday, and took the bun
out of Sodom and Gomorrah, when it announced,
under the big heading “Protectionist Misstate-
ments,” that the Barton party only scored 11,017
votes at N.S.W. general election against over
82,000 for the Reid party. Its logic is that all the
old members of the Barton way of thinking are
simply Protectionists who would have been re-
turned anyhow, and their votes shouldn’t be
counted, and they don’t show that the country
approves of Barton’s Federal policy though they
advocated that policy. And, on the other hand,
when the old members were returned on the other
side they aren’t simply Freetraders who would
have been returned anyhow on the Freetrade
ticket, and their votes should be counted, and they
do show that the country approves of Want and
Reid’s Federal policy. Think of the joy of being
any party’s newspaper hack and having to stake
your immortal soul—if you have one—on yarns
like that for a living !
B. R. Wise, in his Convention remarks on
the Referendum —the remarks which were so
wildly garbled at election time—pointed out one
weak spot in the arrangement. Some one must
formulate the question to be submitted to the
people, and a great deal depends on the way it is
formulated. For instance, the prohibition ques-
tion could be put to Australia in general in these
two ways :
First way : “ Are you in favor of the abolition of the
drink curse?”
Second way : “ Are you in favor of the total prohibition
of alcohol and the raising of about £2,200,000 per annum
by some new form of direct or indirect taxation to make
up the loss of revenue ? [lf this loss is made good by
customs it will involve thedoubling of all existing duties.
If by direct taxation it will be equal to about 2s. in the
£ income-tax or about 4d. in the £ land-tax in addition
to existing taxation.]
Even with this drawback, however, the Referen-
dum will give a vastly more direct answer on any
big problem than a general election can do. For
it keeps the local culvert out of the question, and
at general elections the culvert intrudes itself into
everything.

Premier’s Turner’s Budget speech is a suf-
ficient answer to the Age’s question—What has
happened to the Vic. Railways rolling-stock, of
which no trace can be found ? Evidently it fell
to pieces, as a rule, during the Age regime of
“ strict economy ” in the dept. Expenditure on
rolling-stock in 1892-93 was .£243,000 ; next year it
fell t0*£37,000, and, during the next three years,
only .£63,000 was spent altogether. In 1896-7 the
ridiculous policy of letting things “go to pot ”
was checked by Commissioner Mathieson. The
present proposal is to spend £130,000 a year for
the next three years in restoring the rolling-stock
to its old state of decency.
“D.C.L.”
When applied to a man is a high Academic
distinction. When applied to whisky “D.C.L.” is
the highest distinction it can possess. “ D.C.L.”
(Black Bottle) Scotch Whisky is perfect.
To judge by N.S. W. election results, there
must be a bad slump in single-tax. Its three most
pronounced adherents went down badly—W.
Harding, with only 126 votes out of 1131; W. E.
Johnson, with 153 out of 2264; and ex-parson
Hessell-Hall, with only 58 out of 1909. Also
Dobbie and Foxall had a bad time. Hessell-Hall
stumped Darlington electorate for nine months,
only in the end to lift another man into Parliament
over the head of Protectionist-secretary Schey.
The Bulletin's London correspondent
writes that the prosecution for fraud of “not
proven ” Monson (of Ardlamont alleged-murder
notoriety) and his confederates (cable says they
were convicted and “ sent up ”) is but the first act
in a drama of hideous crime which will shock the
world.
“ Lex ” re the Vie. County Court Judgjs :
Judge Williams’ sneer about the Vic. County Court
Judges being failures in the profession is as untrue as it
was uncalled for. Take the present minor Bench.
Molesworth was a brilliant advocate—especially on the
criminal side ; his defence of Burns, the Beep Lead mur-
derer, stands as one of the very best in Vic. annals. He
was also most successful at common law : and was run
after by solicitors for civil cases. Hamilton is most
learned in the law, and, although certainly no advocate,
he was a success as a chamber barrister. He has edited
the Judicature Act, and his work is accepted as the recog-
nised authority on the Statute. Casey is a political
Judge—a real mediocrity, but has a wonderful fund of
common Sense, and has made a capital Judge. Gaunt
was an able criminal pleader, and only Finlayson knows
more about mining law than his Honor. Chomlev
was secretary to the Crown Law Department; and after-
wards Crown Prosecutor. No one ever said this high-
toned #nd urbane gentleman was a failure in that posi-
tion. He was not a Crown Persecutor, but no barrister
in Vic. ever held the Crown criminal briefs more ably or
successfully than this Judge. On his elevation to the
Bench, Mr. Purves told his Honor this, and surely that
leader of the Bar, above everyone else, should know.
WATCH.
WATCH your footsteps; you may slip and injure yourself.
WATCH your habits ; they may grow upon you unawares and get beyond
your control.
WATCH the tendency of the times. The world is moving and changing
every day, and you cannot afford to be left behind.
WATCH carefully the interests of your true friends. They are always few in
number, and you cannot do too much for those who have been
tested and tried.
WATCH above all things your health. It is the most precious possession you
can ever have in this life.
WATCH for the first signs of a cold, a cough and symptoms of malaria?
pneumonia or consumption.
WATCH for the little leaks which make the difference between failure and
success in business. And remember that these leaks can only be
stopped by using
