SPORTING NOTIONS.
“ There is cursing and coughing and clicking of
noofs, for most of the ‘ wafers ’ have proved to be
‘spoofs’—of late, anyhow,” writes The Bulletin’s Lon-
don correspondent. “If Auruin—landed safe from the
‘ Damascus ’ big and well—and one or two of the others
don’t blur the judge’s eye shortly, the next of ’em better
trot to Footscray glueworks in place of mal-de-merring
all their speed and stamina on the broad Pacific. When
Maluma went for a £lOO crop of oats in the Diamond
Plate mile and a-half the others couldn’t get out fast
enough, and Lord Ellesmere’s three-year-old Ultimatum,
with Bst. 41b. of lead, and Loates, only started for a nibble
and to give the Malua mare a victory -so it seemed. On
the Australian, 9st. 91b., 100 to 30 was laid, and as she
never got in front at all ‘Mr. Jersey’ must have ex-
hausted the limits of her unfeminine lingual resources in
explaining the merits of Maluma as a second-placer. It
was unfortunate for the whitewashed Charley Wood
that he had the mount on this deadest of dead * snips.’
H. C. White’s Form is another able ‘ seconder,’ twice in
the past fortnight, missing 200 sovs. by four lengths in
Summer Handicap and 300 by a neck in the High Weight
which Acmena won in the same colors last year. The
last has 7st. 121 b. and more than a chance in Liverpool
Cup. Newhaven and Merman have been pigeon-holed,
and Carnage sold to the sauer-kraut Government for
£10,500 for stud purposes.”
* * • m
“Form put in a claim to his title and made his
first win,” writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent,
“ in the Wat-erbench £3OO Welter, carrying Bst. 71b., and
netting lOst. in a field of 15. Day trained, Bradford rode,
and owner H. C. White won his money by half-a-dozen
lengths. A few days later, in the Nottingham Bramcote
Stakes, he carried his 30st. 41b. nowhere that the handi-
cappers might notice. Maluma won her second
race here, 195 sovs. stake and Lily’s little bit,
in the Yigner Handicap, at 9 to 2, under 9st.
51b. The seven furlongs suited the daughter of Mad-
cap, and she was IJ4 lengths before the second. Fairie,
a Bananalander, has been * scooping the boodle ’ with
Eager, who won in a week or so the Rous Memorial Stakes,
£950, and the Duchess of York Stakes, £955. The same
potentate owns Chubb and Cauliflower, neither safe nor
flowery for backers just now. ‘Billy’ Jones’English
purchase, Stonebow, ran third in the Bottesham Plate,
and W. Cooper’s Charleareny fifth in the Royal Hunt
Cup. Survivor has had a flutter or two restricted by un-
limited avoidupois, and to lose weight will have to wait a
little longer.”
* * * *
Realgar and Bell Metal, in the Caulfield G.N.
Hurdle Race last Saturday, ran true to their form of 17
months ago, when they finished second and third in a
hunters’ flat race at Moonee Valley. Realgar then gave
Bell Metal 91b. and a decided beating. On Saturday he
gave away 121 b. Both horses were favorably handicapped
for the Caulfield event, but Mr. Miller’s uncertain son of
Malua had the best of the terms on paper.
* * * *
Betting on the first race at Caulfield was very
characteristic of V. A.C.T. sport. It opened with Majesty
as favorite, some of the others not being expected to
“come.” Then Hailstorm was backed down to a shorter
price than Majestic, and finally Ike Foulsham’s Massinissa
sprang from 10 to 1 to 5 to 1, equal favorite with Hail-
storm. Massinissa won, with Hailstorm second. The
market gives some very straight tips—at Caulfield.
* * * •
Mai to, who is fancied for the Cups, ran a very
disappointing race in the Balaclava Stakes. Getting
away in front, the Ballarat nag led the field to the turn
and then dropped away. Something galloped into him,
perhaps.
* * ♦ *
It is mainly N.Q. money that is being dribbled
on to Merloolas for Melbourne Cup. If he gets home at
Flemington hundreds of boundary riders and stockmen
will flourish for a season. When Ben Bolt won at Caul-
field years ago, beer and whisky flowed for days in sleepy
Bowen. But, good as Merloolas is, 9st. 101 b. is a steadier.
* * * *
The action of the V.R.C., in summoning a
meeting of country racing-club delegates to discuss the
tote has caused the countenance of Ikey Mo to drop
about half-an-inch. There are two meanings to the ex-
pression, “got another mug,” and in this case Ikey’s
mug is a sad circumstance instead of a joyful one.
V * * 9
Significant the silence of candidates for V.R.C.
committee when invited to express their views re the
Totalisator. The conference with country clubs, as sug-
gested by chairman Miller, means simply a conference of
metropolitan delegates, who may, or may not, voice the
opinions of the clubs they represent. A true expression
of public opinion can only be obtained by a referendum
of racegoers. And, after all, the wishes of Tom, Dick,
and Harry deserve as much consideration as those of the
big and little men of the horse-owning fraternity—if
not more. Owners and big punters, of course, view the
machine with dismay. The fact that the cleverest
devices to obtain the early market will serve naught
under a system whereby everyone is placed on equality
as regards the odds to be obtained, is obnoxious to their
predatory instincts. They can’t look beyond—they don’t
want to—to the vista of large stakes, more horses, and
honest running. Yet these are the men whose views are
invited and whose cries are hearkened to as coming from
the high places of authority. Let them have their say,
I>Y all means, but let it be also understood that such say
is representative of only a section, the smaller one, of the
sporting community.
Opposition to the tote comes from three sources
—from religious bodies, from bookmakers, and from
owners and big betters. The hysterical shrieks of Rev.
Snufflebuster and his brethren may be dismissed as the
possibly well-meaning, but ignorant, protests of a crew of
i usvbodies “agin everything” but their own religious
doctrines. The bookie sees his living gone. Regrettable,
no doubt, in the case of certain worthy men who have
been convenient mediums to the public. But Mo is
a parasite, and, as such, is generally undesirable. The
owner realises that a new set of tricks will be necessary to
choke a gullible public off his good things, and so scoop
the dividends for himself. The professional punter, arro-
gant with his knowledge of “ ways that are dark,” resents
a system which will minimise his advantages in the
matter of odds. The hawks, in fact, see the dovecote
safeguarded and the pigeons secure from plucking, and
the parson pronounces the benediction over their pro-
testing flutters. Imagine an opposition conference—the
rooks under the W.C.T.U. roof 1
• * * *
The Chief Commissioner of S.A. police testifies
that the tote has “ done away with forward betting, and
no case of embezzlement, or similar offence, which can
be traced directly or indirectly to totalisator betting has
come under his notice.”
* • * •
A motion is to be brought before the A.J.C. to
the effect that all horses not intended competitors for
any race must be scratched 12 hours prior to such race.
If the time were extended to 36 hours, the public would
be safeguarded in its most vulnerable part.
* * * •
Another motion to be brought before the A.J.C.
wishes to raise the minimum weight in handicaps from
6-st. 71b. to 7st., and deserves all the consideration it can
get. If the minimum were even 7st. 71b. racing would be
improved, for owners could then put up a strong lad, or
a small, thin lad, instead of the mannikins who now
ride, or rather sit upon, the featherweights and begin
to go to the devil on the night of their first important
win.
* * * *
Sydney loses a speedy and honest mare and a
t rue daughter of “ Old Jack ” in Raven’s Plume, who is
going to her owner, Capt. Scott, late of Kirkham, now
doing a little racing in Ireland. Raven’s Plume will have
as travelling companion the Maorilander, Levanter,
lately purchased by Capt. Scott for 500 guineas.
* • * *
Lochiel’s stock have won 97 races in Australia
during the past season, and he thus heads the list of
winning sires with £15,2225, whilst the expatriated Car-
bine and Trenton are second and third respectively with
£11,138 and £10,840; Gozo comes a good fourth with
£10,059; and then a great hiatus, Neckersgat, scoring
° n ’y £5205. Like Mr. Stead, in M.L., for the fourth time
Mr. W. R. Wilson tops the list of winning owners with
the goodly sum of £10,300 to his credit, Mr. Duggan, the
owner of Amberite, coming next with £7076.

Dear Bulletin.—The drawing of Tatt. on the
morning of the race is gambling pur et simple. With all
due deference to you I should put that down. It has no
more to do with racing than the daily Madrid lottery, the
Louisiana State lottery, or pak-a-pu. But (which is not
likely to occur) if I had my way, I should have, or allow,
municipal or State daily gambles. As I told you long
ago, the fire brigades were supported in New Orleans by
the daily Howard State lottery. The principle was similar
to pak-a-pu—Bo numbers, choose sor 10, I forget which,
and divvy accordingly. lam not sure but that it was a
tote choosing three numbers ; all the cash, minus a big
percentage for the brigade, was ante’d up at once on pro-
auction of ticket. To stop all chance of hanky-panky I
should make the sweep-conductor heavily fineableif clear
proof were given that (say) a country ‘ticket had been
sent and the horse in any way disclosed.—Scotty W.

* u? n a r . Sydney pony-race meet an
affable stranger drifted into conversation with me and
descanted, amongst other things, upon the pocket-
pickmgmdustry. “See that man with the red moustache ?
Smartest hand at the game in Australia ; why, if he were
ever to go to heaven, he’d pick Peter’s pocket of the
keys. When you see him about you should do like me—
put your hand on your money-pocket and keep it there.
You won’t come down to the paddock ? Ah, well, good
bye. For an hour after this I kept my hand on my
money and my eye on the red-moustached man, who was
also evidently watching me, for though I edged away from
him he always seemed to be near. Then I met a friend
and pointed out the “ smartest hand in Australia.”
“Why,” he said, “that’s Detective 8., I know him well.
Come,, and I’ll introduce you. The D. listened to my
friend’s yarn, and asked me if mv money was safe, as he
knew the man I had been talking to. As I had kept my
hand on it for the last hour I assured him that was ail
right, and asked him ’to irrigate, but when I planked
down my sov. on the bar there was a painful silence —it
was a half-penny ! So were the other three, and my
ticket had scribbled on it “ keep your eye on the red-
moustached man.”

A young Sydneyite’s wife, finding that hubby
was getting rather stiff with the coin, recently prospected
his pockets while he was asleep, and was horrified to find

“Jennie. 10s. ; Flossie, 7s. 6d.; Maud, 155.” The result-
ant kick-up educed the cause of the “ stiffness "—pony-
backing 1
* * • *
Years ago, two Dunedin heavy-weight sports
had each a good jumping horse, and excitement ran
high when, after four matches and two wins each, they
met for the “ convincer.” Both horses were greys, and so
much alike otherwise that the starter said they looked
like one horse as they got away. They kept together till
the last jump, when one fell, bringing down the other.
The first roan up and mounted again finished an easy
winner; but, alas ! —it was on the wrong horse l
* * * »
That repentant horse-dealer story recalls the
“Saved” horseman. He was attached to a stable
famous for its “commercial principles.” He sought
grace at the penitent form, and—which horrified his em-
ployer much more—announced his intention of revealing
the Dark Secrets of his Past Life. A carefully-bottled-up
horse, whose jaws showed signs of the tremendous wear
and tear incurred in deceiving handicappers, was to be
“ slipped ” at the next race-meeting, and a disclosure on
the jockey’s part meant the revealing of this, among
other stable secrets. Pathetic appeals from the owner
had little effect on the redeemed soul. A threat on the
trainer’s part was taken more seriously, as an interven-
tion of the devil, and a compromise was effected. Instead
of a public utterance, the jockey would limit his con-
fession to the ears of the “saved” in private meeting
assembled. Thus, all seemed well. But when the event-
ful hour arrived, with the nag at the post, and the com-
missioners in the market, nothing like a decent price
could be got. The cream of the odds had been snapped
by outsiders, while yet the saddle-girths were being
tightened in the straight. And to this day that owner
solemnly asserts that “ the Sal. Army commissioner
spoilt him of the best thing he ever had in his life. ”
» * • *
Westralia has at last got hold of a Judge who
does not hunt for opportunities to emphasise his inno-
cence of the turf and turf ways. His Honor James,
during the hearing of a horsey case, was told that the
animal had been represented as being a four-year-old.
“ Ah, yes,” sighed ’sonah, “it’s hard to find a horse at
the sale-yards that’s more than ‘ rising four.’ ”
* * • •
“O, the joys of watering 1 ” says Daudet, in
“Sappho.” And O, the delights of “ two-up ! ” The other
week the Melb. police raided a “two-up” club, and the
fascination of that great national sport was revealed at
the City court. Two pennies are placed on a piece of
wood, and spun up in the air, and the players bet on
whetherthe coins come down “ head ” or “ tail,” and after
every sequence of three heads a shilling is paid to the pro-
prietor of the “ club.” Twenty-eight men were caught in
the enjoyment of this intellectual pursuit, but none of
them could say why he went to a club for his “two-up.”
Why not form themselves into 14 couples and “ toss ” for
10s. a time until one or other is “broke” or both are
exhausted ?
* * * *
Rabbit-coursing with terriers, now very popular
at Melb. suburban racecourses, is a most cruel sport. The
unfortunate bunny, put down amid a yelling crowd, and
dazed by the yapping of dogs, has to be kicked and
shooed before it makes a feeble run ; usually, ere it has
gone five yards it is picked up, mangled and half-dead,
to be thrown aside by an attendant.
* * • •
The gentlemanly, well-dressed deadbeat has
struck a new wrinkle in Melb. suburbs. Whenever a
cricket or football club holds a meeting, he is to be found
at the door of the pub., and, by a few judicious remarks
about being a new' resident in the locality and an ardent
admirer of the game, he gets invited inside. Then, at
the first opportunity, he rises to announce that he will
give a £lO trophy to the best follower, a £5 gold medal
to the man w'ho kicks most goals, and a silver cigar-case
to the best all-rounder. Of course, he is forthwith elected
aV.P., and liberally wdned-up, but subsequent attempts
to discover the new V.P. at address given are productive
only of energetic and prolonged blanky !

The world’s broad-jumping record did not stay
long with the Americans, it having been wrested from

9|in. Roseingrave, of N.S.W., is credited in private

do this at N.S. Wales A.A.A. meeting in October.

Dear Bulletin.—Re average man’s distance-
estimating capacity. Was once witness at a drowning-
inquest, and gave as breadth of river at place of accident
“ between 40 and 45 yards.” Was pooh-poohed by
coroner, who said he knew the spot well, and that the
breadth w r as little, if at all, short of 100 yards. Measured
it afterwards and found it 43. The'official wasn’t a
shootist. —Alex. M.

Auction-sales and cheap bicycles don’t cut any
ice with the great sale of Red Bird cycles in Australasia.
The Sydney branch of the Goold Bicycle Co., Ltd., claims
to have used more than 1,750,000 ft. of timber in packing
cycles for shipment to various customers during the
past season. From this some idea can be formed of the
big demand there is for this famous cycle.*
The “funny” column in an Australian
weekly is conducted by a German Jew and a
Scotchman. Who can wonder at the cross-bred
“ humor ” born of such a union ?
The Late George Loyan.
George Loyau, who died recently in Queensland,
was in his day and generation one of the hardest-
working and most enthusiastic of Australian
journalists—and, like so many hard-working jour-
nalists, he has left a widow and family absolutely
without a penny. Loyau was not a man of bril-
liant capacity, but he was industrious and sin-
cere, and did much to spread light in dark Aus-
tralian places. Possibly, had he worked a little
less for the publio, and a little more for himself,
it would have been better for those depending on
him. At no time, however, was his remuneration
great, and in his old age he had to struggle hard
for daily subsistence. Loyau in his time contri-
buted to more than 200 newspapers in S.A., Vic.,
Q., and N.S.W., and The Bulletin, which sub-
scribes *5, trusts that some of his old-time
friends may be able to forward to this office some-
thing further in aid of Mrs. Loyau and family.
The only members of S.A. Assembly who
have held seats for 20 consecutive years are Oppo-
sition leader John Downer (Barossa) and Land-
seer (Mt. Barker). Speaker Coles, Committee-
chairman Catt, Premier Kingston, and Gilbert all
date from 1881.
Is the N.S.W. Labor-party so busy sup-
porting the Reid-Gould Government which pro-
mises to preserve the nominee Council, that it
hasn’t time to say a word on behalf of its fellow-
toiler, Suffield, now doing ten years in gaol on
absolutely no reasonable evidence? More than
one of the party has been in gaol, and knowing
what it is like should have some sympathy. And
Suffield hasn’t the satisfaction of knowing that
his spell in prison will help him to get into Parlia-
ment, and have <£300 a year, and a possible fat
billet on the Public Works Committee as well.
S. Law Society, whose members are
mostly indignant at Premier and Attorney-General
Kingston’s heckling of Insolvency Commissioner
Russell, has adopted the unusual course of not
appointing the Attorney-General as its president.
And, more galling still, the choice has fallen on
J. H. Symon. Mr. Russell, who has stood firm
against Kingston throughout the Harrold case,
has gone up enormously in the province’s estima-
tion. “ Sonah ” is a retiring family man, who
even shuns clubland, and had been put down as a
Civil-service milksop. Judge Boucaut may not
think it worth while to keep in harness much
longer, or arrangements may be made by which
Way, C.J. and P.C., may go to England and a
title permanently ; in either case Russell will go
up. And, sitting Ministerially, Kingston will be
the first man to acknowledge Russell’s recent
manifestation of spine as an additional qualifi-
cation.
“Creeve Roe” re Melb. postal arrange-
ments :—
There is an evening delivery in the suburbs. I get my
Bulletin in the evening always. About 6 p.m., as a rule.
Under previous conditions I used to get it the following
day (Friday). On the other hand, my morning letters
are delivered about 10 a.m., instead of 7.30 a m., as of
yore. Also, the red-coated men hate the new regime,
and are always worrying me to write a fearfully blas-
phemous letter to the papers about it. Poor devils, they
have to leave home at 5 in the morning, and get no break-
fast until nearly noon.
[“Creeve Roe” speaks for his particular suburb. In
some other suburbs the morning delivery is two hours
earlier than of yore, and the second (and last) delivery is
at 4.30 p.m., which can’t be called evening. Under the
new management, the suburban postman makes only two
rounds daily, instead of three, with an interval of eight
to nine hours between the rounds. The system effects a
saving of about £8000 per annum to the Dept.—and a
great loss in publio convenience.]
T. P. O’Connor writes of Hooley : —
He had a certain kind of curious delight, if not vanity,
in the terrible absorption and ferocious excitement of the
financier’s life—in beginning business at 7 in the morning
in his bedroom with half-a-dozen clerks and as many
brokers to take his instructions. He asked you to lunch,
telling you that you and he would be alone. Duly you
turned up ; you waited for ten minutes ; you waited h'alf-
an-hour ; by that time a score of people who had received
the same invitation had turned up in your room, in the
next room, in half-a-dozen rooms, but there was no Mr.
Hooley to meet any of them. The host of them all was
wildly busy, and then, perhaps, retired into one of the
labyrinth of rooms, and ate his frugal meal all alone,
doing justice to all his guests by being host to none. You
were never sure of an answer to any letter; you were
never sure of the keeping of an appointment; everything
in that vast, resounding, buzzing-floor—the very atmo-
sphere of which seemed close, feverish, almost insane—
was on the same principle of high disorder, vast, gigantic
inaccuracy, unprecedented easualness. Perhaps this is
the real explanation of the breakdown; though I am
bound to add that he is by no means the first great
financier I have known who did not seem to know the
very spelling of the words “ order ” and “ accuracy,” the
keeping of appointments, or the answering of letters.
Yic. Judge Williams, in admitting that
divorce-cases should not be heard in camera, con-
soles himself with the reflection that other Judges
have thus erred as well as he. But not even a
whole bench-full of wrongs make a right.
“ Ruapehu Has it ever struck you what
a prominent place fire occupies in the ideas of the
Creator ? His ruling passion seems to lead Him to
make millions of worlds and set fire to them. Or
is the whole visible universe only sparks from the
anvil where God makes really big things ?
The Asiatic alien, who may land in other
parts of Australia, usually makes at last for
Queensland. The Hindoos who landed down
South lately are nearly all busy now on Banana-
land plantations. According to the Govt. Resi-
dent of the Northern Territory 189 Japs, landed
in South Australia in the last 12 months, of whom
121 drifted off to Thursday in very short order.
And the Q. Democrat looks ever more yearningly
to the Federal bond of the near future—his only
hope of salvation from the alien plague.
Norfolk Islanders get no chance to vote
at N.S.W. general elections. Nominally they are
in the King Division of Sydney, but in reality
they are outside the country which makes laws for
them. Forty years ago Gov. Denison granted
these islanders the suffrage of one person one vote,
with ability to read and write the sole condition,
and had in contemplation a scheme whereby they
could exercise a voting influence in the N.S.W.
Legislature, but nothing came of it. D. Davis,
Shoalhaven’s new M.L.A., is a Norfolk Islander
by birth.
On Aug. 15, Dr. Salvado, of New Norcia
(W.A.), celebrates _ the 49th anniversary of his
elevation to the episcopate. His consecration (as
Bishop of Port Victoria, North Australia) took
place in Sydney in 1849. He became Lord High
Abbot of the Benedictine monastery at New
Norcia in 1867. There are 50 fathers and brethren
engaged in the mission (to the aborigines), which
is 82 miles from Perth, and they are nearly all
Spaniards. They rise regularly at 3 a.m., pray
for 1J hours, then breakfast and work till 11—then
dolce far niente till 3, and work till sunset. Father
Dominquez is second in command.
The Chamber of Commerce, at Bunbury,
W.A. (Premier Forrest’s electorate), decided,
apropos their deity’s declaration in favor of
Federation, that it would have none of it, because
Federation meant interprovincial Freetrade, and
the abolition of the protection of the local farmer.
But after awhile the Chamber thought some more,
and decided that as Federation would mean the
free admission of local timber into the other Aus-
tralias, they had better take it, after all. Sample
of town - pump reasoning which affects other
places besides the Westralian agricultural dis-
tricts. Federation — the great national ques-
tion, the birth of a new nation, to be weighed in
the balance with the local potato-trade and
passed, finally, because it will help the export of
wood!
Francis Dillon Bell, who died last month
at Shag Valley, Maoriland, was in the history of
that province ever since it had a white man’s his-
tory. He was an official of the New Zealand co.
as far back as 1839, and settled in the country
almost as soon as settlement commenced. He was
in the Leg. Council of New Munster (the old name
for the South Island) in ’48, and afterwards
in the Parliament of Wellington. He was
Treasurer for 13 days in the first Responsible Min-
istry (the Bell-Sewell Govt.) in 1856, and he was
Treasurer for 15 days, Native Minister for three
months, and Commissioner of Customs for 14 days
in poet Domett’s short-lived Cabinet of ’62. Also,
Cold-Tea Fox gave him an honorary seat in his
Ministry without office for a couple of years. No
one ever entrusted him with a portfolio again, but
he was Speaker for five years, and finally he had
ten years as Agent-General, and in ’91 the good,
respectable, hard-working old man dropped out of
public life for good.
A number of Q. legislators, including a
Minister now in the old country, paid a visit, some
time ago, to a S.Q. mining town. The Minis-
ter was suffering from a cold (it was evident) and
complained, while in town, of feeling dull and
stuffy in the head. A kindly fellow legislator of
Radical fame, sinking political differences, offered
the Minister a pinch of snuff—averring it was an
excellent thing for clearing the head. (Everybody
knows the eminent Labor Light who takes snuff.)
Minister accepted but couldn’t even raise a sneeze.
However, just as the train was departing, and the
M'sP. were craning their necks from the carriage
windows to say “Good-bye” to the crowd, the
snuff began to work and the great man very natur-
ally put his thumb and fingers expectantly to his
nose. But the crowd outside mistook the Minis-
terial movement for a certain yamin gesture indi-
cative of the highest contempt. The spirit of the
North was roused. In an instant 150 thumbs
touched 150 noses, 150 citizens spread out their
fingers, and the Hon. Joskins departed amid such a
salvo of salutations as never struck a Q. Minister
before.

Important Announcement with regard to “THE AUSTRAL WHEEL RACE.”
ADDITIONAL PRIZE MONEY.
The Austral Wheel Race is to be run on the 26th of November and the 3rd and 12th of December on the Melbourne Cricket Ground, upon the same
unbanked grass track as was used last year. As it seems likely that there will be more riders than ever this year, the Committee of Management have decided
to limit the number of riders in each heat. There will, therefore, be a greater number of heats than last year, and the riders in the final will have run greater
risks than ever. s
The Austral Cycle Agency Ltd. have therefore decided that the winner shall be rewarded by an additional £lOO to the prize already offered making
£330 in all. ’ &
The Austral Cycle Agency are convinced that on such a dangerous track it is of the first importance for riders to be mounted on Machines of the
very highest grade, and they believe that if low grade Machines were excluded by the Executive many accidents would be avoided. With a view, therefore, to
encourage competitors to provide themselves with the best possible mounts, and so lessen their own risk and the risks of those competing with them, they make
the additional £lOO conditional upon the winner riding a “ SWIFT ” Machine fitted with Dunlop Tyres.
This Machine, as ridden by Walne, Parsons, and others, has proved itself unequalled for speed, style, strength, and safety, and, as these four qualities
are essential to the success of the machine used by the winner of the Austral Wheel Race, it is obvious that the condition imposed by the Austral Cycle Agency
is rather advantageous than otherwise. b
The accompanying illustration gives some idea of the design of the Machine, and the following are the prices of the “ SWIFT ” PATH RACER in
the different Colonies, allowance being made in the absence of Federation for the Differential Customs Tariffs now in operation. We also give the specifications
of the Machine. & p

AGENCIES EVERYWHERE.
“W.” : Recognising that at least half of
Reid’s recent Federal enthusiasm is due to the
fear lest Barton should get before him and eclipse
him, how does “ Bar-Reid ” strike you as a name
for the Federal capital ?
Being hoist with one’s own petard doesn’t
possess the comic element that lies in being potted
with one’s own pistol, as recently befell the Syd-
ney policeman whose apparently lamb-like pair of
prisoners suddenly rushed him down and disarmed
him. Otherwise than finger-on-trigger, a revolver
at close quarters is worse than useless.
At the Australian Arc Exhibition in Lon-
don, that well-known art-connoisseur, Mr. Charles
Sedelmeyer, of Paris, bought at .£75 and .£52 10s.
respectively Mr. Lister-Lister’s large water-colors,
“Mount Kembla” and “Passing Showers.”
Both pictures were shown at last year’s exhibition
of N.S.W. Art Society.
Rumored in Sydney that Premier Reid
contemplates resigning his seat in favor of
“ Mother ” Millen. The little man, by the way,
was unmercifully chaffed about the “ Mothers of
Bourke ” when he returned to fix up things at the
Assembly, and now the unwary person who refers
to those kindly dames in the presence of aforesaid
warlike midget stands a good chance of being
demolished.
One of the features of N.S.W. general
election was the defeat of Minister Garrard for
Sherbrooke, where he thought his seat was a
“dead-snap.” No man ever ran an election in
N.S.W. at so small a cost as Jake’s opponent.
Young O’Conor had no election committee;
neither the Federal Association nor the Protec-
tionist committee sent a speaker to help him ; he
fought the election single-handed, with all the
local papers against him—and beat a Minister of
the Crown at a total cost of .£14 13s.
Of course, William the Mouthpiece of the
Almighty, tells us that “ God created Bismarck
as His instrument for the realisation of German
unity and greatness.” But how hollow these
words, and the recent reconciliation, seem when
we ponder the adroit and dignified snub in the
Iron Man’s self-written epitaph ; “ A faithful
German servant of Emperor William the First.”
One of the oldest of N.S.W. squatters,
W. 0. Bundock, died at Casino last week, aged
87 ; his estimable wife, whose generosity had
made her beloved in the district, died the week
previously, aged 80 years.
The Melb. agents for Tattoo Oil once again
desire to warn this thoughtless young country
against the danger ot inflicting indelible stains
upon the annual wool-crop. Tattoo Oil claims
the virtue of getting easily wiped out by the wool-
cleaner, which is more than can be said for the
cheaper compounds. Tattoo is absolutely inno-
cent of pitch, tar, or lead; moreover, its innocence
having been proved for the last 15 years, the sale
of the article is now advancing steadily through-
out the wool-growing world. Thk Bulletin
doesn’t know whether this is a suitable dye for the
human skin, but now that the British aristocracy
are seized with a craze for pricking patterns into
its arms, legs, and other parts, The Bulletin
would like to hear of Tattoo Oil being tried upon
a duke.*
R. D. Meagher, of Dean-case notoriety,
returned for the Tweed (N.S.W.) by a big
majority, went there saying he was a maligned
angel, and posed as a martyr. The majority be-
lieved him, especially the women. Hence this
post-election ballad in the Tweed Herald :—
Wonderful, isn’t it, what a short lapse of time and
a little distance will do !
Only 18 members of Vic. Assembly were
bold enough to vote against the second reading of
the One-man-one-vote Bill in Vic. Assembly, the
other evening. Sixty-one voted for it. The
womanhood-suffrage idea is not tacked on to the
measure this time, and the 48 Rich Landlords will
have no excuse for dodging the issue.
Sydney Telegraph claims that the result
of N.S.W. election was a triumph for Federation
of the Reid - Want brand. The hypothesis is
that the fight was a Federal one; but the same
week the same journal unblushingly proves that
Freetrade beat Protection by claiming the whole
anti-federal and the Protectionist-Labor votes as
cast for its foreign-trade policy !
“ The Princess Victoria, second daughter
of Albert Edward, if indications may be judged
by, will be allowed to marry Lord Revelstoke,”
writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent,
“ though the head of the Baring Bank is but a 13-
year old baron. The lady’s 30 summers appear to
have been accompanied by more than the usual
number of winters, and these, if her appearance is
considered, must have been unusually severe in
blighting the buds of feminine attractiveness.
The Baring is honest, though poor, on ,£lOOO a
year, the remainder of his income being devoted
to clearing off his dead father’s liabilities.
When the royal grandmother looked askance
on the suit the maid fell ill to an extent
that threatened her reason and showed her mad-
ness by shutting herself up from ‘society.’ A
brand-new baron is a bitter pill for H.R.H.’s
family, but what can be done ? Polly Brown, the
butcher’s daughter, refused to eat her father’s or
anyone else’s meat after he kicked out her lover,
Tom, the grocer’s son ; and Love, liquorice-like
Love, levels all. To assist the twain swains
Revelstoke’s sister has been made maid-of-honor
to the princess, so there ought not to be any more
difficulty in the exchange of ‘billies-do.’ Anyhow,
it is very fair odds that Victoria Junior’s family
will not be ‘ made in Germany. ’ ”
Our brave young Dick’s got home to-day,
And proud and glad is he ;
Full many a year he’s had to brave
The storm of obloquy.
But the clouds have flown, the sun shines free,
His heart is full of melody—
He scarce can tell the joy he feels,
Our Dick’s got home to-day.
Let cowards howl, and lawyers scowl,
And trait’rous asses bray,
But honest men with joy proclaim
Dick Meagher has won the day.
For his heart is true and gives thanks to you,
Tweed voices have made him gay,
The man you’ve sent to Parliament—
Our Dick’s got home to-day.
There is a gentle Bondi home
And loving wife is there.
And children’s rosy little lips
Breathe out a gentle prayer,
For Tweed voices bring news comforting,
And all are proud to say
The race is run, the vict’ry won,
Our Dick’s got home to-day.
Murwillumbah, 27/7/80.

The present M.L. Parliament doesn’t ex-
pire through old age for another nine months, but
the report still keeps circulating that it will die
comparatively young. The Opposition makes no
secret of its intention to talk out the old-age pen-
sion scheme and Council reform to the bitter end,
and the promised Bill for the suppression of
diseased meat is to be violently scoffed at as a
grandmotherly interference with private enter-
prise. The' hope of the Tories is that it may
worry sturdy Dick Seddon into an appeal to the
country, and that something good for the fossil
party may then turn up.
“ P.H.S.” to The Bulletin :
Editor Bulletin.— Re Pentridge and other penal es-
tablishments. Let me confirm your assertion that the
suffering involved in nine months “solitary” is beyond
expression. But worse still is the N.S.W. treatment of
juveniles. They have also to do solitary; first fortnight
on lib. bread a day : afterwards, same ration, alternating
weekly with a fairly good meat and vegetable ration.
1 But no tea ; no hot drink on the bitter winter mornings.
Prisoner has to be up, dressed and bed made by 6.30 ;
then bread and cold water for breakfast. I have myself
seen a yout h come out after three inont hs of this (solitary
included) miserably emaciated in body and completely
dazed in mind. Much as Oscar Wilde has shown of the
brutalisation of gaol life, he has failed to sufficiently
accentuate the petty tyranny of gaolers and warders. A
prisoner is absolutely at the mercy of these men, who will
and do band together in perjury to prevent a prisoner’s
complaint being made good, Not that many do com-
plain. Within prison walls, a man has no chance what-
ever of justice. He lives under the rule of a petty, and
often spiteful, autocrat, against whose fiat there is no
appeal. True, the prison regulations are equitably
enough framed, and, if properly administered, would ren-
der gaol life much more endurable, but they are simply
ignored in all cases where they tell in a prisoner’s favor.
Prisoners, for instance, have the right-on paper ! —of
appeal to the Comptroller-General, but I have known a
man get 14 days bread and water for daring to insist upon
this right. (I can give name, date and place for this).
The V.J. was the creature of the gaoler, who used to
liberally lubricate him with the dispensary brandy. I
was there, and I know! On the bestiality among the
lower class of prisoners themselves it is impossible to en-
large, even in the non-prudish Bulletin, but unspeakable
things are habitually winked at by the officers, merely to
save themselves trouble. Let, however, a poor, harmless
wretch of a clerk, say, accidentally tear his jacket or fail
to salute one of the tyrants, he will be run in and get
three days’ cells. The whole administration of theN.S.W.
gaols is desperately rotten, and inevitably brutalises and
debases all but the very strongest-minded men. To go
to gaol means mostly to become a miserable “ crawler,”
who will lick dirt for a bit of tobacco or the chance of a
billet.
N.S.W. “ Justice ” Department, however, after
long maintaining that its treatment of juvenile
offenders left nothing to be desired, has re-
cently stultified itself by considerably modifying
its regulations as regards diet and “solitary.”
There is no doubt whatever, though, that things
penal are otherwise in the horrible condition above
set forth by a man who knows whab he is talking
about.

R.A.F. : Is there a bump of spelling in
the human cranium, or is it merely the logical
mind abetted by a defective memory, which re-
fuses to accept the arbitrary ? Writer’s grand-
father, a parson, fairly well-educated, while editor
of a Corn-law League newspaper, had to employ his
daughter in correcting his proofs —-his father, a
mathematician, who knew four foreign languages
well and wrote them accurately, dared not write
an English letter without a dictionary beside him.
Writer frequently looks up the same word twice or
three times in one evening. Note—females of the
three generations had and have no difficulty in
spelling properly. The above screed is suggested
by a friend, who admits a like failing, and, almost
with tears, attributes his loss of fortune to it.
Engaged to a Scotch lady with pot of money, and
day fixed. But in an anticipatory note which he
wrote to her, he put it—“ when you are my honey
bride.” He spent reams of paper and no end of
time trying to convince her that he had intended
“ bonny.” No go ! She was a bit high in bone.

HERR RASMUSSENS
mission in the Herbal |||orld.
Herr Rasmussen has devoted his whole life to
the study of medical herbs and manufacture of
Herbal remedies, and his great mission has been
most wonderfully successful.
The great virtues of the Herr Rasmussen’s Alfa-
line Herbal Remedies are so well known that it is
quite unnecessary to say much in regard to them ;
the very fact of their having been before the pub-
lic for so many years, and continually growing in
popularity, is quite sufficient evidence that they
are all they are claimed to be. Taking into con-
sideration the fact that they have been awarded
the Parisian Gold Medal, and that so many thou-
sands of original and unsolicited testimonials have
been received, and maybe seen at the Head Office,
some of which appear in his pamphlets, there
should be no difficulty in predicting a great future
for these health-giving Remedies unprecedented in
the History of Herbal Remedies.
Herbal Remedies are gradually pushing then-
way forward, entirely taking the place of those
remedies which contain mercury, arsenic or strych-
nine.
The Australian press has fully recognised the
noble puission of Herr Rassrnussen, and praise
bestowed upon him from this important source has
not only been most whole-hearted, but also very
generous. Space will not permit to repeat one-
tenth of his best press notices, but few will suffice
to show the public the estimation in which Herr
Rassrnussen is held by the Australian press.
It is not unreasonable to ask, “ Is it possible
such success should follow unless these well-known
Herbal Remedies are all they are claimed to be,
and is it likely that their popularity should keep
growing to such an extent, unless they contained
exceptional virtues out of the common?” The
evidence that Herr Rassmussen’s Herbal Remedies
contain wonderful virtues is simply overwhelming,
as he possesses thousands of testimonials to that
effect.

Myra Kemble has lately- suffered much from rheuma-
tism.
A Shakespearian Fancy-Dress Ball in aid of dramatic
charities is to be produced—in a theatrical way of speak-
ing—at Melb. Town Hall, Aug. 23rd.
Josef Hofftnan, the pianist, fell off his bike the other
day and sustained injuries to the extent of £BOO. That is
to say-, he sprained his wrist and had to miss four recital,
at £2OO a recite.
“ Round the World in Eighty Days” is to be staged at
Melb. Royal early in September by Maurice and Bateman.
Bateman is a local dentist.
Haresfoot: “Billy Hoskins is on the exhume. The
saying was he would get out of his bed in the middle of
any winter’s night to act Affable Hawk in the ‘ Game of
Speculation.’ I saw Mathews in it first, and thought,
‘ Ah, Billy- can knock him into a cocked hat,’ but when I
saw Hoskins act it I only realised the splendid art of
Mathews. Indeed, Hoskins was an indifferent and most
mannered actor, though he achieved success under
Phelps, at Sadler’s Wells.”
The comedy “Lord and Lady Algy-,” which the
Broughs have bought in London for Australian pro-
duction, must be a curious piece, judging from the
notices. Thus, G. Bernard Shaw describes it as “ the
most immoral play I ever saw.” Lord and Lady Algy, he
says, “are a middle-aged pair, more completely and
shamelessly void of seif-respect than any other couple for
whom the theatre has ventured to claim sympathy.”
“Melb”: Scholarly Fred Appleton—he acted at the
Bijou the other week—was, 20 years ago, the Cecil Ward
of this city, and hundreds retain a pleasant recollection
of him, without exaggerating his talent. He was very
likeable in “School,” “Pygmalion and Galatea,” and
many other pieces Associated with Coppin, Harwood, &c.
Octogenarian Joe Raynor, who claims to be the oldest
theatrical manager and actor in Australia, was going
around with a diorama not very long ago.
“Paradis”: A congress of side-shows has just opened
in the place known as the Hippodrome, corner of Lons-
dale and Exhibition (formerly Stephen) streets, Mel-
bourne. This historical kind of area was first the site
of Rowe’s circus, and afterwards of the Iron Pot, Coppin’s
Olympic, which was destroyed by fire, having served as a
Turkish bath and then as a furniture manufactory.
Coppin fetched the Olympic from England when Bishop
Perry brought the iron churches, which figure at Williams-
town, p ort Melbourne, Collingwood, and elsewhere.
“ Henry V.” was first acted in Melb., not by Rignold, but
by Brooke, at the Olympic.
A Coolgardie paper complains that a theatrical la'dy
who came into the office to insert an advt. carried her
money in such a safe place (having been led to believe
that W.A. was full of pickpockets and spielers) that she
had to undress to some extent on the premises to get at
the necessary half-crown.
A theatrical benefit performance in London, whereat
the programmes were hawked around by “ pretty or
popular actresses ” who got fancy prices for the goods,
has reminded somebody that such good nature (or
vanity) on the part of “leading ladies” lessens their
attractiveness. No actress is a heroine to her pro-
gramme-purchasers.
Pattie Browne made her first appearance at the old
Queen’s, Melb., as Cobb, the stable-boy, in “Formosa,”
under the management of Hegarty, brother of the
Hegarty who now runs the baths at St. Kilda.
Illustrated English Magazine, this July, interviews
Melbi, who expatiates on her delicious home of child-
hood at Yarraberg, about the most unromantic spot of
Melb., between Richmond Flat and Collingwood ditto.
Father had an organ built for her, but the best organ he
built was her larynx. Her voice was a decided contralto
up to her first change in life. She is a lovely whistler,
and that was considered her only accomplishment at
Melb. Presbyterian Young Ladies’ College. Argus critic
Guenett taught her some music, departed Cecehi more.
Dear Bulletin. — Re “Miss Chicago.” The comedienne
attempting to pourtray an artless, unconventional, giddy
little thing always has a scene in which she flops on a
table and nibbles an apple. To sit on a table and nibble
an apple in up-to-date drama signifies that you are a
wild, impetuous damsel, shockingly original and all that,
but just choke-full of heart and the finer emotions. Very
good ; but audiences must have obsen ed that the dare-
devil heroine never does more than gnaw a small spot off
the pippin. Some day an actress will make herself
famous by taking the apple and biting it, and eating it as
a good, honest apple deserves to be eaten. The first
actress, the mother of ’em all, did not peck at her apple,
I’ll swear ; but, then, Eve’s teeth were her own, and,
possibly, the ladies of the “ perfesh ” have in mind the
case of the soubrette who playfully bit the finger of her
stage adorer at Ballarat Academy of Music, and left a full
set of ivories dangling on his dexter finger, like a minia-
ture rat-trap.— Silas Snell.
Mme. Modjeska is calling on the women of America to
purify the drama by insisting on the production of
“ morklly-healthful plays.” [Madame must be getting
old.] She is trying to bring about a combination of
actresses to refuse to take the bold, bad parts with right-
thinking ladies who will boycott all plays containing such.
But, without the wicked woman as a foil, how unutterably
tame and insipid would be the virtuous heroine. Flossie
has her place in the scheme of things, after all.
Fred. Belton, who wrote his very bird-limey “Stage
Recollections” in Sydney, told how, in his young days,
he acted with Mrs. Honey, a most lovely woman, in “The
Middy Ashore,” she playing the Middy. The first night
Fred saw her standing helplessly at the top of the stairs
which led to her room, and dressed as the Midshipman.
“Oh, Mr. Belton, I’m so afraid of these—these—” With
that she nervously ventured down with one foot, as if
dipping into the water. He skipped up, and carried her
down under one arm. Who could refuse such an office
for plump Pattie Browne ?
“ P.E.” : Apropos the suggestion that “ Nellie Farren
ought,to write a ho'Ok-” Judging by her tale to an inter-
viewer once in London, she has a vivid imagination, or
else she has the Malaprop faculty of getting things
mixed. “ When I was 1 out there ’ ”—lhey speak of Aus-
tralia as a sort of dark continent—“ I'heard of poor
Frederici—you must know Fred, had an opera-co. of his
own, and for long they had awful bad luck. Nothing
seemed to go right, and they were just on the point of
disbanding when suddenly Fred, struck it rich. He put
on ‘ Faust.’ He played Meffy, and his wife played Mar-
guerite. There was a tremendoushouse, and his fortune
was as good as made, but in the last act, just as Faust and he
went down the trap, he dropped dead. Poor Fred, it was
a dramatic exit! ” Novi', this is all true, except that
Federici—not Frederici—never was a manager, and never
had any bad luck ; on the contrary, he had the best of
luck ; for he got a fat salary for a very thin voice. He
also was not married to Nellie Stewart, the lady who
played Marguerite, and he did not die going down the
trap. He had a heart trouble, and used to carry in the
pockets of his various costumes nitro-glycerine capsules
to stimulate that organ. On the fatal night he had
omitted to supply himself with capsules, and when he
felt for them and found none, the fright caused him to
faint; he died in his dressing-room ere the restorative
could be procured. “Feddy’s” dynamite pills used to
be a grim joke amongst the pros., and had he not forgot-
ten them that night the parsons would have been de-
prived of a “ shocking example.” The poor fellow died
Of a bad memory. Nellie Farren should not write a novel;
with a talent like that for misrepresenting facts, she
ought to be a historian, or at least, a colonial treasurer.
Melb. P.O. bells chime, at six o’clock, the well-known
air of Sir Harry Bumper’s song in “School for Scandal
Here’s to the maiden of bashful fifteen,
Here’s to the widow of fifty,
Here’s to the flaunting, extravagant quean,
And here’s to the housewife that’s thrifty.
Let the toast pass, drink to the lass,
I warrant she’ll prove an excuse for the glass.
“Shifter" : About time that struggling actors and
actresses combined to save themselves from being
“ rooked ” by unscrupulous, callous managers, who occa-
sionally dole out a few shillings to members of the
clamoring co., and finally get away with a good many
pounds. One notorious offender seems to live upon the
foolish vanity of pros, which prompts them to act for
nothing rather than not act at all.
“ Holdar M.” : In town, to-day, watched two beauteous
actresses climbing down a ’bus. The first fair creature
safely negotiated the winding stair, but her companion’s
ravishing skirt clung on to some sharp corner, and the
dainty occupant slipped several steps. As a glimpse of
gaudy garter, real blue silk foundations, and other
treasure tantalised the public curiosity, a chutney-colored
alien rushed forward to' help. He seemed well-to-do, but
for once the white lady wasn’t having any nigger, and,
whacking the bland, foreign gentleman over the ear with
her parasol, righted herself and bore off.
: T* l ? reception given the “American Girl,”
at Melbourne Princess reminds me of a cheerful dramatio
incident at the eternal Temperance Hall, in a Tasmanian
township, about 14 years back. A snide variety co. struck
the diggings in a woeful condition of dirt, and thirst, and
disrepair, and appeared on the following evening. The
show wasi simply “ offal,” but the men bore with it till an
inhumanly ugly comedian came forward to chortle the
pathetic ballad, 111 be all smiles to-night, mothah.”
Hus was too much, and, after the second verse, the audi-
ence arose in revolt, and filled the house and the town
with cat-calls and a most doleful groaning. The singer
made two or three brave attempts to get. along with his
ditty, but the miners absolutely refused martyrdom, and
then, after a brief consultation with the vocalist, the
manager came bobbing over the slush-lamp foot-
lights. Gents, he said, sweetly, “give a poor
Woke a show, carn’t yer ? See here, Mr. Mo-
rafferfcy is willing to do the fair thing by
youse if youse 11 do the fair thing by him. Let him
finish his song-, an afterwards he’ll fight any gent in the
w» d ! r er !u e c S f a ? Objectlo ". s tohis style of vocalisation.
Now, that s fair n square, ain’t it ? ” A storm of applause
followed, and Mr. Morafferty finished his song in peace.
Then an underground boss from one of the claims went
up on the stage and declared that in his opinion Moraf-
ferty had no more music in him than a bunvip, and
Morafferty convinced him of his error in five rounds. For
five the variety co. drew crowded houses, and each
m^b t Morafferty sang “ I’ll be all smiles to-night,
mothah and subsequently whipped a local critic, after a
more or less animated encounter, whilst the patrons kept
the solitary policeman blaspheming out in the darkness.
It was only on the arrival of a relay of constables from
Launceston that the argument about Mr. Morafferty’s
vocal abilities was abandoned, and the company fled
loaded down with gold and glory,
A waxworks show recently struck a certain Australian
tmvn, and, amongst its attractions, announced that the
effigy of a notorious murderess would be shown. Before
the opening of the exhibition, however, the showman re-
ceived pathetic appeals from many well-connected
people to eliminate the feature alluded to from the exhi-
bition as the unfortunate woman was related to them.
The showman complied with the request. On the day
I! gu °? r °P a lady-like woman madeaprivate’appeal
to the showman to see the figure. “ She was our mother,
you know . said the visitor. She saw the waxen model
of her mother and wept over it for an hour. During the
stay of the waxworks in that town nearly every relation
of the murderess had a “ private view.” Human nature is
indeed a queer thing.
Main entrance to Melb. Royal, on a crowded night,
too/is a veritable death-trap. Stalls, upper-circle and
gallery all pour occupants in to swell the jostling mob on
the ground-floor entrance, which is like a funnel, with
the narrow end towards the street. Imagine how, in a
few moments of panic, this would become choked with a
surging mass of terrified stragglers ! Emergency exits
may exist, but are not made sufficiently conspicuous by
notices on the walls, as they should be.
S.S.” : I object to the stupid indifference with which
the actors and actresses receive the rattle of epigrams in
some of our better class latter-day plays. When a char-
acter in drama utters a particularly good thing, it makes
the same impression amongst his audience of “ pros.” as
if he had made some casual reference to the weather. A
clever talker would not continue to scatter his pearls
before such intellectual paofaydenns as he is usually-
associated with in the theatrical drawing-room. The man
capable of epigrams would rather waste his heart’s blood
than squander them on the intellectually- incompetent,
and an appearance of interest is called for in the recipi-
ent of a bon mot on the boards. “ Dot ” Boucieault took
care that his people should. manifest an intelligent
appreciation of the things they were supposed to listen
to, but then Bouci. was an artist every inch of him, and
a little over,
Telescopic William Don, when Southerly in Austra-
lia, was introduced to a certain individual now a Judge,
and said afterwards, “ Why, he was a turnkey in America,
and locked me up for debt.”

Clement Scott has related some of his theatrical ex-
periences of 28 years ago, when he was the dashing young
pioneer of “ independent and truthful ” criticism in
Hingland’s metropolis, and managers used to send fre-
quent notes to the editor requesting that another person
should be appointed in the place of Mr. C. Scott, whose
name had been struck off the free-list. If the gushful
Scott was a pioneer in “truthful” notice writing, what
sort of criticism did the drama receive prior to his “ bold
and fearless ” advent!
Speaking of critics, there is none more terrible in his
wrath than the representative of a certain cycling rag
when he has to deal with a management which is back"
ward in paying for its little ad. He goes behind the
scenes, and, fixing the impecunious lessee with his busi-
ness eye, says : “ Now- look here, So-and-So, if that little
account isn’t paid by to-morrow afternoon, I shall take
the ad. out of the paper (the Wheelers’ Miscellany, call
it), and withhold my review of your show. And (with
awful emphasis) you know what that will mean to you ! ”
The chances are that he doesn’t get the little account
settled, and then the almighty ad. ceases to appear, and
the world looks in vain for the missing review, and the
show probably- goes bung. Whereupon the Miscellany
man pulls his haf, over his eyes so as to look like a
destroying angel, and smokes a cigarette on the footpath
outside the deserted playhouse, gloating over the ruin he
has wrought. It is only necessary for a cycling jour-
nalette to say “ Die !”to a showman—and hie perisheth.

“Hans”: The average mummer’s opportunities of
seeing a play from the front are so infrequent that he is
as much impressed as the greenest backbloeks visitor.
Close observation of several prominent Melb. actors in
the auditorium of different theatres showed me that those
who were not superciliously criticising rivals on the
boards were regarding the most flimsy situations with
clenched hands and staring eyeballs. At the “ Idler ”
matinee recently a popular comedian was dissolved in
sympathetic tears.

“I will be down a little late this morn-
ing,” N j.W. Supreme Court Judge Manning
telephoned to his court last Monday morning.
Half-an-hour afterwards he was dead —of phthisi-
cal hemorrhage.
In asserting that Zola’s father had been
dismissed the French army for theft, the novelist’s
detractors confess themselves reduced to that last
forlorn device of impotent spite—the libel per
parentes suos. The Esterhaziness of their side of
the question, too, is fast being cleared up, if there
is anything in the confession of the major’s cousin
that said cousin assisted Esterhazy to forge the
Dreyfus pidee de conviction. Looks as if truth
were this time really going to prevail in the long
run.
The irrepressible Captain Slocum, who
voyaged round the world, lone-handed, in his little
yawl, the “ Spray,” writes to a friend in Sydney
that it was when off the Brazilian coast, near the
mouth of the Amazon, he first heard of the
Spanish-American war. “ The cruiser Oregon
came foaming up astern, asking had I ‘seen any
Spanish men-o’-war ? ’ I replied that I hadn’t,
and ran up my signals, ‘ Let us keep together for
mutual protection. ’ Captain Clark of the Oregon
considered that unnecessary, I suppose, for he
steamed away with a rush, looking for the
Spaniards. I was loaded, however, and had the
Spaniards attacked me I would have sunk ’em
with one of my lectures.” This is real Yank.
The interesting case of Wallace v. Wal-
lace is down for hearing at Melb. on 11th inst.
Old Johnny Wallace, M.L.C., is accused by his
sons (or some of them) of maladministering the
estate of his late father-in-law, Tommy Monahan,
in which many relatives of that unlovable million-
aire are interested. In short, pbre Wallace must
either disprove certain assertions made by plain-
tiffs, or lose his executorship of the estate. By-
the-way, it was old-man Wallace who paid a
£lOOO-fee to surgeon O’Hara for amputating, or
mending, the leg of one of his sons. Is the ex-
pensively-doctored young Wallace a plaintiff in
this case ?
According to N.S.W. Government’s in-
spired organ, Sydney D.T., it is now proposed that
N.S.W. Parliament should appoint delegates, and
ask the other Parliaments to do likewise, to con-
sider alterations in the Convention Bill. In
short, N.S.W. Parliament, which annulled the
popular majority vote on June 3, now proposes to
abolish the people’s elected delegates—the ones
whom the nation specially appointed to make a
Constitution, and whose Constitution the national
majority accepted ; and to create a new lot who
are not elected for the special purpose by the
people, and the delegates the people didn't elect
are to overrule those that it did elect !! ! Majority
rule and the Referendum and the voice of the
people have a good deal to put up with at the
hands of Reid’s alleged “ Liberal ” party.
“ Sabretasche ” : Nicolson being almost
the last of the Victorian police-cadets, I rake the
fires, or ashes, of memory in regard to a few.
Standish queerly eluded the rungs of the ladder,
and the more so because he came from England
under a cloud and alleged false name. Hare,
Hookey, Burton, and Brook Smith were among
the most dashing, while Palmer, Chambers, and
Beaver were about the quietest. None became
more popular than Kabat—once an Austrian
cavalry-officer. Walstab drifted to journalism.
Chomley alone attained the top of the tree, though
Winch and Nicolson at times held the billet of
Acting-Chief Commissioner. But the cadet-
officers were, taken altogether, unsavory, through
a little leaven acting on the lump, what with a
penchant for wine and women and borrowing
money from subordinates, who lent only too
gladly.

Dear Bulletin. — Re England’s losing
of tricks in the Anglo-Russian game at
Pekin. China has plenty of cause to hate
Britain for being the first to force her out
of her hollow-log, with its blissful ignorance
of the outer world. Further, Britain is very
far away ; its promises are indefinite; and its
support unreliable according to Li’s reading of
its rail-sitting policy during the past 40 years.
Also, its (xovernnu nt is at the beck of the people,
which Li looks on as the mob, and for the mob he
has a profound contempt. And Russia is very
near and very powerful, and its Government is an
autocratic and masterful one after Li’s own heart.
Likewise, Russia promises what China wants, while
Britain promises only what the British merchant
and missionary want. They both lie, but that
doesn’t matter. China’s question is—Which is
most to be feared ? —C. J.T.
“ Holy Roman ” to The Bulletin :
Archbishop Carr, a few weeks ago, interviewed Pope
Leo Xllf. at Rome, and on same occasion introduced
little T. E. Verga, printer, of Little Collins-street, Mel-
bourne. Verga’s claim to hobnob with His Holiness was
that he raised the funds to build the big church of St.
John’s on Clifton Hill, opposite his own house. The
story of how Verga began the church is worth telling.
The land -one of the finest sites around the southern
capital —was reserved for R.C. Church purposes, but, for
long after, the neighborhood was uninhabited. When
Clifton Hill was colonised and named, the land-grabbers
arose and looked about for sites to buy and re-sell at a
big profit. Inquiries were made about the fine slice of
land on the crown of the bill on Heidelberg-road, now
known as Queen’s Parade, and it was found it was a R.C.
reserve. This was too'much for the Yellow Pup, who
started to sneak round and bite the Papal calves. The
calves were sheep of Bishop Goold’s fold named Mul-
cahy, Hooligan, Fogarty and a lot more Bulgarian and
Cuban settlers. A petition was taken round for names,
asking Govt, to cancel the reservation, as no use was
being made of the land. Well, little Verga, though he
has a foreign name, is a Cheshire man, and the promoters
of the petition didn’t know he was a papist, and they
hauled the petition in for his blessing and autograph.
Verga, however, made some excuse for not signing, and
when the L. 0.1. had gone, and were well down the street,
he clapped on his bat and drove up to Archbishop
Goold’s. Next morning a big ad. appeared in the papers
announcing that the first stone of a new church would be
laid the following Sunday at Clifton Hill. On that day
the Bishop, Verga, the choir of St. Patrick’s Cathedral
and Father W. Kelly, the versatile Jesuit, went in force
to the Hill, assembled in a tent, and laid a stone that
crushed the hopes of the L. 0.1. then and for evermore
regarding that coveted allotment. And if ever such a
dignitary is created as the Lord Bishop of Northcote and
Oollingwood, the united diocese has a splendid Cathedral
ready-made right off.

General Debility
and Nervousness
A Truthful Narrative of Great Suffering
and A Radical Cure.
THE CASE OF MRS. JAMES WILLIAMS.
(From the “ Mrtrriehville Revieio,” 1617/98.)
(BY OUIi special reporter.)
“ Get the plain, unadulterated truth ! ”
These were the instructions given to cur
(Weeltly Reviev)) reporter when he was de-
spatched to interview Mrs. James Williams,
of Holmesdale Street, Marrickville, Sydney.
Our reporter was fortunate enough to find
Mrs. Williams at home, and on his explain-
ing the motive of his visit that lady ac-
corded him a most hearty welcome. No
one, to look at Mrs. Williams now, would
ever suspect that she bad suffered from any
serious illness. She is a fine specimen of a
woman, and looks in grand health. Our re-
porter complimented her on her appearance
“ I was very different a little while ago,”
said Mrs. Williams, with a smile.
“ And that's what I want to hear all
about,” responded the newspaper man.
“I suffered,” continued Mrs. Williams,
“from great
General Debility and Nervousness,
for I was seriously ill for many months.”
“ How do you account for it, Mrs
Williams ?”
“I was travelling by rail once, and three
pickpockets followed me, and jostled me as
f was getting into the car, and they suc-
ceeded in picking my pocket of my purse,
which contained » considerable sum in gold.
They were very violent, and the shock of it
all
Seriously Upset Me.
A few seconds after the assault I got very
faint in the train, and then commenced to
vomit violently. From that moment I date
all my trouble. When I got home I was
Entirely Prostrated.
Terrible headaches came on, which were so
violent as to nearly drive me mad. It was
ten times worse than neuralgia. Day by day
I got worse. I had no longer any desire for
food, and I never got any sleep, but only
dropped off now' and again into an uneasy
doze. Then I should get wide awake in a
moment, and find myself
Shaking all Over.
Of course I was not able to do any of my
housework.”
“Isuppose not, Mrs. Williams. I wonder
at your being able to get about at all.”
“Yes, during the earlier stages of my
disease I managed to get up and sit in a
chair, and sometimes I felt as though the
chair was sinking through the floor with me,
and at other times 1 shoidd feel benumbed
all over. And when I attempted to stand
up I was seized with giddiness, and fell
back in my chair.”
“Were there any other marked symp-
toms ? ”
“Yes, in addition to the headaches I have
spoken of I had a pain in the chest and
between the shoulders. It felt as though
there was a moving ball of wind around my
heart. ”
“Had you obtained medical advice at this
time ? ”
“Yes. One doctor thought
It was Consumption
that had attacked me, and another medical
man suggested that fatty degeneration of
the heart was the cause of this pain. They
prescribed forme, but their medicines had
no effect upon my complaint. Then they
recommended a change of air and scene—
they said nothing else would do me any
good—but I was unable to act on this
advice.”
“ And how did it all end M rs. Williams ?”
“Well, I got from bad to worse, and i.
was forced to
Take to my Bed,
and to get my sister-in-law to come to nurse
me. I had now got into such an irritable
and nervous state that the noise of the
traffic (and there is not much of that down
this way) would throw me into fits of
trembling! I wasted away terribly, and I
was the colour of death. The many doctors
who attended me w r ere unable to do anything
for me, aud at last
They Gave me Up.
I never expected to get better. I thought
my end was near. I was in this state when
a neighbour brought me in a small bottle of
Clements Tonic—it was nearly full. She
pressed me most earnestly to try it. ”
“ W hich you did do ? ”
“ I did, and for which I am devoutly
thankful, for this small bottle was the be-
ginning of my recovery from my dreadful
disease Clements Tonic worked wonders
with me from the first. It gave my shat-
tered nerves a calm and peaceful tone, thus
allowing me to get some natural sleep, the
want of which
Was Killing Me.
It strung me together, and braced me up,
and I soon got that I could stand more
noise and conversation, which only recently
would have thrown me into
A Shaking Fit.
The remedy gave me an appetite, and 1
could eat a hearty meal.”
“ I suppose you soon became entirely re-
suscitated ? ”
“ Yes, being able to sleep and eat once
more, I so n got strong, and my cheeks filled
out rapidly. A few bottles completely put
me right. lam sure there is no remedy in
the world to be spoken of in the same
breath with Clements 'I onic, and I have
proof of that, for, in addition to my own
cure, it
Rescued my Little Girl,
of three and a half years of age, from a
most distressing malady.”
“ I should like very much to hear about
that,” said our reporter.
“ Well, I will tell you all about it. I
took the child to a funeral, and I now think
it was a very unwise thing to do. My little
girl was very frightened, and went off in a
dead
Faint at the Graveside.”
“ What was the result ? ”
“ For seven months she suffered from
what the doctors said was
St. Vitus’ Dance.
Herlimbs would shake and move in a strange
way. In addition to this eczema broke out
all over the back of the child’s head. It was
a very bad case. She was a fine fat child be-
fore she received the shock to her nerves I
spoke about, but afterwai ds got very thin.
Her appetite was very queer, and she craved
for strange foods.”
“ How came you to give the child
Clements Tonic, Mrs. Williams ? ”
“ I read a case in a little book very like
that of my child’s. It much. impressed me,
and I started to give the child Clements
Tonic. Perhaps you will hardly believe it
but it is perfectly true, two bottles
Entirely Cured Her
of both her complaints—the St. Vitus’
dance and the eczema. I was astonished
that the remedy should drive away these
two very serious ailments so very speedily.
The child at once started to eat with a
natural appetite, and got fat and well in no
time. Clements Tonic cured the St. Vitus’
Dance in
A Marvellous Manner,
and the little girl’s head was perfectly clean
in a very short time. So now you can see
why I have such faith in Clements Tonic.”
“ And may we publish all this, Mrs.
Williams ? ”
“ Every word of it. It is but my duty to
make it known, that other sick ones may
have the benefit of Clements Tonic as my
child and myself did.
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Elizabkth Williams, of Coree House, HolmesdaU;
street, Marrickville, near Sydney, in the Colony of New
South Wales, do solemnly and sincerely declare thai
I have carefully read the annexed document, consist-
ing of ten folios and consecutively numbered from one
to ten, and that it contains a true and faithful account
of my illness and cure by Clements Tonic, and also
contains my full permission to publish the same in
any way; and I make this solemn declaration con-
scientiously believing the same to be true, and by
virtue of the provisions of an Act made and passed in
the ninth year of the reign of her present Majesty,
intituled “ An Act for the more effectual abolition of
Oaths and Affirmations taken and made in the various
Departments of the Government of New' South Wales,
and to substitute Declarations in lieu thereof and for
the suppression of voluntary and extra-judicial Oaths
and Affidavits.”
Declared at Marrickville, near Sydney, this 80th
day of June, 1898, before me,
R. G. BRERETON, J.P.
I can endorse every word my wife says as to both
her cure as well as my child’s cure by Clements
Tonic.
