Sarah.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was always Sarah lately. A rasping, complain-
ing voice was for ever querulously crying out
against Sarah, keeping up a running fire of accu-
sations against Sarah’s honesty, against Sarah’s
faithfulness, against Sarah’s capabilities, and even
against Sarah’s morals. To all of which the child
would not, or dare not, reply. But when the
voice meandered momentarily away to an up-
stairs floor she would lift up her head proudly and
the hot tears would trickle down her cheeks un-
restrained. Then, the voice coming back, she
would dry her tears with hard, wicked blows of
her small hand on her face. The voice would re-
commence :
“ ’Taint no use of me talking ; you don’t take a
bit of notice of me. I’m nobody lately. It’s a
poor case that I can’t ”
Sarah went outside ; not defiantly, with a slam
of the door, but very gently. She was crying
again. It seemed such a large, incomprehensible
world, and she was such a small Sarah.
People said it was the baby that troubled Mrs.
Wilson. She should never have been a mother on
that occasion, they averred. She was too old—
they guessed 45. And the child was such a mite
—a poor, tiny little thing that never cried, but
lay looking up into its mother’s face with a
thoughtful, painful gaze, as though it felt itself to
be a very queer kind of baby indeed, and was
pondering the best way of breaking the news that
it could talk and think and read and write—in
fact, was quite an old man. The doctor saw at a
glance that it must die soon. But, being a com-
passionate man, he kept his knowledge to himself.
In three months the child died.
Then it happened that Sarah, the little orphan
and domestic servant, aged nine, began to find her
life a very hard one. Mrs. Wilson, whose young
husband had run away with dashing Polly Bin-
ders, became suddenly very harsh and irreconcil-
able. She was kind and gentle and patient no
longer. And Sarah missed the baby, too. She
wondered where the queer little fellow had gone
to,
“Where’s Freddy?” she asked once of the
mistress.
The woman did not answer.
“Oh, I know, ’cause Jimmy Smith said Freddy’s
dead.”
Mrs. Wilson commenced a hysterical sobbing,
and Sarah, not knowing enough to get out of her
way, was fiercely grabbed, and thrashed unmerci-
fully.
That afternoon she lay in wait for Jimmy Smith,
the butcher’s boy. He came along as usual.
“Greasy, greasy, slip along easy ! ”
The shrill cry of Sarah rang out, and, with her
apron full of stones, she made an onslaught on
Jimmy and his white horse. They fled. Sarah
was revenged.
“That’s for telling lies,” she said. “And
now,” she added, with an air of business, “ I
think I’ll go and look for Freddy.”
It was Mrs. Shaw’s darling baby-girl she kid-
napped. She had watched her chance for hours,
and then made her swoop when she considered
herself undetected.
Her mistress was upstairs when she returned,
so Baby Shaw was easily smuggled into the
front room, and gently deposited in the little
empty cradle which the lonely woman would still
rock sometimes just for the sake of rocking
back sweet memories. It sometimes mercifully
happened that she rocked herself to sleep.
Sarah went upstairs.
“Please, Mrs. Wilson,” she said, in a matter-of-
fact way, “Freddy’s come home.”
“Freddy ! ”
“ Yes ; he’s asleep in the cradle.”
Mrs. Wilson gave a short cry, and her thin lean
face went very white. Her iong fingers closed
over the girl’s hand. Sarah was trembling now.
“ What do you mean ? ”
“ Freddy’s—come—home.”
They went downstairs and entered the room.
The woman saw the cradle and the child sleeping
peacefully. She fell on her knees.
“God ! ” she said, “ help me ! ”
Sarah was kneeling beside her.
“ God! ” she said, “ please don’t tell on me ! ”
A knock was heard on the door, and the handle
turned. Mrs. Shaw, breathless and alarmed,
made a rush at Sarah.
. “You little wretch !” she said, as she caught
sight of Baby Shaw in the cradle.
Mrs. Wilson, still on her knees, smiled bitterly,
and did not speak.
The other woman lifted the child out of the
cradle and carried it in one arm out of the house.
With the other hand she led Sarah. She gave a
look at Mrs. Wilson as she passed. The baby was
crying.
Outside, she laid the child roughly on the
ground, and shook Sarah till her teeth chattered.
“ You little devil,” she said ; “do you want to
break that poor woman’s heart ? ”
“ Don’t beat me ! ” murmured Sarah.
But Mrs. Shaw knew she owed a Duty to
Society.
N.S.W.
J. J. O’M.
“ C.R.” : Flinders Petrie’s discovery that
Beer was the ancient Egyptian national drink
seems considerably subsequent. Years ago I
remarked in The Bulletin that the A.E. tipple
was beer and was called Booza, and drew the
obvious inference that our slang epithet Booze is
of Pharaohnic origin and older than the Pyramids.
Either Herodotus or Diodorus Siculus (filtered)
was my authority.
Thus the Argentine Consul on the “ Rise,
Decline and Fall ” of New Australia: —
Some 4000 or 5000 members were enrolled in Australia,
and over £20,000 subscribed to the movement. It is one
thing, however, to project an ideal state, but quite
another to enrol people fitted to populate such a place ;
most of the citizens enrolled had their own ideas and
were not backward in expressing them. From the taming
of a bullock to the building of a house each had his own
plan. The chairman of the concern held very strong
views on the ideal life to be led, but the majority of the
people were entirely out of touch with him. Thus ill
feeling was caused and secessions took place. When a
second batch of immigrants arrived from Australia
further differences were engendered and another separate
colony was established by malcontents some 80 miles
away. The very essence of working co-operation is
mutual good will, but these people had not got it in them
to bear and forbear with one another; they had neither
the religious element of the old Pilgrim Fathers nor the
fanaticism of fervent political reformers. This Utopia is
now a Government colony, though the administrator is
one of themselves. Whether the colonists who still cling
to the ideal —and the 60 squares of land—are able and
strong enough to revive the idea which underlies New
Australia, time alone will tell.
It sounds sarcastic !
Three Cards.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Challiner’s eyes shone with interest as they fol-
lowed the fall of the cards upon the worn um-
brella. The man who had just left the group.it
was evident from his contemptuous grin, had
halted on the margin of temptation. The bearded
man and the tall one who lost his silver with such
wanton exuberance, were only second-rate “ but-
toners.” Next to Challiner stood an elderly man
who had just transposed himself from the opposite
side of the improvised gaming-table. He moved
closer and examined the cards.
“Thought so,” he drawled. “A fellow can
tell the queen every time. Drop of rain’s fallen
in the corner. ”
I seized Challiner’s arm, and told him not to be
an idiot, but he made an impolite remark, and
shook himself free again. After all, it wa3 no
business of mine.
Challiner kept his eye on the card in the middle.
The tall man staked ss. and picked up one from
the outside—the ace of clubs.
“ Plain as pie, if you’d only followed the cards,”
commented Challiner. “ Here’s the queen.”
When the dealer suggested another try the
bearded man forestalled his tall confederate, and
won. The tall one staked again and won, and
went once more and loit. Challiner fumbled in
his pocket. Meanwhile the cards fell with the
spotted one uppermost. Challiner handed over
5s. —and turned the ace of clubs. Astonishment
was plain upon his face.
A second venture was suggested, and Challiner
lost again. A third ; but he intimated that he
was moneyless. The cards were already distri-
buted, and the tall spieler had taken another
chance and gone down. He also proclaimed him-
self cleaned out. Next time he proffered his
watch, but the virtuous dealer refused anything
save money - “ heaven knew what charges
mightn’t be rigged up against a bloke.” The
elderly gentleman near Challiner offered a loan.
The tall man gave his gold watch as security,
picked the q ueen, and redeemed his property.
The cards went down once more, and someone
hinted that Challiner should borrow money.
Again the cards were dealt, but he was obdurate.
Again, and he watched them as a cat about to
make a spring. A fourth time, and Challiner had
exchanged his watch for £i of the elderly gentle-
man’s.
The bystanders crowded closer. The confede-
rates nudged and worried Challiner. Simulating
intense interest in his fortune, the elderly philan-
thropist pointed eagerly to the soiled card. Then
I was astounded to see the simpleton pocket the
money and stride away from the excited group.
The elderly philanthropist was the first to over-
take their departing victim.
“ What’s your game now ?” he shouted. “ Any-
thing wrong ? ”
“ No. Changed my mind, that’s all.”
“ ’Off about th’ four quid ? ”
“My concern entirely. You see ”
A foot twisted dexterously round Challiner’s
ankle, and he fell. As I ran up, a leg shot in
front of mine—and there was a shove from behind.
Challiner and I sat where we had fallen, and
sheepishly regarded three retreating figures.
“ Satisfied now ? ” I asked, at last.
“ Cert’nly,” chortled Challiner ; “I’ve diddled
’em out of four quid. Chain’s only Yankee rolled
gold Oh, the land of the wooden nutmeg’s
a glorious country. Great scheme, isn’t it? Got
anything like a thirst on you ? ”
I admitted that I had.
* « * *
But the scheme did not appear so wonderful to
Challiner when he was passed along to the gaol
authorities for uttering a counterfeit sov., three
replicas of which were found upon him. Nor to
me, when I was permitted to depart with a sting-
ing reprimand.
A magisterial Solon told Challiner that, if his
yarn about the spielers was veracious, six months
would suit the case admirably.
THOROLD DALRYMPLE.
From Printer’s Ink, New York :
0. E. Osgood Co., house-furnishers, of Boston, an-
nounces that any of its patrons having unsettled accounts
who may enlist [i.e., to light Spaniards], may have pay-
ments omitted during term of services, and in case of
death the bill will be receipted in full.
Thus interestingly; “ Eko ” to The Bulle-
tin re Victorian Usury Bill :
“ I served for two years as clerk to the lead-
ing pawnbroker in a big Vic. provincial town, and
know a little about the subject. We had a large
‘ Saturday night ’ clientele—people who pawned the Sun-
day go-to-meets on Monday and took them back on
Saturday night. The boss used to talk of them as ‘ two-
penny lousy pledges,’ and truly many were ‘ fitted ’ by
the remark. The charge was, and still is, 2d. per month
per 2s. 6d. For this a duplicate was given to the
‘ popper,’the ticket written, the name, address, occupa-
tion, &0., re-entered in the pledge-hook, and the pledge
shelved, labelled and fumigated. All for twopence. The
pawnbroker had also to pay a heavy license-fee, bring
references of good character from neighbors, and run the
risk of losing his money r through the article being stolen,
he receiving no compensation. The idea that the pawn-
broker lives on the misery of the poor is all rot. Take
our office, for instance. We took in on an average
20 pledges a day, of which 15 ranged from 2s. 6d. to 10s.,
giving an average of 6d. per month, which means £l4 per
month. When you took out of that my screw (£lO a
month), and allowed for risks, you can judge how much
we screwed out of the poor. It was in the other five
pledges, ranging from 10s. to £25, where the profit lay.
These stood him in £4 or £5 a week. The pawnbroker
doesn’t give a rap for the washerwoman’s musty petticoat,
but he smiles with giee at the hard-up swell’s diamonds.
In proof of my statement that pawnbroking is not a good
game, can anyone show me one who made a pile by the
game? Of course, many of them do a large bill of sale
and promissory-note biz., and these prospered when they
laid to. their book. The man I worked with had
been in the same shop for 23 years, had the
biggest trade in , the place, and rarely speculated.
Like all Jews, he was economical, and yet, with all
this, when he went aloft, he left behind about £4500. He
screwed about £4500 out of the poor—in 23 years. There’s
barely a single trade that does not give better returns.
For example : When I left him I opened a photo.-studio in
the same town. The other photographers charged £1 a
dozen for cabinets. I reckoned it out. One plate, 2Jd. ;
12 sheets of paper, Is. ; chemicals, 6d. ; mounts, is. ;
printing, 2Jd. Total actual cost, 2s. lOd. I charged 15s.
a dozen. The second week I cleared a tenner over exes.
Then the other men dropped to 12s. 6d. I retorted with
10s. They fell to 7s. 6d. ;I to 6s. ; they to ss. As they
had more money than I, they drove me out, and returned
to their higher price. What per cent, per annum would
this reckon out? The pawnbroker, besides his other
handicaps, has to compete against the moneylender and
second-hand dealer. The latter, in nearly every case, is
an unlicensed pawnshop, and the former lends sums at
exorbitant rates to hard-up minors, and others. I could
name two of Vic.’s most famous lawyers who clear over
£lOOO a year by ‘ trust moneys to lend.’ What is needed
is a strict hand on moneylenders who fleece the unknow-
ing. A pawnbroker’s rates are fixed by law, but the in-
terest charged by the David Henry class is fixed by the
lenders.

Burgoo on the Border Line.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was a most distinguished mau,
With high silk-hat and manners fine,
Professor Dugald of the clan
He rode up in the coach with Dan
To Burgoo on the Border Line.
Now, Burgoo is a thriving place,
And built it was by thrifty men.
Tis famed for canniness and grace.
There Macs and thistles thrive apace,
And everything is Scotch, ye ken !
No Mac is easy taken down,
And chaps who go to prey, rem tin
Till done a crisp, inviting brown,
And clever spielers up from town
Depart, and never come again.
But of its hospitality
The town is proud, and men win shine
In art, or boast a pedigree,
Get bed, and board, and whisky free
At Burgoo on the Border Line.
The suave Professor was a Scot
Of goodly lineage ; apart
From which his dialect was not
Impaired by time and change—a lot
He knew of lit’rature and art.
He posted bills about the town,
And far and wide his dodgers spread.
These testified his great renown ;
And wit and learning both did crown
His famous monologue, they said.
“ Scotch humor ” was the theme, an 1 he
Was pressed by happy Macs to dine.
They gave the man a haggis tea,
And one protracted whisky spree,
At Burgoo on the Border Line.
The hall was crowded on the night
With sober men of middle age ;
The curtain folded out of sight;
Expectant of a keen delight,
The Macs beheld a darkened stage.
They waited long, the room was cold,
Yet no man murmured, but instead
The red assembled nobs they told
To see what Burgoo Hall would hold
In cash at half-a-crown a head.
An hour crept by like this, and they
Saw just an empty stage. No strain
Of Scottish humor made them gay;
And then at last to their dismay
The curtain drifted down again.
They yelled. Their wonder was intense.
The bold Professor, bowing low,
Stepped forth. “What is it, friends? Comm
Why, bless my soul, how very dense !
The show is over, don’t you know.”
The patrons wore a studious air,
But soon they grasped the base design,
Then sought the stranger here and there,
And fast he fled on Elkin’s mare
From Burgoo on the Border Line.
EDWARD DYSON.
On Our Selection—Two Horses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We had two saddle-horses at our selection — a mare
and a colt. “ Nell ” and “ Ned ” we called them.
Fine hacks they were, too ! Anybody could ride
them, they were so quiet. Dad reckoned Ned was
the better of the two. He was well-bred, and had
a pedigree and a gentle disposition, and a bald-
face, and a bumble-foot, and a raw wither, and a
sore back that gave him a habit of “ flinching ”
a habit that discounted his uselessness a great
deal, because, when we weren’t at home, the
women couldn’t saddle him to run the cows in.
Whenever he saw the saddle or heard the girth-
buckles rattle he would start to flinch. Put the
cloth on his back—folded or otherwise—and, no
matter how smart you might be, it would be off
before you could cover it with the saddle, and he
wouldn’t have flicked it with his tail, or pulled it
off with his teeth, or done anything to it. He
just flinched —made the skin on his back—where
there was any quiver. Throw on the saddle
without a cloth, and he would “give” in the middle
like a broken rail—bend till his belly almost
touched the ground, and remain bent till mounted ;
then he’d crawl off and gradually straighten up as
he became used to you. Were you tender-hearted
enough to feel compunction in sitting down hard
on a six-year-old sore, or if you had an aversion
to kicking the suffering brute with both heels and
belting his hide with a yard or two of fencing-
wire to get him to show signs of animation, you
would dismount and walk—perhaps, weep. We
always rode him right out, though.
As a two-year-old Ned was Dad’s hope. Point-
ing proudly to the long-legged, big-headed, ugly
moke mooching by the door, smelling the dust, he
would say: “Be a fine horse in another year !
Little sleepy-looking yet; that’s nothing ! ”
“Stir him up a bit, till we see how he canters,”
he said to Joe one day. And when Joe stirred
him up—rattled a piece of rock on his jaw that
nearly knocked his head off—Dad took after Joe
and chased him through the potatoes, and out into
the grass-paddock, and across towards Ander-
son’s; then returned and yarded the colt, and
knocked a patch of skin off him with a rail
because he wouldn’t stand in a corner till he
looked at his eye. “ Wouldn’t have anything
happen to that colt for a fortune ! ” he said to him-
self. Then went away, forgetting to throw the
rails down. Dave threw them down a couple of
days after.
We preferred Nell to Ned, but Dad always
voted for the colt. “You can trust him; he’ll
stand anywhere,” he used to say. Ned would l
Once, when the grass-paddock was burning, he
stood until he took fire. Then he stood while we
hammered him with boughs to put the blaze out.
It took a lot to frighten Ned. His presence of
mind rarely deserted him. Once, though, he got
a start. He was standing in the shade of a tree
in the paddock when Dad went to catch him. He
seemed to be watching Dad, but wasn’t. He was
asleep. “Well, old chap,” said Dad, “ how are y’ ?”
and proceeded to bridle him. Ned opened his mouth
and received the bit as usual, only some of his
tongue came out and stayed out. “ Wot’s up w’
y’ ? ” and Dad tried to poke it in with his finger,
but it came out further, and some chewed grass
dropped into his hand. Dad started to lead him
then, or rather to pull him, and at the first tug he
gave the reins Ned woke with a snort and broke
away. And when the other horses saw him look-
ing at Dad with his tail cocked, and his head up,
and the bridle-reins hanging, they went for their
lives through the trees, and Blossom’s foal got
staked.
Another day Dad was out on Ned, looking for
the red heifer, and came across two men fencing—
a tall, powerful-looking man with a beard, and a
slim young fellow with a smooth face. Also a
kangaroo-pup. As Dad slowly approached, Ned
swaying from side to side with his nose to the
ground, the elder man drove the crowbar into the
earth and stared as one that hadn’t seen a man on
horseback before. The young fellow sat on a log
and stared, too. The pup ran behind a tree and
growled.
“ Seen any cattle round here? ” Dad asked.
“ No,” the man said, and grinned.
“ Didn’t notice a red heifer ? ”
“ No,” grinning more.
The kangaroo pup left the tree and sniffed at
Ned’s heels.
“ Won’t kick, will he?” said the man.
The young fellow broke into a loud laugh and
fell off the log.
“ No,” Dad replied—“ he’s 'perfectly quiet.”
“He looks quiet.”
The young fellow took a fit of coughing.
After a pause. “ Well, you didn’t see any about,
then ? ” and Dad wheeled Ned round to go
away.
“ No, I didn't, old man,” the other answered,
and snatched hold of Ned’s tail and hung back
with all his might. Ned grunted and strained
and tore the ground up with his toes; Dad
spurred and leathered him with a strap, looking
straight ahead. The man hung on. “ Come
’long,” Dad said. The pup barked. “ Come ’long
with yer l ” Dad said. The young fellow fell off
the log again. Ned’s tail cracked. Dad hit him
between the ears. The tail cracked again. A
piece of it came off ; then Ned stumbled and went
on his head. “What the devil !” Dad said,
looking round. But only the young fellow was
laughing.
* * * *
Nell was different to Ned. She was a bay, with
yellow flanks and a lump under her belly ; a
bright eye, lop ears, and heavy, hairy legs. She
was a very wise mare. It was wonderful how
much she knew. She knew when she was wanted;
and she would go away the night before and get
lost. And she knew when she wasn’t wanted ;
then she’d hang about the back-door licking a
hole in th 9 ground where the dish-water was
thrown, or fossicking at the barn for the corn Dad
had hidden, or scratching her neck or her rump
against the cultivation-paddock slip-rails. She
always scratched herself against those slip-rails—
sometimes for hours —always until they fell down.
Then she’d walk in and eat. And how she could
eat!
As a hack, Nell was unreliable. You couldn’t
reckon with certainty on getting her to start.
All depended on the humor she was in and the
direction you wished to take—mostly the direc-
tion. If towards the grass-paddock or the dam,
she was off helter-skelter. If it wasn’t, she’d go
on strike —put her head down and chew the bit.
Then, when you’d get to work on her with a waddy
—which we always did—she’d walk backwards
into the house and frighten mother, or into the
water-hole and dirty the water. Dad said it was
the fault of the cove who broke her in. Dad was
a just man. The “cove” was a union shearer—
did it for 4s. 6d. Wanted five “bob,” but Dad
beat him down. Anybody else would have asked
a pound.
When Nell did make up her mind to go, it was
with a rush, and if the slip-rails were on the
ground she’d refuse to take them. She’d stand
and look out into the lane. You’d have to get off
and drag the rails aside (about 20, counting broken
ones). Then she’d fancy they were up, and would
shake her head and mark time until you dug your
heels into her ; then she’d gather herself together
and jump high enough for a show—over nothing !
Dave was to ride Nell to town one Christmas, to
see the sports. He hadn’t seen any sports before,
and went to bed excited and rose in the middle of
the night to start. He dressed in the dark, and
we heard him going out, because he fell over Sandy
and Kate and their baby. They were sleeping on
the floor in the front room. We also heard him
throw the slip-rails down.
There was a heavy fog that morning. At break-
fast we talked about Dave, and Dad “ ’sposed ” he
would just about be getting in; but an hour or
two after breakfast the fog cleared, and we saw
Dave in the lane hammering Nell with a stick.
Nell had her rump to the fence and was trying
hard to kick it down. Dad went to him. “ Take
her gently; take her gently, boy,” he shouted.
“ Pshaio! take her gently /” Dave shouted back.
“ Here ” —he jumped off her and handed Dad the
reins—“ take her away and cut her throat.” Then
he cried, and then he picked up a big stone and
rushed at Nell’s head. But Dad interfered.
But the day Dad mounted Nell to bring a doctor
to Anderson! She started away smartly—the
wrong road. Dad jerked her mouth and pulled
her round roughly. He was in a hurry—Nell
wasn’t. She stood and shook her head and
switched her tail. Dad rattled a waddy on her
and jammed his heels hard against her ribs. She
dropped her head and cow-kicked. Then he
coaxed her. “ Comeon, old girl,” he said, “ come
on,” and patted her on the neck. She liked being
patted. That exasperated Dad. He hit her on
the head with his fist. Joe ran out with a long
stick. He poked her in the flank. Nell kicked
the stick out of his hands and bolted towards the
dam. Dad pulled and swore as she bore him
along. And when he did haul her in, he was 200
yards further from the doctor. Dad turned her
round and once more reverted to the waddy. Nell
was obdurate, Dad exhausted. Joe joined them,
out of breath. He poked Nell with the stick
again. She “kicked up.” Dad lost his balance.
Joe laughed. Dad said, “St-o-op!” Joe was
energetic. So was Nell. She kicked up again—
strong—and Dad fell off.
“Wot, could’n’ y’ s-s-s-stick to ’er, Dad ? ” Joe
asked.
“ Stick be damned —run — catch her! — d n y’!”
Joe obeyed.
Dad made another start, and this time Nell
went willingly. Dad was leading her!
• * * *
Those two old horses are dead now. They died
in the summer when there was lots of grass and
water —just when Dad had broken them into har-
ness—just when he was getting a good team
together to draw logs for the new railway line !
